

2020 WL 1244535 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE LUIS GONZALEZ
File: AXXX-XX6-276 - Orlando, FL
February 3, 2020
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Maithe Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Manuel Ramirez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before us on June 23, 2016, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to terminate removal proceedings and ordering his removal from the United States. The respondent, a native and citizen of Colombia, has submitted a second motion to reopen on August 15, 2019.1 The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has submitted a brief opposing the respondent’s motion to reopen. The motion will be denied.
 
For clarity, we provide a brief recitation of the undisputed facts and procedural history in this case. On April 3, 2015, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to terminate his removal proceedings, finding him inadmissible under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), because his Florida conviction for resisting an officer with violence is a crime involving moral turpitude. On October 30, 2015, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s second motion to terminate and ordered his removal from the United States to Colombia. On June 23, 2016, this Board affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision and dismissed the respondent’s appeal. Thereafter, the respondent filed a petition for review before the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit, the controlling federal jurisdiction in this matter. On October 5, 2017, the Eleventh Circuit denied the respondent’s petition for review (see Respondent’s “Reply to DHS’ Opposition to the Respondent’s Motion to Reopen,” submitted on September 17, 2019, at Tab O).
 
On August 15, 2019, over 3 years after our decision, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen arguing that these proceedings should be reopened based on ineffective assistance of his prior counsel (see Respondent’s ““Corrected Motion to Reopen Removal Proceedings Based on Ineffective Assistance of Prior Counsel,” submitted on August 15, 2019, at 7-11 (“Motion to Reopen”)).
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely as it was filed over 3 years after the entry of the Board’s final administrative decision in this case. A party may file only one motion to reopen no later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Moreover, it is well-settled that motions to reopen removal proceedings are disfavored and that reopening may properly be denied solely in the exercise of discretion. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314, 316 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104-05 (1988).
 
*2 In his motion to reopen, the respondent claims that he retained an attorney in December of 2017, for the purpose of reopening his proceedings, but his prior attorney made misrepresentations to him and never filed a motion on his behalf with this Board until almost 2 years later (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 7-9). The respondent further claims that although his prior attorney purportedly filed a motion to reopen with the Orlando Immigration Court, he failed to include evidence of the visa petition filed on his behalf by his United States citizen spouse (id. at 7-8).2
 
To establish an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, (1) the motion must be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent attesting to the relevant facts, (2) former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed the opportunity to respond, and (3) if the prior counsel’s conduct violated ethical or legal responsibilities, the motion should reflect whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988); see Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005) (confirming the applicability of these procedural requirements within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit, wherein this case arises).
 
Although the respondent has submitted a copy of a grievance that was filed with the appropriate bar association against his former counsel, there is no evidence that the respondent provided his former attorney with an opportunity to respond to those allegations prior to filing the instant motion (see Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at Exh. D).3 Significantly, the respondent has not established that his prior counsel’s conduct prejudiced him in any way, particularly since he has not shown a reasonable probability that his untimely4 motion to reopen would have been granted, but for his prior counsel’s alleged ineffectiveness. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d at 1274. Based on the foregoing, we conclude that the respondent has not established that reopening is warranted based on his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1).
 
Moreover, the respondent has not demonstrated a basis for the filing deadline to be equitably tolled See Avila-Santoyo v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 713 F.3d 1357, 1363 n.5 (11th Cir. 2013) (stating that “equitable tolling requires a litigant to show (1) that he has been pursuing his rights diligently, and (2) that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way”) (internal quotations and citation omitted); see also Booth v. Carnival Corp., 522 F.3d 1148, 1150 (11th Cir. 2008) (stating that equitable tolling is an “‘extraordinary remedy’ that should be used sparingly, but courts may toll time limitations when an inequitable event prevents a party’s timely action”). Initially, we note that there is no evidence that the respondent took any action to seek reopening of his case until he was detained by the DHS in July of 2019 (see Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 7 ¶ 21). Although the respondent claims that he retained the services of his prior attorney in 2017, and he was under the impression that his prior counsel filed a motion to reopen on his behalf, the respondent has not explained why he took no further action until 2019 when he was detained by the DHS (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 7 ¶ 26 and 9 ¶ 39). Thus, we conclude that the respondent has not demonstrated due diligence in pursuing his right to seek reopening such that the filing deadline should be equitably tolled. See Avila-Santoyo v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 713 F.3d at 1363 n.5.
 
*3 Based on the foregoing, we conclude that the respondent has not shown that untimely reopening of these removal proceedings is warranted. We also conclude that the respondent has not shown exceptional circumstances that would warrant the favorable exercise of our sua sponte authority to reopen these proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that the power to reopen sua sponte “is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”).
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The record reflects that the respondent’s prior counsel filed a motion to reopen with this Board on August 2, 2019. While that motion was pending, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen with the assistance of his present counsel.

2

We find the respondent’s argument unpersuasive, however, since his motion reflects that the visa petition was filed on August 7, 2019 (see Respondent’s ““Reply to DHS’ Opposition to the Respondent’s Motion to Reopen” at Tab L) - shortly before the instant motion was submitted - not in 2017 when the respondent married his United States citizen spouse or when his prior counsel purportedly filed his motion with the Immigration Court.

3

We also note that the respondent has not provided any additional information regarding the outcome of his bar complaint notwithstanding the passage of time during the pendency of this motion.

4

Notably, prior counsel’s motion would have been untimely even if he had not allegedly rendered ineffective assistance, since he was not hired until over a year after our entry of a final order of removal on June 23, 2016.

2020 WL 1244530 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KEVIN JOSUE ARRIAZA-RIVERA
File: AXXX-XX4-263 - Memphis, TN
January 28, 2020
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eric A. Henton, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s July 15, 2019, decision denying his motion to reopen proceedings. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia after he did not appear for a hearing on January 21, 2015. The respondent argues that exceptional circumstances should excuse his failure to appear. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
An in absentia removal order may be rescinded only if the alien files a motion to reopen: (1) within 180 days after the date of the removal order in which he establishes that his failure to appear was because of “exceptional circumstances,” as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1); or (2) at any time in which he establishes that he did not have proper notice of the hearing at which the removal order was entered. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen as untimely (IJ at 2). A motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances must be filed within 180 days of the failure to appear. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act. The respondent’s motion was filed on June 3, 2019, and was thus untimely by nearly 4 years.
 
We disagree with the respondent’s argument that the deadline set forth in section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act should be equitably tolled based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Respondent’s Br. at 8-11. First, the respondent has not specified when an attorney was initially retained in his case and whether that attorney could thus be charged with contributing to the respondent’s inaction in filing a timely motion to reopen within the relevant 180-day period (IJ at 2). Further, the Immigration Judge correctly concluded that the respondent did not satisfy the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to document an ineffective assistance of counsel claim (IJ at 2-3). The respondent did not submit documentary evidence to show that the impugned attorney has been both informed of the ineffective assistance of counsel allegation and provided with an opportunity to respond to the allegation. See id. at 639. Written correspondence between the respondent and his former attorney that was submitted in support of the respondent’s motion to reopen pertains only to a request for the respondent’s file. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Tab H. With respect to additional steps required under Matter of Lozada, the respondent has neither submitted evidence of a disciplinary complaint filed against his former attorney nor explained why such a complaint has not been filed. See id. These requirements are intended to discourage baseless allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See id. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent has not established that the deadline for filing a motion to reopen should be tolled because it is not apparent that the impugned attorney was retained during the relevant period and because the requirements to document an ineffective assistance of counsel claim have not been fulfilled. Accordingly, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen as untimely.
 
*2 We affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s request that proceedings be reopened sua sponte (IJ at 3-4). The Immigration Judge’s authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte is “an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations.” Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1134 (BIA 1999). It is not intended as a general remedy for instances where a respondent has not complied with the applicable law and regulations. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). The respondent in this case had proper notice of the hearing at which he was ordered removed and did not seek the reopening of proceedings until more than 4 years later. We agree with the Immigration Judge that sua sponte reopening was not shown to be warranted in this case.
 
For the aforementioned reasons, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
William A. Cassidy
FOR THE BOARD
2020 WL 1244530 (BIA)
End of Document

2020 WL 1244527 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SUJITNO SAJUTI
File: AXXX-XX3-993 - Hartford, CT
January 27, 2020
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Diana R. Blank, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Amit Patel
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Indonesia, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s April 24, 2018, decision that denied his motion to reopen. The respondent has also requested remand. The Department of Homeland Security opposes reopening. The appeal will be dismissed, and the motion will be denied.
 
The respondent does not dispute that his motion to reopen is untimely, but instead contends that he warrants equitable tolling of the time limits or sua sponte reopening and remand. On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion because 1) the respondent was deprived of due process and effective assistance of counsel at his National Security Entry-Exit Registration System (NSEERS) interview; 2) the respondent was prejudiced by ineffective assistance of counsel at his immigration court proceedings; and 3) the respondent’s prima facie eligibility for a U visa is sufficient to merit reopening in the Immigration Judge’s discretion (Respondent’s Br. at 13-39). In his supplemental brief, the respondent further argues that the Immigration Judge did not have jurisdiction over his removal proceedings and that the Board should reopen sua sponte due to a recent change of law (Respondent’s Supp. Br. at 7-21). In his motion to remand, the respondent argues that the Board should remand to the Immigration Judge because Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) has found the respondent prima facie eligible for a U visa and granted him deferred action status (Respondent’s Motion to Remand at 5-12).
 
The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in not considering the due process violations at the respondent’s NSEERS examination (Respondent’s Br. at 14). The respondent argues that his prior counsel committed ineffective assistance of counsel in the NSEERS examination process, but the respondent has not established that he was prejudiced by any potential ineffective assistance of counsel (Respondent’s Br. at 17-21). Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988) (requiring, inter alia, prejudice to sustain an ineffective assistance of counsel claim). Under the former NSEERS registration requirements, the respondent was obligated to provide certain information, including whether he was in compliance with his nonimmigrant visa, and the presence of counsel would not have changed that obligation. Rajah v. Mukasey, 544 F.3d 427, 440 (2d Cir. 2008) (registrees have no right to remain silent or to refuse to provide information about immigration status). The respondent did not, and does not now, dispute that he overstayed his nonimmigrant visa without authorization and therefore was removable as charged in the Notice to Appear (NTA) (Exh. 1).
 
*2 Although the respondent argues that, had counsel who represented him at the NSEERS interview screened him for potential U visa eligibility, the legacy Immigration and Naturalization Service may not have issued the respondent an NTA, the respondent’s argument is speculative (Respondent’s Br. at 19-20). Moreover, he does not indicate what prejudice he suffered because his NSEERS counsel did not timely turn over his file to his later counsel (Respondent’s Br. at 19-20). Therefore, the respondent has not established that any ineffective assistance of counsel at the NSEERS interview prejudiced him in his removal proceedings before the Immigration Judge. He also has not established that the interviewing officers’ failure to delay the interview to wait for the respondent’s attorney, who arrived late, constituted conscience-shocking conduct or a deprivation of fundamental rights (Respondent’s Br. at 19). Rajah v. Mukasey, 544 F.3d at 447 (holding that pre-hearing regulatory violations are not grounds for termination of immigration court proceedings, absent prejudice that may have affected the outcome of the proceeding, conscience-shocking conduct, or a deprivation of fundamental rights.)
 
The respondent further argues that his second attorney was ineffective during removal proceedings because she did not challenge the NTA and did not screen him for potential U visa eligibility (Respondent’s Br. at 21-30). We agree with the Immigration Judge, however, that the respondent has not established prejudice because he has not established that he was not removable as charged in the NTA or that he was eligible for any relief the Immigration Judge may grant. 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(1) (only the USCIS has jurisdiction over U visa petitions). 8 C.F.R. §§ 214.14(c)(ii), (c)(iv)(5)(i)(B) (outlining eligibility to apply for a U visa despite a removal order or from outside the United States).
 
In his appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge overlooked some of his claims and evidence in his motion to reopen (Respondent’s Br. at 11). Because the respondent has not established that he merits reopening for the reasons discussed above, we do not address these potential omissions.
 
In his supplemental brief, the respondent also argues that the NTA served on him was defective under the Supreme Court’s decision in Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S. Ct. 2105 (2018), because it did not include the time and place of his hearing and therefore the Immigration Judge did not have jurisdiction over his proceedings (Respondent’s Supp. Br. at 7-10). Since the respondent filed his brief, however, the Second Circuit and the Board have issued decisions regarding Immigration Court jurisdiction over proceedings begun by such defective NTAs. Banegas Gomez v. Barr, 922 F.3d 101, 110-111 (2d Cir. 2019) (“[W]e conclude that Pereira’s self-described disposition of this ‘narrow question,’... is not properly read to void jurisdiction in cases in which an NTA omits a hearing time or place.”); Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. 441 (BIA 2018) (holding that a notice to appear that does not specify the time and place of an alien’s initial removal hearing vests an Immigration Judge with jurisdiction over the removal proceedings and meets the requirements of section 239(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a), so long as a notice of hearing specifying this information is later sent to the alien). On May 30, 2003, the Immigration Court sent a notice of hearing which specified the time and place of his hearing. Therefore, the Immigration Judge properly had jurisdiction over the respondent’s 2003 removal proceedings.
 
*3 In his motion to remand, the respondent argues that the Board should remand proceedings because the USCIS has found him prima facie eligible for U visa status and has placed him in deferred action status due to the unavailability of U visas (Respondent’s Motion to Remand at 5-12). The respondent contends that reopening would “allow him to avail himself of the relief for which...he is eligible,” but does not indicate that he is eligible for any relief that the Immigration Judge can grant (Respondent’s Motion to Remand at 10). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 214.14(c)(1), (c)(iv)(5)(i) (outlining procedures for motions to reopen of respondents with final orders of removal who have been granted U visas by the USCIS). Until the respondent is granted a U visa, he remains removable and therefore the Immigration Judge cannot terminate proceedings. See Matter S-O-G- & F-D-B-, 27 I&N Dec. 462 (A.G. 2018). As the outcome of the proceedings will not change due to the approval of a U visa, the new evidence is not material. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(1), (c)(4) (motion to remand based on new evidence must be, inter alia, material). Accordingly, the respondent has not established eligibility for a motion to remand. See also Matter of L-V-K-, 22 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1999).
 
The respondent also argues that the Board should reopen the proceedings sua sponte (Respondent’s Supp. Br. at 20). We decline to do so, however, because, as discussed above, the respondent has not established eligibility for any relief the Immigration Judge may grant, and therefore has not established “a substantial likelihood that the result in his case would be changed if reconsideration or reopening is granted.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216, 1219 (BIA 2000).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered:
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2020 WL 1244527 (BIA)
End of Document

2020 WL 1244522 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LEANA PATRICIA OLIVIA RENDEROS
File: AXXX-XX9-706 - New York, NY
January 23, 2020
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marisa-Andrea Moore, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; termination
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Honduras, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 23, 2018, that denied her motion to reopen proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under a clearly erroneous standard, and all other issue de novo. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The record reflects that the respondent was placed in removal proceedings by personal service of a Notice to Appear (NTA) on November 1, 2013 (Exh. 1). The NTA informed the respondent of the consequences of failing to appear for an immigration hearing and advised the respondent of her responsibility to keep the Immigration Court informed of her current address. The record reflects that the respondent provided an address in the Bronx, New York, and that she was released on her own recognizance pending an immigration hearing (Exh. 2).
 
The respondent alleges in an unsworn statement that she thereafter reported to Immigration and Customs Enforcement (ICE) in January 2014 (Respondent’s Mot. at Tab A). The respondent was informed that her next reporting date was in August 2014, but she claims that she somehow became confused and believed that she was required to report in October 2014. The respondent claims that she remained in contact with ICE through April 2014. The respondent claims that ICE gave her permission to relocate to an address in Nebraska, which she provided to an immigration officer. However, the respondent states that she did not move to that address because no one met her when she arrived in Nebraska. The respondent moved instead to an address resulting from a chance encounter with another woman from Honduras. The respondent indicates that after moving to Nebraska, she lost contact with the family at the Bronx address she had provided on her release from immigration detention. The respondent claims that she was in possession of an ICE document with an appointment scheduled for October 4, 2014, in Omaha, NE.1 However, the respondent did not keep the appointment because the person she had arranged to transport her to Omaha telephoned to cancel the trip. That person informed the respondent that she had contacted the appointment location directly and was told that a removal order had been entered against the respondent.
 
The respondent indicates that she took no steps to inform immigration authorities of her circumstances after learning of the removal order because she did not know whom to contact (Respondent’s Mot. at Tab A). The respondent states that she consulted various un-named attorneys about her situation. However, she did not hire anyone. The respondent argues that she was prevented from pursuing reopening in a timely manner because none of the attorneys she consulted advised her to file a motion for the purpose. The respondent filed the instant motion after accumulating funds to hire her current counsel.
 
*2 The respondent asserts on appeal that language barriers and a lack of education constitute exceptional circumstances that prevented her from reporting her change of address and appearing for her scheduled removal hearing (Respondent’s Br. at 6). See section 240(b)(4)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(4)(C) (providing for rescission of an in absentia order of removal within 180 days upon a showing of exceptional circumstances). The respondent argues that the 180 day time limit for filing a motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances should be equitably tolled. She argues that informing ICE of her move to Nebraska, the respondent’s alleged intention to report for a scheduled appointment with immigration authorities in that state, and her alleged consultations with various attorneys about her situation demonstrates diligence in attempting to comply with her responsibilities in removal proceedings (Respondent’s Br. at 5). Alternatively, the respondent argues that these proceedings should be terminated because the NTA served on her to commence the removal proceedings was defective (Respondent’s Br. at 7-15). The respondent also requests in the alternative that this Board exercise its sua sponte power to reopen and reinstate the proceedings (Respondent’s Br. at 15).
 
The respondent’s motion to terminate is based on the decision of the United States Supreme Court in Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S. Ct. 2105 (2018), holding that the NTA in that case, which did not designate a specific time or place of an alien’s hearing, was not a “Notice to Appear” under section 239(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a), for purposes of qualifying for cancellation of removal (Respondent’s Mot. at 1-3). The respondent argues that inasmuch the NTA in her case was similarly defective, because it did not specify a date and time for his initial hearing, the NTA could not confer jurisdiction over this matter on the Immigration Court. Therefore, the order of removal entered by the Immigration Judge is invalid, and the proceedings should be terminated.
 
The respondent is correct that the NTA issued to her does not specify the time or date on which her initial hearing would be held (Exh. 1). However, in Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. 441, 447 (BIA 2018), this Board held that a NTA that does not specify the time and place of an alien’s initial removal hearing will vest an Immigration Judge with jurisdiction over removal proceedings and meet the requirements of section 239(a) of the Act so long as a notice of hearing specifying that information is later sent to the alien. The United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit reached the same conclusion in Benegas Gomez v. Barr, 922 F.3d 101, 110-11 (2d Cir. 2019) (rejecting jurisdictional argument for termination based on Pereira v. Sessions, and agreeing with the Board’s reasoning in Matter of Bermudez-Cota). There is no dispute that the respondent was issued a notice for her initial hearing, and constructive receipt is indicated because the notice was sent to the address of record for the respondent. Accordingly, we are not persuaded that termination of the removal proceedings is warranted.
 
*3 The Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen will be affirmed. The motion is untimely. As indicated above, the Immigration Judge correctly found that the respondent may be charged with constructive receipt of her hearing notice because it was sent to the last address she provided to the Immigration Court (IJ at 1-3). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated due diligence to equitably toll the 180 day time limit for filing a motion to rescind the in absentia order of removal in this case. The respondent took no action to inform immigration authorities of her whereabouts and explain the failure to report for her schedule hearing even after the respondent became aware that she had been ordered removed (IJ at 2). The Immigration Judge properly rejected the respondent’s attempt to blame her inaction on the various un-named attorneys she consulted because, aside from the lack compliance with the procedural requirements designed to insure the truthfulness of claims ineffective assistance, the respondent’s assertions in that regard lack sufficient detail to show that she reasonably relied on the advice of prior counsel to her detriment (IJ at 3). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (outlining the procedural requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (an alien asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must demonstrate prejudice).
 
The respondent’s argument that a language barrier and lack of education constitute exceptional circumstances excusing compliance with her responsibility to keep the Immigration Court apprised of her current address is also unpersuasive. The NTA reflects that the respondent received advisals concerning her responsibility to inform the Immigration Court of a change of address and the consequences of a failure to appear at future proceedings. The respondent does not claim that those advisals were not communicated or explained to her. Moreover, the respondent concedes that she was aware that she was required to keep ICE similarly informed of any change of address, yet nevertheless failed to do so. Therefore, it is not clear that she would have complied in any event. Under the circumstances, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s actions and explanations appear to reflect “a casual unconcern” with her legal responsibilities in removal proceedings (IJ at 4). We affirm his determination that the respondent did not establish exceptional circumstances that would excuse her failure to appear.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen sua sponte will also be denied. The respondent has not presented exceptional circumstances to justify sua sponte reopening in the exercise of discretion, as she merely seeks to evade the regulatory requirements for rescission of the in absentia order of removal. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. at 984 (stating that the power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship).
 
*4 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
William A. Cassidy
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The document submitted by the respondent reflects that the appointment was actually for August 4, 2014 (IJ at 2; Respondent’s Mot. at Tab F).


2020 WL 1244522 (BIA)

2020 WL 1169210 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OSEI BOADI
File: AXXX-XX9-403 - New York, NY
January 9, 2020
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nima M. Moshggoo, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Termination
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s June 19, 2018, orders finding him removable, denying his motion to terminate proceedings, and ordering him removed to his native Ghana. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the credibility determination, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues involving questions of law, judgment and discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to terminate proceedings. The respondent sought termination in order to have his Form N-400, Application for Naturalization, adjudicated by the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS). He reports, and has provided evidence showing, that a prior application for naturalization was denied on December 4, 2007, as he did not demonstrate the requisite good moral character for naturalization (Motion, Tab B). When he subsequently attempted to submit another application for naturalization, it was administratively closed on March 24, 2018, because he was in removal proceedings (Motion, Tab C).
 
In denying the respondent’s motion to terminate, the Immigration Judge found that no good cause had been shown (6/19/18 IJ Order). The respondent argues on appeal that his prior counsel failed to properly question the authority of the Immigration Services Officer to issue the Notice to Appear (Respondent’s Br., para. 12; Exh. 1). Although he asserts that he had ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent has not complied with any of the requirements for reopening based on such a claim (Respondent’s Br. at 16-18). Twum v. INS, 411 F.3d 54 (2d Cir. 2005); see also Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 47 (2d Cir. 2005) (stating that a respondent who fails to comply substantially with the Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) requirements for reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel “forfeits” his claim). Therefore, reopening or termination in this regard is not appropriate.
 
The respondent did not identify any basis upon which termination of the proceedings was warranted. He neither challenged the validity of the factual allegations contained in the Notice to Appear, nor the charges of removability under section 237(a)(1)(A) of the Act (Exh. 1).
 
Furthermore, the respondent’s desire to challenge the denial of his original naturalization application, and/or the administrative closure of his second application, without more, is not a basis for termination of the proceedings. Rather, he has not shown that he is eligible for naturalization as he has not addressed the determination that he did not satisfy the good moral character requirement. Sections 101(f)(8), 316(a)(3) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 329(d); see also Matter of Castillo-Perez, 27 I&N Dec. 664, 666 (BIA 2019). Furthermore, removal proceedings may only be terminated pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1239.2(f) where the Department of Homeland Security has presented an affirmative communication attesting to an alien’s prima facie eligibility for naturalization. See, e.g., Matter of Acosta-Hidalgo, 24 I&N Dec. 103 (BIA 2007). No such attestation is of record.
 
*2 The respondent’s motion may also be viewed as being in the nature of a request for adjournment of the proceedings. In this regard, we note that the Immigration Judge properly found he did not present good cause for such a request. See Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785, 790-92 (BIA 2009) (setting forth factors to consider in determining whether good cause exists for a continuance to await adjudication of a pending visa petition, including prima facie eligibility for adjustment); see also Matter of L-A-B-R- et al, 27 I&N Dec. 405, 407 (A.G. 2018) (stating that continuances may only be granted upon a showing of good cause); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 and 1240.6.
 
Since the respondent did not demonstrate good cause for either termination or adjournment of the proceedings, the Immigration Judge properly denied his motion to terminate. Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE MARIA CANTU-MARTINEZ
File: AXXX-XX5-622 - Los Fresnos, TX
December 27, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jodi M. Goodwin, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Francisco J. Muniz
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the July 24, 2018, decision of the Immigration Judge denying his motion to reopen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was ordered removed from the United States on December 3, 1999, as an inadmissible returning resident who was convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude. See sections 101(a)(13)(C)(v), 212(a)(2)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1101(a)(13)(C)(v), 1182(a)(2)(A)(i). His removal order was effectuated the next day and, according to the respondent, he returned to the United States the same month (Respondent’s Br. at 4; Motion to Reopen at 63).1
 
An alien seeking to reopen his removal proceedings must file a motion within 90 days of the date of his administratively final order of removal.2 Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i). The respondent does not argue that the instant motion was filed within 90 days of his removal order becoming final but rather argues that the deadline should be equitably tolled because (1) the Immigration Judge who presided over his 1999 removal proceedings misapplied the law by determining that his conviction was an aggravated felony, thus rendering him ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a); and (2) he obtained incorrect advice from a series of attorneys over the years since his removal (Respondent’s Br. at 5-10).3 An alien seeking reopening past the 90-day deadline is entitled to equitable tolling if he establishes “(1) that he has been pursuing his rights diligently, and (2) that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way and prevented timely filing.” Lugo-Resendenz v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337, 344 (5th Cir. 2016).
 
Here, we discern no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s finding that equitable tolling does not apply because the respondent has not identified any extraordinary circumstances that prevented him from filing a timely motion to reopen (IJ at 2). See Penalva v. Sessions, 884 F.3d 521, 525 (5th Cir. 2018) (“[W]hether equitable tolling applies to a [respondent’s] motion to reopen is a question of fact.”) (quoting Dominguez v. Sessions, 708 F. App’x 808, 812 (5th Cir. 2017)); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). Specifically, the respondent does not persuasively explain how the Immigration Judge’s conduct during the removal proceedings “stood in his way and prevented” him from filing a motion to reopen within 90 days after the proceedings (Respondent’s Br. at 6-8). Lugo-Resendenz v. Lynch, 831 F.3d at 344. He has also not presented any evidence showing that he sought legal advice within the 90-day period following the entry of his removal order (Motion to Reopen at 63). Therefore, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen on the basis that it was untimely (IJ at 2).
 
*2 In addition, the prior Immigration Judge’s legal conclusion that the respondent’s conviction was an aggravated felony, which would make him statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal, did not render the proceedings fundamentally unfair. The respondent did not meaningfully contest that he was removable as charged in the Notice to Appear (NTA) before the Immigration Judge, nor did he pursue his right to appeal, and “because an alien’s eligibility for discretionary relief from removal [such as cancellation] is not a liberty or property interest deserving of due process protection, an IJ’s failure to explain the eligibility for such relief does not rise to the level of fundamental unfairness.” United States v. Cordova-Soto, 804 F.3d 714, 723 (5th Cir. 2015) (internal quotation omitted); see also Miresles-Zuniga v. Holder, 743 F.3d 110, 112 (5th Cir. 2014) (noting that cancellation of removal is a discretionary form of relief).
 
The respondent also has not identified any newly submitted material evidence that was “not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3); see also INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104 (1988) (A motion to reopen may be denied if “the movant has not introduced previously unavailable, material evidence.”). Specifically, the respondent has not explained how (1) his conviction record dated May 13, 1996, (2) evidence of continuous physical presence prior to 1996, and (3) evidence of positive equites prior to service of the NTA were unavailable during his 1999 removal proceedings (Respondent’s Br. at 11). Thus, we also affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision on the basis that the motion does not allege any new facts to be proven (IJ at 2).
 
Because we affirm the decision below on the foregoing bases, we need not address the respondent’s arguments regarding the Immigration Judge’s exercise of discretion (IJ at 2; Respondent’s Br. at 11-13).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Mark Noferi

See Martinez v. Johnson, 740 F.3d 1040, 1043 (5th Cir. 2014); Anderson v. Napolitano, 611 F.3d 275, 278-79 (5th Cir. 2010).

The respondent is barred from seeking sua sponte reopening under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) due to his departure from the United States. See Ovalles v. Holder, 577 F.3d 288, 299-300 (5th Cir. 2009).

U.S. Department of Justice

IN RE: ALFREDO ARRINDELL A.K.A. ALFREDO ARRINDEL A.K.A. ALFREDO ARRINDEL MERAN A.K.A. ALFREDO ARRINDELL MERAN
File: AXXX-XX0-245 - Boston, MA
December 23, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey B. Rubin, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 In an April 12, 2018, decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent appeals from that decision and the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) did not file a brief in reply to the respondent’s appeal.1 The respondent additionally requests termination of proceedings. The request will be denied, and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent argues that proceedings should be terminated for lack of jurisdiction (Respondent’s Br. at 1, 4-6); we disagree. A notice to appear (NTA) that does not specify the time and place of the alien’s initial removal hearing vests an Immigration Judge with jurisdiction over the removal proceedings when a notice of hearing specifying that information is later sent to the alien. Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. 441, 445-47 (BIA 2018); see Goncalves Pontes v. Barr, 938 F.3d 1, 7 (1st Cir. 2019). The NTA served on the respondent did not list the date and time of his initial removal proceeding, but the notice of hearing served on him one month later provided the missing information (Exh. 1; Notice of Hearing in Removal Proceedings, June 10, 2010). Thus, the respondent’s jurisdictional argument is unavailing because the NTA, coupled with the notice of hearing, vested the Immigration Judge with jurisdiction. Therefore, we will deny the respondent’s request for termination of proceedings.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely (Respondent’s Br. at 6; DHS’s Opposition to Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 1). See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i). However, the respondent advances two arguments to show his motion warrants the “‘rare remedy”D’ of equitable tolling. See Neves v. Holder, 613 F.3d 30, 36 (1st Cir. 2010) (quoting Wallace v. Kato, 549 U.S. 384, 396 (2007)). To prevail in this regard, the respondent must show “extraordinary circumstances” prevented him from complying with the statutory deadline and that he “diligently pursued his rights the entire period he seeks equitably tolled.” See id. (citing Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 134 (2d Cir. 2000)); see also Holland v. Florida, 560 U.S. 631, 649 (2010). The respondent’s first argument, that a serious illness prevented him from attending his final hearing, is of little benefit because the record indicates that the respondent was hospitalized for 1 day--over 7 years before he filed the motion to reopen notifying the Immigration Court of the reason for his absence (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 4-6, 12, 37-40). See Guerrero-Santana v. Gonzales, 499 F.3d 90, 94 (1st Cir. 2007) (“The doctrine is not available as a means of rescuing a party who has failed to exercise due diligence.”).
 
*2 The respondent’s second argument--that his attorney provided ineffective assistance2 by stating that he had no relief available following the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order (see Respondent’s Br. at 7; Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 4-7, 13)--is unpersuasive as it is unaccompanied by compliance with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Ferreira v. Ban, 939 F.3d 44, 45-46 (1st Cir. 2019). “[T]he Lozada requirements are designed to give the BIA sufficient information to inform its decision without resorting to an evidentiary hearing.” Id at 46. Absent such an evidentiary basis, we decline to conclude that the respondent’s former counsel provided ineffective assistance--particularly when the respondent had years to gather evidence in support of his claim. See Asaba v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 9, 11-13 (1st Cir. 2004). Therefore, the respondent’s motion was untimely filed and did not warrant equitable tolling of the statutory deadline.
 
Furthermore, the respondent has not demonstrated that the particular circumstances of this case presents an “exceptional situation” that warrants the exercise of sua sponte authority to reopen these proceedings as a matter of discretion. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (“[A]s a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”) (citing Matter of J-J-, 22 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997))). We note that many aliens who have been present in the United States for a period of years have accumulated favorable equities subsequent to being ordered removed and generally these are not so exceptional as to warrant reopening sua sponte. See Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216, 1218 (BIA 2000) (“[I]t is the respondent’s burden to demonstrate that such a situation exists.”). In the present case, upon consideration of the record, including the respondent’s arguments of eligibility for adjustment of status relief and his family ties in the United States (Respondent’s Br. at 7-8), we conclude that sua sponte reopening is not warranted. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

However, the DHS opposed the respondent’s motion to reopen before the Immigration Court.

2

We construe the respondent’s position that “his attorney gave him the wrong advice” as an assertion of ineffective assistance of counsel in this context, because a less serious, “‘garden variety claim of excusable neglect”D’ would not warrant equitable tolling. See Holland, 560 U.S. at 651-52 (quoting Irwin v. Dep’t of Veterans Affairs, 498 U.S. 89, 96 (1990)).

IN RE: MIGRAN NSHANOVI ERZRUMYAN A.K.A. MIGRAN ERZROUNYAN A.K.A. MIGRAN N. ERZROUYAN

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Areg Kazaryan, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on May 10, 2018, when we denied the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen the Board’s dismissal of his appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal due to ineffective assistance of his prior counsel. On September 11, 2019, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of his prior counsel.1 The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and number-barred. See Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000); Matter of L-V-K-, 22 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1999). An alien is allowed to file a motion to reopen and to rescind an in absentia order of removal based on exceptional circumstances or because the alien did not receive a notice of hearing issued under sections 239(a)(1)-(2) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229(a)(1)-(2). Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). However, an alien may file only one such motion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The motion therefore will be denied as barred by regulation.
 
Even if we reached the merits of the motion, we remained unconvinced that the respondent has established that he put forth a viable ineffective assistance of counsel claim. To the extent that the respondent is continuing to raise a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and has submitted another letter from his prior counsel explaining in more detail why the respondent showed up a day late for his hearing, the respondent still has not demonstrated that he was prejudiced by his prior counsels’ actions or inactions (Respondent’s Motion, Attachment). The respondent’s motion reiterates the arguments raised in his prior motion. We considered those arguments and found them unpersuasive when viewed within the record as a whole before we denied his previous motion.
 
Regardless of whether the respondent has shown attorney error, he has not met his burden of demonstrating that counsel’s deficient performance prejudiced him, where the Immigration Judge verbally advised the respondent of the date of his hearing and personally served him with the notice of hearing (IJ at 1). Irrespective of prior counsel providing purportedly erroneous information to the respondent and the Immigration Judge, the respondent was aware that his immigration hearing was April 14, 2005. Martinez -Hernandez v. Holder, 778 F.3d 1086, 1088 (9th Cir. 2015) (an alien can show prejudice where counsel’s performance may have impacted the outcome of the case).
 
*2 Further, we do not find that the respondent’s case presents an exceptional situation that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Stuart V. Couch
FOR THE BOARD

1

We have considered all of the respondent’s supplemental filings in the adjudication of this motion.
