2019 WL 4054076 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MATEO LOPEZ-ABREGO
File: AXXX-XX9-873 - New York, NY
July 11, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pankaj Malik, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s January 29, 2019, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) did not file a response to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C), an in absentia removal order may be rescinded only (1) upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances, or (2) upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice of the hearing in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act, or that the alien was in Federal or state custody and did not appear through no fault of the alien. Sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and (ii) of the Act; see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii); Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722, 722-23 (BIA 1999).
 
The respondent entered the United States on March 11, 2001. He was detained upon entry, and on March 13, 2001, was personally served with a Notice to Appear (“NTA”). On September 25, 2001, a Notice of Hearing (“NOH”) was sent to the respondent, informing him that he should appear for his hearing on January 3, 2002, at the Immigration Court in New York, NY. The respondent did not appear for his hearing on that date, and an Immigration Judge ordered him removed after a hearing held in absentia. The respondent was mailed a copy of the in absentia removal order at his address on record.
 
More than 17 years later, on January 8, 2019, the respondent filed a motion to reopen his in absentia removal order with the Immigration Judge. On January 29, 2019, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion finding that he had not established exceptional circumstances or overcome the presumption of delivery of the notice of the hearing.
 
The respondent presents a due process challenge to the Immigration Judge’s 2002 in absentia order of removal, premised on his argument that he did not have proper notice of the hearing. The respondent asserts that he demonstrated a lack of notice or exceptional circumstances for not attending the scheduled hearing on January 3, 2002. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). He claims he only learned of the in absentia removal order in 2019 when he was taken into DHS’s custody (Respondent’s Br. at 6). He claims that the non-receipt of the January 3, 2002, hearing constitutes sufficient evidence to overcome the presumption of delivery.
 
*2 The respondent’s motion to reopen and to rescind the in absentia removal order was untimely, as it was filed on January 8, 2019, more than 180 days after the in absentia removal order was entered on January 3, 2002 (IJ at 1-3). See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not establish that equitable tolling of the 180-day deadline for filing his motion is warranted (IJ at 3-4). Alternatively, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not establish that exceptional circumstances beyond his control excused his failure to appear for his January 3, 2002, hearing (IJ at 3-4). See section 240(e)(1) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
The respondent was personally served with an NTA, advised of his address obligations, and the NOH was sent to the address he provided on the Form I-830, Notice to EOIR: Alien Address (IJ at 1-2; Exh. 1; NOH dated September 25, 2001). Ineffective assistance of counsel may constitute an exceptional circumstance if the ineffective assistance results in an alien’s failure to appear at his hearing. See Aris v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 595, 599 (2d Cir. 2008). We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s motion to reopen based on his claim of ineffective assistance of prior counsel does not comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-40 (BIA 1988) (IJ at 3).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the NOH mailed to the address in the Form I-830 was sufficient to constitute notice to the respondent (IJ at 4). The Form I-830 contains indicia of reliability. Specifically, the form indicates that upon release from legacy Immigration and Naturalization Service (“INS”) custody, the respondent reported his address to be 1047 Wilson Boulevard, Central Islip, New York 11722. In addition, the form is signed by a legacy INS officer. As the respondent was personally served with the NTA, he was required to notify the Immigration Court and the legacy INS of any change of address within five days. The last address provided by the respondent was the Form I-830, and therefore it was proper to use that address to send notice of hearing. Section 1229(a) of the Act requires notice of a change of address, yet does not place any restrictions on who may provide that notice.
 
The respondent asserts that unbeknownst to him, Ms. Quevedo, a notario, provided the address in the Form I-830 presumably when she posted bond. He implies that Ms. Quevedo did not advise him of the address she provided in the Form I-830. To the extent the respondent implies that because he did not sign the Form I-830, he is not responsible for the address reflected therein, we disagree (Respondent’s Br. at 4, 14). The notice indicates that the Wilson Boulevard address was provided by the respondent. The form does not indicate that the address was provided by Ms. Quevedo. The respondent has failed to show error in the mailing of the notice of hearing to the Wilson Boulevard address obtained from the Form I-830.
 
*3 We presume that a properly addressed notice sent via regular mail is received by the addressee. See Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665 (BIA 2008). However, the burden of proof for rebutting that presumption is weaker when items are sent by regular mail as opposed to certified mail. See id. The Board considers all relevant evidence in determining whether an alien has rebutted the presumption. Such evidence includes but is not limited to factors such as affidavits from the respondent and others who are knowledgeable about whether notice was received, whether due diligence was exercised in seeking to redress the situation, any prior applications for relief that would indicate an incentive to appear, and the respondent’s prior appearance at immigration proceedings, if applicable. See Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. at 665; see also Matter of C-R-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 677 (BIA 2008).
 
We are not persuaded that the respondent has rebutted the presumption of delivery. The NOH was mailed to the address provided by the respondent upon release from custody (Form I-830). The Immigration Judge reasonably noted that although the respondent claims that he advised the court at his June 14, 2001, hearing that Ms. Quevedo’s address was not where he was residing, the record lacks a change of address form that reflects any other address for the respondent other than the one he “repeatedly provided to the Court” (IJ at 4 and n.4). See section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(B) (eliminating the notice requirement where the alien fails to provide the required contact information); see also M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. at 674-75 (finding that a respondent cannot evade delivery of a properly sent Notice of Hearing by relocating without providing the required change of address and then request reopening of in absentia proceedings on the basis of a claim that he did not receive notice) (citation omitted). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not rebutted the presumption of delivery.
 
On appeal, the respondent maintains that he should have been provided more time to respond to the DHS’s opposition to his motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia removal order (Respondent’s Br. at 7-10). The respondent asserts that before he had an opportunity to respond to the DHS’s opposition, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion (Id.). Citing the Immigration Court Practice Manual, the respondent contends that he should have been allowed 10 days to respondent to DHS’s opposition (Id. at 8-9). See Immigration Court Practice Manual Rule 3.1(b)(iv). However, the Immigration Judge is not bound by parties’ filing deadlines from the Immigration Court Practice Manual, which itself is not binding authority. Appendix D from the Manual states that the deadlines are for guidance only, and that the Immigration Judge has discretion to set deadlines. Although the respondent asserts that the DHS’s opposition was untimely (Respondent’s Br. at 7), the Immigration Judge has discretion as to deadlines. Moreover, the respondent has not articulated what his response would have been if he had been provided additional time to respond, or otherwise shown that the outcome of his case would have been different.
 
*4 The respondent also relies upon an unpublished Board decision (Respondent’s Br. at 16-17). However, as the respondent acknowledges, unpublished Board decisions are not binding precedents (Id.). Matter of Echeverria, 25 I&N Dec. 512, 519 (BIA 2011). Further, the unpublished Board decision presented by the respondent on appeal rests upon specific factual scenarios pertinent to that case.
 
Moreover, having received proper notice of his hearing, we agree with the Immigration Judge that under the totality of the circumstances, the respondent has not met his burden of establishing that his failure to appear for his scheduled hearing was due to “exceptional circumstances.” See Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503, 509 (BIA 1996) (noting that one must look to the “totality of circumstances” to resolve this issue of exceptional circumstances); see also Matter of J-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 33 (BIA 1998); Matter of B-A-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 57 (BIA 1998).
 
The respondent also asserts that the in absentia removal order cannot stand because it is based on a non-compliant NTA or NOH (Respondent’s Br. at 11-12). He relies on Matter of Bulnes, 25 I&N Dec. 57 (BIA 2009) and Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 181 (BIA 2001). We disagree. For the reasons cited above, the respondent has not established that he received less than sufficient notice. Therefore, on this record, he has not established a non-compliant NTA or NOH.
 
Further, if it is necessary to reach this issue, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not shown that he exercised due diligence in his case (IJ at 4). The respondent does not allege that he took any steps to inquire into the status of his case during the more than 17-year period between the issuance of the in absentia removal order on January 3, 2002, and the filing of the motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia removal order on January 8, 2019.
 
The record establishes that the respondent was properly notified of the time and place of his scheduled removal hearing. The respondent does not allege that he was in Federal or state custody at the time of the hearing, and has not otherwise shown that his failure to appear was due to “exceptional circumstances” as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act. We are not persuaded by the respondent’s arguments that the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order should be rescinded. See 8 C. F. R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Therefore, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen his in absentia removal order.
 
*5 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
2019 WL 4054076 (BIA)

2019 WL 4054064 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WILLIAM ORLANDO JUAREZ-ARAUJO
File: AXXX-XX6-921 - Harlingen, TX
July 1, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kimberly Vega, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John G. Jenkins
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals the Immigration Judge’s February 6, 2019, decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind his in absentia order of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a motion for summary affirmance. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was detained shortly after entry into the United States in 2006. He was issued a Notice to Appear, specifying that he was to appear at the Immigration Court in Harlingen, Texas, on April 4, 2006, at 9:30 a.m. He concedes that he knew he was supposed to appear on April 4, 2006 (Motion to reopen, Tab C). He asserts that he did not appear because he moved to Houston and hired an attorney who turned out to be a notary. He asserts that the notary told him she would have his hearing moved to Houston, and that it might take a year or longer. The respondent claims he returned to her office a couple of months later, and she was no longer there. The respondent does not assert that he did anything else to obtain information about his removal proceedings or his immigration status until 2018.
 
A motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia removal order based on exceptional circumstances must be filed within 180 days after entry of the order. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel and that the deadline for filing his motion to reopen should be equitably tolled.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not demonstrate the required due diligence to merit equitable tolling of the entire period, approximately 12 years, that he seeks to toll. See Holland v. Florida, 560 U.S. 631, 649 (2010) (holding that the doctrine of equitable tolling will only be invoked when an applicant shows “that he has been pursuing his rights diligently”). The lack of any indication that the respondent contacted the Immigration Court or did anything else during the years after losing contact with the notary to show he was pursuing his rights diligently precludes him from demonstrating due diligence to merit equitable tolling of the time for seeking reopening and rescission of his in absentia removal order. The respondent otherwise does not establish any basis for reopening these proceedings. Accordingly, we will dismiss the appeal.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD
2019 WL 4054064 (BIA)

2019 WL 3857821 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DANIEL ORLANDO PIMENTEL-HERRERA
File: AXXX-XX5-977 - San Francisco, CA
June 11, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jody E. Santiago, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Cynthia A. Gutierrez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening; termination
 
*1 This case was last before us on April 30, 2002. At that time, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s underlying January 6, 2000, decision, in which he denied the respondent’s motion to reopen the proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on October 22, 1999, pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5). On July 23, 2018, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider and terminate the proceedings in light of Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S. Ct. 2105 (2018), and in the alternative, a motion to reopen and remand to the Immigration Judge. The Department of Homeland Security has filed a response in opposition to the respondent’s motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion to reconsider is untimely because it was not filed within 30 days of our April 30, 2002, decision. See section 240(c)(6)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6)(B); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). The respondent argues that his motion should be considered timely under the doctrine of equitable tolling. He argues that the Supreme Court’s decision in Pereira v. Sessions constitutes an extraordinary circumstance that prevented him from timely filing a motion to reconsider and that he pursued his case with reasonable diligence. He further argues that, under Pereira v. Sessions, the “putative” notice to appear issued against him did not specify the time and place of his first removal hearing and was insufficient to vest the Immigration Judge and the Board with jurisdiction over the proceedings. Thus, the respondent argues that the Board should terminate the removal proceedings.
 
We deny the respondent’s motion to the extent that he argues that the Immigration Judge lacked jurisdiction pursuant to Pereira v. Sessions. See Respondent’s Motion at 4-6. Subsequent to the Supreme Court’s opinion in Pereira, the Board issued Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. 441 (BIA 2018), in which we held that Pereira does not stand for the proposition that the respondent contends. See Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. at 443; Karingithi v. Whitaker, 913 F.3d 1158 (9th Cir. 2019) (deferring to the Board’s decision in Matter of Bermudez-Cota);; Lopez v. Barr, No. 15-72406, 2019 WL 2202952, at* 8 (9th Cir. May 22, 2019) (noting that a regulation, not the statutory definition of a notice to appear, governs what a notice must contain to vest jurisdiction and that Pereira simply has no application to the Immigration Court’s jurisdiction). Additionally, the respondent’s case is controlled by Matter of Miranda-Cordiero, 27 I&N Dec. 551, 554 (BIA 2019), in which we distinguished Pereira and held that pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5), a notice to appear that did not specify the time and place of the initial removal hearing does not require the rescission of an in absentia order of removal nor termination of proceedings. See also Matter of Pena-Mejia, 27 I&N Dec. 546, 548 (BIA 2019). Under these circumstances, we conclude that jurisdiction properly vested with the Immigration Judge such that reconsideration of our prior decision, reopening and rescission of the in absentia order of removal, or termination of these proceedings on jurisdictional grounds is not warranted.1
 
*2 The respondent also argues in the alternative that the Board should reopen the proceedings and remand to the Immigration Judge based on the ineffective assistance of his prior attorney. He acknowledges that his motion to reopen is untimely as it was filed more than 90 days after the final order of removal. See section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). However, he argues that the filing deadline should be equitably tolled because he did not learn of his prior attorney’s ineffective assistance until he met with his new attorney on July 17, 2018, and that he acted with due diligence in pursuing his case. Further, he argues that his prior attorney’s ineffective assistance prejudiced his case because she did not submit any evidence in support of the motion to reopen his in absentia removal order, which deprived him of the opportunity to remedy the prior in absentia order and to seek any relief from removal.
 
We conclude that, while the respondent has complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), he has not demonstrated due diligence warranting equitable tolling of the filing deadline. In order to be entitled to equitable tolling based on ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent must show due diligence in discovering his attorney’s deception, fraud, or error. Bonilla v. Lynch, 840 F.3d 757, 582 (9th Cir. 2016). Three factors are considered: (1) if (and when) a reasonable person in the respondent’s position would suspect the specific fraud or error underlying the motion to reopen; (2) whether the respondent took reasonable steps to investigate the attorney’s suspected fraud or error, or, if the respondent is ignorant of the attorney’s shortcomings, whether he made reasonable efforts to pursue relief; and (3) when the tolling period should end; that is, when the respondent definitively learns of the harm resulting from counsel’s deficiency. Id.; Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672, 679 (9th Cir. 2011).
 
The respondent was 16 years old at the time he entered the United States and has stated in his affidavit attached to his motion that his father hired Attorney Reinholdt to file a motion to reopen the in absentia removal order with the Immigration Judge and an appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion with the Board. He states that after the Board denied his appeal in 2002, he assumed his case was lost and that he did not become aware of the ineffective assistance of Ms. Reinholdt until he met with and retained his new attorney Jody Santiago in July 2018.
 
The Board found in its 2002 decision that the respondent had ample opportunity but did not provide any affidavits or other evidence to establish his claim that he believed he did not have to appear on his hearing date because he was a minor and thought he could appear with his father at his hearing on a different date. The Board also held the respondent did not show anything more than a misunderstanding about the hearing date and did not show any reason for failing to appear that rose to the level of exceptional circumstances. The respondent was an adult at the time of the Board’s decision, and a reasonable person in his position would suspect fraud or error or would take reasonable steps to investigate the attorney’s suspected fraud or error. Even assuming the respondent was ignorant of the attorney’s possible fraud or error, the respondent has not shown that he made reasonable efforts to pursue relief between 2002 and 2018 when he filed his motion to reopen. Bonilla v. Lynch, 840 F.3d at 583. The respondent has not detailed any efforts to seek legal assistance between 2002 and 2018 or explained why he waited 16 years to address his 2002 removal order. Id. Thus, the respondent did not make reasonable efforts to pursue relief and did not demonstrate the diligence necessary for equitable tolling.
 
*3 To the extent that the respondent requests sua sponte reopening (Respondent’s Motion at 16-17), the circumstances of this case do not present any exceptional situation that would warrant the exercise of our sua sponte authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the power to sua sponte reconsider or reopen proceedings is reserved for “exceptional situations” and “is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”).
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent also argues in his motion that under Pereira the putative notice to appear did not interrupt his period of continuous physical presence required to qualify for cancellation of removal, and the proceedings should be reopened to allow him to apply for such relief. However, in light of our finding that the respondent has not exercised the due diligence necessary for equitable tolling of the filing deadline and has not shown that the in absentia order of removal should be rescinded, we need not reach this issue.


2019 WL 3857821 (BIA)

2019 WL 3857814 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARLOS R. ARBAIZA CABRERA
File: AXXX-XX5-375 - Los Angeles, CA
June 3, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Karla Patricia Avelino, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kimberly Daniels-Stemley
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 10, 2018, denying his motion to reconsider the September 4, 2018, denial of his motion to reopen an in absentia order of removal. While his appeal was pending, the respondent filed a motion to remand to allow him to apply for adjustment of status. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion to remand. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded.
 
This Board must defer to the Immigration Judge’s factual findings, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, unless they are clearly erroneous. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
A motion to reconsider will only succeed if it identifies an error of fact or law in the Immigration Judge’s prior decision. Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b).
 
The procedural posture of the respondent’s appeal is as follows. After the respondent failed to appear for a December 11, 2008, hearing, an Immigration Judge ordered him removed in absentia (Exhs. 2, 4). On May 14, 2010, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order, which was granted by the Immigration Judge on June 7, 2010 (Exhs. 5, 7). After previous counsel withdrew from representation, the respondent appeared pro se at a February 10, 2012, hearing. He was given a continuance until July 13, 2013, to retain counsel. The Immigration Court changed the date of the hearing to January 11, 2013, and mailed a Notice of Hearing (NOH) to the address confirmed by the respondent at the February 10, 2012, hearing.
 
The respondent failed to appear at the January 11, 2013, hearing, and the court entered an in absentia removal order. On July 18, 2018, the respondent filed a motion to reopen based on his assertion that he failed to appear for the hearing because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing. On September 4, 2018, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent filed a motion to reconsider that decision, which the Immigration Judge denied on October 10, 2018. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reconsider.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen on several bases, including inter alia that, even if the respondent had rebutted the weaker presumption of delivery of a NOH sent through regular mail, the respondent failed to exercise due diligence in redressing the situation following the discovery of his in absentia order, thereby failing to show he is entitled to equitable tolling (9/4/18 IJ at 2). In particular, the Immigration Judge relied upon the respondent’s acknowledgment in his motion to reopen that he discovered that he had missed his hearing and was ordered removed in July 2013, yet he failed to file his motion to reopen his in absentia removal order until 2018 (Id.).
 
*2 We find it necessary to remand the record to the Immigration Judge for a proper analysis under Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665 (BIA 2008), as to whether the NOH was properly received by the respondent. In her September 4, 2018, denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen, the Immigration Judge, after assuming that the delivery presumption had been rebutted under the factors outlined in Matter of M-R-A-, incorrectly imposed an additional due diligence requirement in concluding that the respondent failed to establish a basis for equitable tolling. However, under the case law of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, in which these proceedings occurred, equitable tolling and due diligence analysis is not a ground for denying motions to reopen for lack of notice since by statute there is no time deadline for such motions. See Andia v. Ashcroft, 359 F.3d 1181, 1184 (9th Cir. 2004) (holding that motion to reopen an in absentia hearing based on lack of notice can be made at any time and may not be denied for lack of due diligence); sections 240(b)(5)(C), (c)(7)(C)(iii) of the Act. However, under Matter of M-R-A-, lack of due diligence can be considered as a factor among others in determining whether the presumption of delivery has been rebutted. See Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. at 674 (“In determining whether a respondent has rebutted the weaker presumption of delivery applicable in these circumstances, an Immigration Judge may consider a variety of factors including, but not limited to, the following: (1) the respondent’s affidavit; (2) affidavits from family members or other individuals who are knowledgeable about the facts relevant to whether notice was received; (3) the respondent’s actions upon learning of the in absentia order, and whether due diligence was exercised in seeking to redress the situation; (4) any prior application for relief, indicating that the respondent had an incentive to appear; (5) any prior application for relief filed with the Immigration Court or any prima facie evidence in the record or the respondent’s motion of statutory eligibility for relief, indicating that the respondent had an incentive to appear; (6) the respondent’s previous attendance at Immigration Court hearings, if applicable; and (7) any other circumstances or evidence indicating possible nonreceipt of notice.”). Based on the foregoing, we will sustain the appeal and remand the record to the Immigration Judge to evaluate the respondent’s motion to reopen his in absentia removal order under the framework of Matter of M-R-A-.1
 
The following orders will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
Mark Noferi
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In light of our remand for analysis of the respondent’s motion to reopen his in absentia removal order, we need not address at this time the respondent’s claims related to ineffective assistance of counsel and his motion to remand to pursue adjustment of status.

2019 WL 3857808 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IVAN RODRIGUEZ LUEVANO
File: AXXX-XX0-619 - Dallas, TX
May 24, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lorena S. Rivas, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely motion requesting that the Board reconsider our August 15, 2018, decision affirming the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen his order of removal in absentia. See section 240(c)(6)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6)(B); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded to the motion. The motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider must identify an error of fact or law in the decision for which reconsideration is being requested. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1). The respondent argues that the Board erred in affirming the decision of the Immigration Judge because his failure to appear in court was due to exceptional circumstances (Respondent’s Mot. to Reconsider at 5-7). Specifically, the respondent argues that he did not receive proper notice of his removal hearings because the DHS served him with an invalid Notice to Appear (“NTA”) pursuant to Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S. Ct. 2105 (2018), because it did not specify the date or time of his removal proceedings (Respondent’s Mot. to Reconsider at 5-7).
 
The respondent also argues that he made a good faith attempt to update the Immigration Court with his new address by notifying his prior attorney, and that his prior attorney’s failure to update the Immigration Court constitutes an exceptional circumstance warranting reopening (Respondent’s Mot. to Reconsider at 7-9). Finally, the respondent argues that because he made “all reasonable attempts to update his address with the Immigration Court, his in absentia order contravenes the Constitutional requirement of due process.” (Respondent’s Mot. to Reconsider at 10).
 
As a threshold issue, an NTA that does not specify the time and place of an applicant’s initial removal hearing vests an Immigration Judge with jurisdiction over the removal proceedings and meets the requirements of section 239(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a), so long as a notice of hearing specifying this information is later sent to the applicant. Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. 441 (BIA 2018). The respondent argues that his case is distinguishable from the circumstance outlined in Matter of Bermudez-Cota because he never received notice of his removal hearing and he does not seek termination of his removal proceedings (Respondent’s Mot. to Reconsider at 5-7).
 
*2 In this case, a hearing notice was sent to the address on file with the Immigration Court on September 23, 2016, specifying the date and time of the respondent’s removal proceedings. Thus, the NTA in this case was not defective and jurisdiction vested with the Immigration Judge, and the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind his in absentia order of removal. See id. at 446-47.
 
Moreover, the respondent did not file a Form EOIR-33 to report his correct address with the Immigration Court, even though the NTA, which was personally served on the respondent, provided the required warnings and consequences of his failure to attend any future hearings, as well as his obligation to notify the Immigration Court of his address and the consequences of failing to do so (Exh. 1). Thus, the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind his in absentia order of removal. See section 240(b)(5) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5); Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 674-75 (BIA 2008) (stating that an applicant must comply with the statutory responsibility to provide current address information under section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1)(F), in order to prevail on a motion to reopen or rescind an in absentia order of removal); see also Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, 560 F.3d 354 (5th Cir. 2009).
 
In addition, the Immigration Judge found that the prior attorney who the respondent claimed provided ineffective assistance of counsel by not updating the respondent’s address with the Immigration Court did not represent the respondent in his immigration proceedings and was not informed of the respondent’s address change (IJ at 2-3). The respondent does not identify any error in this determination, which is not clearly erroneous. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (the Board reviews findings of fact for clear error); see also Anderson v. City of Bessemer City, 470 U.S. 564, 574 (1985) (holding that ““[w]here there are two permissible views of the evidence, the factfinder’s choice between them cannot be clearly erroneous.”). Thus, because the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen on this basis, the respondent has not identified any error of fact or law in the Board’s affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s decision. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
Finally, as discussed above, the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. In addition, although the respondent argues on appeal that he exercised “all reasonable attempts to update his address with the Immigration Court,” there is no indication in the record that he attempted to file a change of address form himself with the Immigration Court (Respondent’s Mot. to Reconsider at 10). See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980) (holding that statements of counsel are not evidence). Thus, the respondent has not established that the Immigration Judge’s decision violated his due process rights. See Ka Fung Chan v. INS, 634 F.2d 248, 258 (5th Cir. 1981) (finding no due process violation when the applicant could not establish prejudice resulting from the claimed error).
 
*3 In sum, the respondent has not identified an error of fact or law in our previous decision in this case such that it merits reconsideration. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1). Accordingly, the motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: MAYRA CATALINA LOPEZ-VENEGAS

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Cecily E. Clements, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; stay of removal
 
*1 This case was previously before the Board on July 18, 2011, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s August 12, 2009, decision denying a motion to suppress evidence.1 The respondent has filed a motion to reopen based on the ineffective assistance of former counsel and to pursue special rule cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(2)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(2)(A), a form of relief for certain victims of domestic violence under provisions of the Violence Against Women Act (VAWA). The record before us does not contain a brief in opposition from the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). The motion to reopen will be granted.
 
Given the facts and circumstances presented in this case, including the lack of opposition from DHS, we find an exceptional situation warranting sua sponte reopening so the respondent has an opportunity to pursue an application for special rule cancellation of removal under VAWA (see Motion to Reopen, Attachments A-W). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). In particular, the respondent’s detailed affidavit and other evidence indicate that she was not advised of her potential eligibility for relief under VAWA and suffered abuse from her U.S. citizen husband (see Motion to Reopen at 4-12, Attachments A, O-P). See Bhasin v. Gonzales, 423 F.3d 977, 987 (9th Cir. 2005) (stating that facts presented in affidavits must be accepted as true unless inherently unbelievable).2
 
Consequently, we will reopen these proceedings sua sponte and remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings. On remand, the parties may submit additional arguments and evidence regarding the respondent’s application (or applications) for relief. Accordingly, the following orders are entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision, and for the entry of a new decision.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
Molly Kendall Clark

2019 WL 2613162 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VICTOR HUGO MIRON-COLIN A.K.A. VICTOR HUGO MIRON COLIN A.K.A. VICTOR H. MIRON-COLIN A.K.A. VICTOR MIRON
File: AXXX-XX2-771 - West Valley City, UT
April 3, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Adam L. Crayk, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Adam N. Greenway
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; administrative closure; remand; adjustment of status
 
*1 In a decision dated February 22, 2018, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed from the United States after denying his request for a continuance or administrative closure of proceedings. The respondent now appeals from that decision and also moves to remand the record for a new hearing based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against his former lawyer. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion to remand will be denied.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, was granted U nonimmigrant status by the DHS in 2014. Sections 101(a)(15)(U) and 214(p) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1101(a)(15)(U), 1184(p) (2012). However, in 2017 he was convicted in Utah of possessing a controlled substance with intent to distribute, a felony. UTAH CODE ANN. § 58-37-8(1)(a)(iii) (West 2017). Based on that conviction, the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) charged him with being removable as an alien convicted of a controlled substance violation (Exh. 1). Section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i). He conceded that charge below, through counsel (IJ at 1-2; Tr. at 6).
 
The respondent did not apply for relief from removal before the Immigration Judge. Instead, he moved to continue his proceedings so that he could apply for adjustment of status under section 245(m) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(m), before United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”), a component of the DHS (Exh. 3). The Immigration Judge denied the motion, however, finding no “good cause” for a continuance in light of the serious adverse discretionary factors militating against the respondent’s claim for adjustment of status (IJ at 2-4). 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6 (2018); Matter of L-A-B-R- et al., 27 I&N Dec. 405 (A.G. 2018).
 
In his appeal brief, the respondent does not challenge the Immigration Judge’s description of his offense conduct or otherwise dispute the judge’s assessment of the discretionary factors bearing on the merits of his continuance request. Instead, he argues that his former attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel (Respondent’s Br. at 4-9) and that the Immigration Judge erroneously found that his Utah drug conviction was an aggravated felony (Respondent’s Br. at 9-11). We find both arguments unpersuasive.
 
*2 The respondent’s ineffective assistance claim is based on former counsel’s alleged failure to file an application for section 245(m) adjustment on his behalf with the Immigration Judge (Respondent’s Br. at 6-8). This omission did not reflect deficient performance of former counsel’s duties, however, because the Immigration Judge would have had no jurisdiction to adjudicate such an application had it been filed. The Act and regulations make clear that jurisdiction over applications for section 245(m) adjustment lies solely with USCIS; Immigration Judges can neither adjudicate such applications in the first instance nor review DHS decisions denying them. See section 245(m)(1) of the Act (granting adjustment authority to “the Secretary of Homeland Security”); 8 C.F.R. § 245.24(k) (“USCIS shall have exclusive jurisdiction over adjustment applications filed under section 245(m) of the Act”). In sum, as the Immigration Judge could not have adjudicated the respondent’s application for section 245(m) adjustment, former counsel’s ““failure” to file such an application was not ineffective assistance.
 
The respondent also argues that the Immigration Judge mistakenly found him ineligible for section 245(m) adjustment by describing his Utah drug offense as an “aggravated felony” (Respondent’s Br. at 9-11). However, that argument mischaracterizes the Immigration Judge’s decision. In assessing whether the respondent had established “good cause” for a continuance, the Immigration Judge stated that he viewed the respondent’s “criminal behavior” as “an aggravated felony, distribution of a controlled substance....” (IJ at 3). We consider this passing statement to have been an expression of the judge’s opinion about the respondent’s conduct, not an announcement of a formal legal conclusion about the elements of his offense.
 
Also, the Immigration Judge did not state that an aggravated felony conviction would render the respondent “ineligible” for section 245(m) adjustment. Rather, the Immigration Judge properly determined that the nature and recency of the respondent’s criminal offense made him a poor candidate for a continuance because it diminished his chances of being granted adjustment of status in discretion. Matter of L-A-B-R- et al., 27 I&N Dec. at 418 (holding that an Immigration Judge may deny a continuance if she decides that the underlying relief is unlikely to be granted in discretion); see also Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785, 790 (BIA 2009) (listing “whether the respondent’s application ... merits a favorable exercise of discretion” as a relevant factor when evaluating a continuance request). Even if we assume that the respondent’s offense of conviction is not a categorical aggravated felony (a question we need not decide), we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the offense justified the denial of his continuance request.
 
*3 In light of the foregoing, we will dismiss the respondent’s appeal and deny his motion to remand.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Michael P. Baird
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: KAYDIAN MCKENZIE

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joshua E. Bardavid, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 On June 4, 2018, the respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, submitted a motion to reopen proceedings in which the Board dismissed her appeal on August 24, 2006. She also seeks a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
The respondent’s motion is statutorily barred as untimely because it was filed more than 11 years after the Board’s final administrative decision. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). However, the respondent asks that the Board sua sponte reopen proceedings. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). In this regard, she avers that the criminal convictions which formed the basis for the charges of removal have been vacated pursuant to New York Criminal Procedure Law section 440.10 on the basis of legal and constitutional defect, and that she has instead plead to criminal trespass under New York Penal Law section 140.10.
 
In support of her motion to reopen, the respondent has submitted copies of the Motion to Vacate Conviction she submitted to the state court, which was based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, as well as current certificates of disposition regarding her convictions (Motion, Tabs B, D). She has provided evidence relating to her rehabilitation, her family, and other related documents. The respondent contends that she is now, therefore, eligible to pursue cancellation of removal. Matter of Rodriguez-Ruiz, 22 I&N Dec. 1378 (BIA 2000)
 
Given the record presented, we find it appropriate to grant sua sponte reopening. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). On remand the respondent bears the burden of establishing that the convictions at issue were vacated on the basis of a procedural or substantive defect in the underlying proceedings, and not because of post-conviction events. Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621, 624 (BIA 2003); see also Saleh v. Gonzales, 495 F.3d 17 (2d Cir. 2007). Accordingly, the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings in accordance with this decision.
 
Edward R. Grant

2019 WL 2613122 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TOMASZ KOLODZIEJCZYK
File: AXXX-XX2-668 - Hartford, CT
March 14, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gail A. Dulay, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Adam E. Mattei
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on May 9, 2018, when we denied the respondent’s untimely and number-barred motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. On June 7, 2018, the respondent filed a timely motion to reconsider requesting the Board reconsider its decision denying his motion to reopen.1 The motion is also in the nature of another untimely and number-barred motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief in opposition to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
We first address that aspect of the motion requesting reconsideration. A motion to reconsider shall specify “errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). The respondent’s motion establishes no error in our determination that he did not establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and did not warrant sua sponte reopening. The respondent reiterates the arguments raised in his previous motion to the Board. We considered those arguments and found them unpersuasive when viewed within the record as a whole before we denied his motion to reopen. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. at 58 (stating that “a motion to reconsider is not a process by which a party may submit, in essence, the same brief presented on appeal . . .”). Disagreement with the result is not sufficient. Therefore, the motion to reconsider is denied.
 
As a motion to reopen, it is untimely and number barred, as it was not filed within 90 days of the Board’s February 22, 2013, final order dismissing his appeal, and it is not the respondent’s first motion to reopen. Sections 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(A), (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent continues to assert that equitable tolling of the time and number limits on his motion to reopen is warranted based on his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and seeks to submit evidence that he has now complied with the requirements laid out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Even assuming that the respondent has now properly complied with the Lozada requirements, he still has not established prejudice from prior counsel’s actions or inactions. See Jiang v. Mukasey, 522 F.3d 266, 270 (2d Cir. 2008) (stating that to succeed on a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance an applicant must show, inter alia, “that the applicant was prejudiced by his counsel’s performance.”). The immediate relative visa petition (Form I-130) that was filed on the respondent’s behalf by his second United States citizen spouse was not approved by the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) until months after the Board’s February 2013 final decision on the merits. Thus, it is speculative that a motion to remand would have been granted, especially given the procedural history of this case, i.e. the respondent previously attempted to adjust his status based on a prior marriage to a different United States citizen who failed to appear for the respondent’s hearing. Consequently, the respondent has not demonstrated that the ultimate outcome might have been affected by his prior counsels. Thus, the motion to reopen is untimely and number-barred, and the respondent has not shown prejudice to warrant equitable tolling based on ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 Finally, we decline to exercise our discretion to reopen proceedings sua sponte because the respondent has not demonstrated exceptional circumstances. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (stating that “as a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”). Having considered the record in its entirety and do not find present an exceptional circumstance sufficient to warrant the exercise of our sua sponte authority.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.2
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SHAREE LESLIE ANN CHERON A.K.A. SHAREE LAIDLEY
File: AXXX-XX3-528 - Miami, FL
March 11, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
George E. Crimarco, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; stay of removal
 
*1 This case was previously before the Board on June 25, 2008, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s November 13, 2007, decision denying her motion to reconsider the denial of a motion reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel.1 The respondent has filed a motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered removed in absentia, and to pursue adjustment of status based on an approved Form I-130 petition. The record before us does not contain a brief in opposition from the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). The respondent also requests a stay of removal. The motion to reopen will be granted, the removal order will be rescinded, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
Considering the facts and circumstances presented in the respondent’s motion, which has not been opposed by DHS, we conclude that reopening is warranted under Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 181 (BIA 2001). See also Matter of Anyelo, 25 I&N Dec. 337 (BIA 2010) (distinguishing Dominguez v. United States Att’y Gen., 284 F.3d 1258 (11th Cir. 2002), and concluding that the holding in Matter of G-Y-R- as to the notice required to authorize the entry of an in absentia order, is applicable to cases arising in the Eleventh Circuit). In Matter of G-Y-R-, we held that entry of an in absentia order of removal is inappropriate where the record reflects that the alien did not receive, and could not be charged with receiving, the Notice to Appear (NTA) that was served by mail at an address obtained from documents filed with the Service several years earlier. We concluded that section 239(a)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1), authorizes the entry of an in absentia order only after the respondent receives the warnings and advisals contained in the NTA.
 
In the instant motion to reopen and throughout these proceedings, the respondent has claimed that she never received the NTA or the Notice of Hearing (NOH) because they were mailed to an old address (Motion to Reopen at 1-3). In support of the motion, the respondent has submitted, inter alia, an affidavit and a change of address form reflecting an updated address (Motion to Reopen, Exhs. A, D). Consequently, we conclude that reopening and rescission of the in absentia removal order is warranted, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings. On remand, the respondent should have the opportunity to apply for adjustment of status or other relief for which she may be eligible. Accordingly, the following orders are entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, and the April 10, 2007, in absentia removal order is rescinded.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision, and for the entry of a new decision.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

As noted in our prior decision, this case has a lengthy procedural history, which is set forth in the Immigration Judge’s November 13, 2007, decision.

2019 WL 2464466 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: REINIS GEORGIYEVICH GURVICS A.K.A. REINIS GURVICS
File: AXXX-XX2-360 - Elizabeth, NJ
March 6, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Judy S. Resnick, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal on November 20, 2018, and he filed a timely motion to reopen on December 7, 2018. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion. We will reopen the proceedings, and remand the case to the Immigration Court.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Latvia, was found subject to removal as an aggravated felon, and as having violated a state law relating to a controlled substance violation. This was based on his being convicted in New Jersey in 2011 of an offense involving heroin.
 
The respondent contends in his motion that his conviction has been vacated, and he seeks to have the removal proceedings terminated. He offers a December 5, 2018, order from the criminal court, which indicates that a request to withdraw his guilty plea was granted. The order also states, however, that the motion for post-conviction relief based on ineffective assistance of counsel was denied.
 
A conviction vacated on the merits - because of legal or constitutional defect, including the failure to advise the defendant of the immigration consequences of his plea - is not a conviction for immigration purposes. However, a conviction vacated solely because of its immigration consequences remains a conviction for immigration purposes. See Matter of Adamiak, 23 I&N Dec. 878 (BIA 2006); Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003). See also Matter of Marquez Conde, 27 I&N Dec. 251, 255 (BIA 2018) (modifying the decision in Pickering to give it nationwide application); Matter of J.M. Acosta, 27 I&N Dec. 420, 428-29 (BIA 2018).
 
We will therefore remand the record to the Immigration Judge to enable the judge to determine the basis under which the conviction was vacated, and for further action, as appropriate. Accordingly, the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2019 WL 2464466 (BIA)
End of Document

2019 WL 2464463 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DENNIS AMILCAR TEREZON ZEPEDA
File: AXXX-XX3-451 - Newark, NJ
March 4, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John P. Leschak, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sean Gorman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals from the April 4, 2018, decision of the Immigration Judge denying his motion to reopen the proceedings sua sponte and based on changed country conditions. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) did not file an opposition to the appeal. During the course of the appeal, the respondent has filed two motions to remand the record based on Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S. Ct. 2105 (2018), and Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. 441 (BIA 2018). The DHS has opposed the respondent’s first motion. The motions will be denied. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent filed a motion with the Immigration Judge on March 27, 2018. In that motion the respondent argued, inter alia, that sua sponte reopening was required because the in absentia order of removal, entered on April 13, 2010, was legally invalid, as the Immigration Judge had improperly reopened previously terminated proceedings. The respondent argued that the Immigration Judge was precluded from issuing an in absentia removal order because the DHS had not issued a new Notice to Appear. The Immigration Judge rejected this argument, noting that proceedings had been terminated without prejudice.
 
On appeal, the respondent makes no persuasive argument that the Immigration Judge’s determination that proceedings had been previously terminated without prejudice is incorrect. Furthermore, the respondent does not cite to any relevant precedent which supports his claim that the Immigration Judge was precluded from entertaining a motion to reopen proceedings, and that the only avenue for the Immigration Judge to acquire jurisdiction would have been the issuance of a new Notice to Appear.
 
The respondent also argued that the in absentia order of removal should be rescinded, and proceedings reopened, based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent did not contend, as a basis for excusing the lack of timely filing of the motion, that he did not receive notice of the April 13, 2010, hearing. Rather he claimed exceptional circumstances. However, to the extent the respondent’s motion was based on exceptional circumstances, it was untimely, as such a motion must be filed within 180 days of the order, unless that period is equitably tolled. The Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent did not establish that he was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. In fact, the respondent conceded that previous counsel has attempted to contact him multiple times. The Immigration Judge rejected the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim as a basis for reopening, and concluded that the respondent did not exercise due diligence, such as to warrant equitable tolling.
 
*2 It is uncontested that the DHS had issued a Notice to Appear on November 1, 2005, but on December 14, 2006, the Immigration Judge terminated proceedings to allow the respondent to pursue adjustment of status. Adjustment of status was denied on February 10, 2009. After proceedings were reopened, respondent’s counsel, Ms. Rondon, appeared for the respondent at the reopened proceedings, on March 11, 2010, but the respondent did not appear and a continuance was granted. Ms. Rondon made several attempts to contact the respondent both prior to this hearing and subsequently. Ms. Rondon appeared again on behalf of the respondent on April 13, 2010, but the respondent did not appear and was ordered removed in absentia.
 
The respondent claims that his mother contacted another attorney, Ms. Quiroz, in March of 2010, but was informed to wait for notice of a court date, and no mention was made of the March 11, or April 13, 2010, dates. The respondent does not fully explain whether or not he had any contact with Ms. Rondon during this time. The respondent received the in absentia order dated April 13, 2010. The respondent’s mother claims she contacted Ms. Quiroz immediately and was informed a motion to reopen would need to be filed. The mother then paid an additional fee and was provided what she claims was a copy of the motion to reopen she believed was filed by Ms. Quiroz (Motion to Reopen at 5-6, citing Tab E). The mother was informed by Ms. Quiroz to simply wait for a decision on the motion to reopen. Ms. Quiroz disputes this account.
 
On March 14, 2018, the respondent was detained and his mother contacted Ms. Quiroz. She also contacted a new attorney, John Leschak, who is the respondent’s current attorney of record. Mr. Leschak learned that no motion to reopen had been filed by Ms. Quiroz, and Mr. Leschak filed the current motion to reopen proceedings, claiming ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of Quiroz, and arguing changed country conditions in El Salvador (Motion to Reopen at 7-19). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s rejection of the equitable tolling and ineffective assistance of counsel claim. We observe that the Immigration Judge specifically found that the respondent’s former attorney, Ms. Rondon, made several attempts to contact the respondent prior to the in absentia order. He found the respondent lacked due diligence, as he waited approximately 9 years before attempting to explain this lack of contact (IJ at 1). He then found that because of this lack of due diligence, the respondent had not shown any prejudice by the later acts of Ms. Quiroz (id.).
 
We acknowledge that the respondent attempted to establish in his motion that he was misinformed by Ms. Quiroz that the motion had been filed. This argument is made less persuasive by the declaration of Ms. Quiroz, in which she plainly states that the respondent did not make himself available to her and that the relevant contacts were through his mother (the respondent was an adult at the time). Furthermore, Ms. Quiroz plainly states that the payment receipts were likely for partial payment, that the draft of the motion was likely prepared for review before full payment, and that full payment was probably not made. Ms. Quiroz explained in detail the practices of her office. While we have no reason not to credit the sincerity of the statement of the respondent’s mother that she was informed that the motion had been filed, the respondent has not established that it is more likely that the declaration of Ms. Quiroz contains materially false statements than that his mother misunderstood the communications from Ms. Quiroz. We have taken into consideration the documentary corroboration of the respondent’s mother’s account - a copy of an unfiled motion and unannotated receipts - but that corroboration is not inconsistent with the statement of Ms. Quiroz.
 
*3 While, due to the passage of time, and due to the respondent’s continuous lack of personal cooperation with his attorneys, there is no record of the respondent’s state of knowledge regarding his removal proceedings, it seems clear that the respondent did not exercise the normal due diligence that an alien in removal proceedings should exercise. This is even more the case because the respondent had engaged in criminal activities, and had - according to the reasonable inferences that the Immigration Judge drew from the record - ignored his responsibility to take reasonable measures to attend his scheduled hearing. Furthermore, the respondent allowed 9 years to pass, after being ordered removed, before he took any significant steps to contact immigration authorities directly about his status, and he did not, in fact, take such steps until he had been detained. Upon de novo review, we agree with the Immigration Judge that due diligence was not shown.
 
The respondent also argued that proceedings should be reopened based on changed country conditions in El Salvador, with respect to the treatment of former gang members in that country. The Immigration Judge did not address this issue.
 
There is no time limit on the filing of a motion to reopen if the basis of the motion is to apply for asylum or withholding of removal and it is based on changed country conditions arising in the country of nationality, if the evidence offered in support of the motion is material and was not available and would not have been discovered or presented at the previous proceeding. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i). If an alien has been ordered removed in absentia, there is no requirement that the in absentia removal order be rescinded. See Matter of J-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 161 (BIA 2013). To the extent that the respondent contends that his motion should be granted because of changed country conditions, we conclude that the provided evidence does not establish materially changed conditions in El Salvador.
 
The respondent submitted three copies of newspaper articles which provided information and analysis on such subjects as violence in El Salvador and the relationship between the government and violent gangs, such as the gang to which the respondent used to belong, and three charts detailing homicide rates in El Salvador in recent years (Respondent’s Br. Exh. B, 3-11, Respondent’s Motion, Exh. T, 104-112). The evidence submitted with the respondent’s motion is insufficient to demonstrate that country conditions in El Salvador have materially changed since the time of his last hearing. A material change is not simply an incremental variation in general conditions of violence, but rather a change with a material effect on a respondent’s potential of being persecuted in the relevant county. The evidence which the respondent relies on for his claim of a material change does not amount to more than incremental variations in the government and society’s attitudes to crime and to former gang members. A continuation of the conditions that existed at the time of the respondent’s last hearing does not constitute a material change, such as to warrant reopening of these proceedings.1 Matter of S-Y-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 247, 257 (BIA 2007). As such, the respondent’s motion to reopen based on changed circumstances will be denied.
 
*4 The respondent, on August 20, 2018, filed a motion in which he argued that the NTA, issued on November 1, 2005, (Exh. 1) was defective for purposes of placing him in proceedings, citing the Supreme Court’s ruling in Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S. Ct. at 2114-16 (holding that an NTA that does not designate a specific time and place of an alien’s removal proceedings does not trigger the Act’s stop-time rule ending his period of continuous presence in the United States for purposes of section 240A(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)). In Pereira v. Sessions, the Supreme Court held that the stop-time rule was not triggered by a notice to appear that failed to identify the date and time for the hearing. This holding does not require or compel termination of proceedings. A notice to appear that does not specify the date and time of the alien’s initial removal hearing vests an Immigration Judge with jurisdiction over the removal proceedings, and meets the requirements of section 239(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1) when a notice of hearing specifying this information is later sent to the alien. Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. at 445-47. See also Karingithi v. Whitaker, 913 F.3d 1158 (9th Cir. 2019); Hernandez-Perez v. Whitaker, 911 F.3d 305, 312-16 (6th Cir. 2018). The record reflects that notice was properly sent to the respondent, on August 3, 2006. Moreover, the respondent did not object to the notice to appear before the Immigration Judge thus waiving any claim with respect to the notice to appear. Therefore, we disagree with the respondent’s contentions regarding subject matter jurisdiction.
 
On September 21, 2018, the respondent filed another motion to the Board. This motion was filed after the issuance of Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. at 441, and the respondent argued that he is eligible for cancellation of removal as the incomplete NTA did not end his continuous physical presence under the holding in Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S. Ct. at 2105 (Motion to Remand, Sept. 21, 2018, at 5-6). This motion will be denied because, in Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000), we held that a motion to remand filed during the pendency of an the appeal of an Immigration Judge’s denial of a motion to reopen is not excepted from the numerical restriction that permits the filing of only one motion to reopen. Even if not number barred, the motion is without merit because the respondent is statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal. An alien who has been subject to an in absentia order of removal is, with certain exceptions not applicable here, ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A of the Act for a period of 10 years following the final order of removal. See section 240(b)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(7).
 
*5 Finally, we decline the respondent’s request to exercise our sua sponte reopening authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Sua sponte authority is invoked sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of the time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The respondent has not demonstrated the presence of such exceptional circumstances.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motions to remand are denied.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

While the Immigration Judge did not address the issue, we review, under the circumstances of this case, the issue of whether the evidence submitted would warrant reopening under a de novo standard. Hence, while we acknowledge the respondent’s statement that the Immigration Judge should have addressed the issue, the respondent cannot demonstrate prejudice from the Immigration Judge’s lack of analysis of this issue.

2019 WL 2464461 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ELIZABETH MARIA ESTEVEZ DE MARTINEZ
File: AXXX-XX1-305 - New York, NY
March 4, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael A. Areizaga, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 On September 26, 2018, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider and/or reopen after the Board, on August 27, 2018, denied her untimely motion to reopen. The Board had dismissed her appeal on August 25, 2009. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider contests the correctness of a decision based upon the previous factual record, whereas a motion to reopen seeks a new hearing based on new or previously unavailable evidence. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 57-58 (BIA 2006); sections 240(c)(6), (7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6), (7); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(b), (c). A party seeking reconsideration requests a decision be reexamined in light of alleged legal or factual errors, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. Id.; Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991).
 
The respondent challenges the Board’s finding, in our August 27, 2018, decision, that reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel was not warranted as she did not exercise due diligence in pursuing her claim. She essentially contends that she was not aware that she could pursue adjustment of status and a waiver of inadmissibility because her attorney did not advise her of this fact (Motion at 5; 2/26/18 MTR, Tab A, para. 14). However, this does not refute the fact that the respondent was present at the two separate hearings where her potential eligibility for a visa petition, adjustment of status, and a waiver of inadmissibility were extensively discussed (Tr. at 184-87, 191-211). The issue of her eligibility for such relief was also raised on appeal, and she has not alleged ignorance of this fact. As such, we find no merit to her assertion that she discovered her former counsel’s ineffectiveness only after speaking with her current counsel. Moreover, we find no error in our determination that she did not show prejudice as she failed to demonstrate that she warranted a favorable exercise of discretion for purposes of adjustment and the noted waiver in light of her extended history of providing falsehoods to United States authorities. As such, reconsideration is not warranted.
 
To the extent that the respondent seeks to introduce evidence for the Board’s consideration, this aspect of her motion is in the nature of one seeking reopening. INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988). As she has filed prior motions to reopen, and since this motion was filed more than 8 years after the Board’s final administrative decision, it is statutorily both time and number-barred. Section 240(c)(7) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent asks that this evidence, relating to her spouse’s medical issues, be considered in conjunction with her prior motion to reopen for reexamination of her equities and evidence of rehabilitation to support a favorable finding with respect to discretion (Motion at 6).
 
*2 However, the respondent has not explained how medical evidence pertaining to her husband relates to a showing of rehabilitation on her part, or would otherwise warrant granting this untimely motion. This evidence also has no bearing on whether she has demonstrated that her proceedings should be reopened based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Absent such a showing, reopening is not warranted.
 
In sum, neither reopening of the proceedings nor reconsideration of the Board’s prior decision is appropriate. As such, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2019 WL 2464461 (BIA)
End of Document
