2019 WL 2464452 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WENSTON EMMANUEL FRAY
File: AXXX-XX9-146 - Miami, FL
February 27, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David A. Silk, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on May 28, 2009, at which time we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings for ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has now filed an untimely and number barred motion to reopen on June 14, 2018. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be granted.
 
On August 11, 2008, this Board affirmed the Immigration Judge’s December 21, 2006, decision finding the respondent removable under section 237(a)(2)(E)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(E)(i), for having been convicted of a crime of domestic violence, and under section 237(a)(2)(A)(ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii), for having been convicted of two crimes involving moral turpitude.1 Regarding the respondent’s removability under section 237(a)(2)(E)(i) of the Act, the respondent was charged with, and pled guilty to, simple battery under Fla. Stat.§ 784.03(1). In a lodged charge, the DHS also charged the respondent with having been convicted in the Palm Beach County Circuit Court for the offense of Burglary of a Conveyance and Grand Theft Auto, in violation of Florida Statutes section 810.02(a) and 812.014(1), (2).
 
In his motion, the respondent argues that due to fundamental, intervening case law, he is no longer removable under sections 237(a)(2)(E)(i) and (a)(1)(A) of the Act, and he has submitted various copies of statutes and case law in support of his argument. We agree. Based on intervening case law, the respondent is no longer removable under section 237(a)(2)(E)(i) of the Act, for having been convicted of domestic violence. Moreover, while the respondent does not address his theft conviction, we agree that under current case law he has not been convicted of a second crime involving moral turpitude for purposes of section 237(a)(2)(A)(ii) of the Act.
 
We first address the respondent’s simple battery conviction under Fla. Stat.§ 784.03(1), and his resulting removability under section 237(a)(2)(E)(i) of the Act. We conclude that this statute is not a crime of violence under 18 U.S.C. § 16(a) because it does not include as an element the use, attempted use, or threatened use of violent physical force against the person or property of another. Under Florida law, a battery occurs when a person either (1) “actually and intentionally touches or strikes another person against (his) will” or (2) “intentionally causes bodily harm to another person”. See Fla. Stat. § 784.03(1)(a). In Johnson v. United States, 130 S.Ct. 1265 (2010), the Supreme Court found that mere offensive touching under Fla. Stat. § 784.03(1) does not constitute a crime of violence because it does not have, as an element, the use of physical force. Moreover, Florida courts have repeatedly held that violating Florida’s battery statute does not require the use of physical force. See Perkins v. State, 576 So.2d 1310 (Fla. 1991); State v. Reams, 961 So.2d 211 (Fla. 2007); Bradley v. State, 106 So. 3d 530, 532 (Fla. App. Ct. 2013); Santiago v. State, 76 So.3d 1027 (Fla. App. Ct. 2011). Lastly, the Board held, in Matter of Guzman-Polanco, 26 I&N Dec. 713 (BIA 2016), that under 18 U.S.C. § 16(a), a statute that requires that the defendant intentionally inflict injury to a victim does not necessarily involve the use of violent physical force as required under Johnson v. United States, because an intentional act that results in injury to the victim can be accomplished in multiple ways that do not involve the intentional use of violent physical force.
 
*2 Similarly, we find that the respondent is no longer removable for having been convicted of two crimes involving moral turpitude under section section 237(a)(2)(A)(ii) of the Act. “Moral turpitude” generally refers to conduct that is “[i]nherently base, vile, or depraved, and contrary to the accepted rules of morality and the duties owed between persons or to society in general.” See Matter of Ortega-Lopez, 26 I&N Dec. 99, 100 (BIA 2013). For a crime to involve moral turpitude, it must involve reprehensible conduct and a culpable mental state. See Matter of Ortega-Lopez, 26 I&N Dec. at 99; see also Matter of Silva-Trevino, 26 I&N Dec. 826, 834 (BIA 2016)1 (Silva-Trevino III) (“to involve moral turpitude, a crime requires two essential elements: reprehensible conduct and a culpable mental state”).
 
In determining whether a crime involves moral turpitude, the categorical approach is first applied in order to examine if there is a “realistic probability,” not a “theoretical possibility” that the criminal statute at issue would be applied to reach conduct that does not involve moral turpitude. See Matter of Louissaint, 24 I&N Dec. 754, 757 (BIA 2009); see also Gonzales v. Duenas-Alvarez, 549 U.S. 183, 192 (2007); Matter of Cortes Medina, 26 I&N Dec. 79, 82 (BIA 2013).
 
In his 2007 decision, the Immigration Judge acknowledged that the respondent had a lengthy criminal record, including a series of arrests as well as the convictions as charged on his Notice to Appear (IJ at 6-9). However, the respondent’s convictions at issue include resisting arrest without violence under Fla. Statutes § 843.02, disorderly conduct under Fla. Stat. § 877.03, and, as discussed above, simple battery under Fla. Stat. 784.03.
 
The crimes of assault and battery include a broad spectrum of misconduct, ranging from relatively minor offenses such as simple assault to serious offenses such as assault with a deadly weapon. See Matter of Fualaau, 21 I&N Dec. 475, 477 (BIA 1996); Matter of Danesh, 19 I&N Dec. 669, 670 (BIA 1988). We have held that assault and battery offenses may or may not involve moral turpitude. See Matter of Solon, 24 I&N Dec. 239, 241 (BIA 2007); Matter of Danesh, 19 I&N Dec. at 670. To find moral turpitude, a statute must require a sufficient level of intent by the perpetrator and a sufficient level of harm to the victim. See Matter of Solon, 24 I&N Dec. at 242; Matter of Perez-Contreras, 20 I&N Dec. 615, 618-19 (BIA 1992).
 
*3 Simple assault crimes generally are not considered to be crimes involving moral turpitude because they may involve mere touching. See Matter of Perez-Contreras, 20 I&N Dec. at 618; Matter of Short, 20 I&N Dec. 136, 139 (BIA 1989). The statute under which the respondent was convicted, Florida Statute § 784.03, provides: “The offense of battery occurs when a person (1) actually and intentionally touches or strikes another person against the will of the other; or (2) intentionally causes bodily harm to another person.” As such, because the statute reaches conduct involving mere touching, Fla. Stat. § 784.03 does not meet the definition of a categorical crime involving moral turpitude. See Matter of Garcia-Hernandez, 23 I&N Dec. 590 (BIA 2003); Matter of Fualaau, 21 I&N Dec. 475 (BIA 1996).
 
Similarly, resisting arrest without violence under Fla. Statutes § 843.02 and disorderly conduct under Fla. Stat. § 877.03 do not categorically involve moral turpitude. The disorderly conduct statute proscribes any acts which “corrupt the public moral, or outrage the sense of public decency, or affect the peace and quiet of persons who may witness them ....” As the respondent argues in his motion, “affecting the peace and quiet of witnesses,” which is the minimum criminal conduct necessary under the statute, requires neither the requisite reprehensible conduct nor culpable mental state to qualify as a crime involving moral turpitude. See generally Matter of Khourn, 21 I&N Dec. 1041 (BIA 1997); Matter of Abreu-Semino, 12 I&N Dec. 775, 777 (BIA 1968) (finding that crimes in which evil intent is not an element, no matter how serious the act or harmful the consequences, do not involve moral turpitude”).
 
Lastly, the statute for resisting arrest, under Fla. Stat. § 843.02, states, in pertinent part, that “whoever shall resist, obstruct, or oppose any officer ... or other person legally authorized to execute process in the execution of legal process ... without offering or doing violence to the ... officer, shall be guilty of a misdemeanor ...”. The crime of interfering with a police officer is analogous to assault, and only involves moral turpitude when it involves the use of violent physical force. See Matter of Logan, 17 I&N Dec. 367, 368 (BIA 1980). Inasmuch as Fla. Stat. § 843.02 does not require physical force, and instead reaches conduct which would constitute simple assault, it does not constitute a crime involving moral turpitude. See Matter of Ahortalejo-Guzman, 25 I&N Dec. 465, 466 (BIA 2011); Matter of Fualaau, 21 I&N Dec. at 475.
 
*4 As such, we find that the respondent is no longer removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(ii) of the Act, for having been convicted of two crimes involving moral turpitude. Inasmuch as the respondent is also not removable under section 237(a)(2)(E) of the Act or section 237(a)(1)(A) of the Act, we will terminate the respondent’s proceedings. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is granted, and the proceedings are terminated without prejudice.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Immigration Judge did not find the respondent removable under section 237(a)(1)(A) of the Act, and this charge of removability is not currently at issue (IJ at 4, 13; Tr. at 57).


2019 WL 2464452 (BIA)

2019 WL 2464429 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RUBEN LOPEZ-PEREZ
File: AXXX-XX6-549 - Cleveland, OH
February 11, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Patrick Espinosa, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeared before an Immigration Judge on July 30, 2018, and agreed to depart the United States voluntarily under safeguards. On August 30, 2018, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, and appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 13, 2018, denying the motion. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that he established in his motion to reopen that his former attorney provided him with ineffective assistance of counsel, inasmuch as counsel erred by not filing an asylum application on his behalf. He further asserts that former counsel incorrectly advised him to request that the Immigration Judge allow him to depart the United States voluntarily. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998) (stating that ineffective assistance of counsel requires that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair). However, the record contains a statement from the respondent’s former counsel wherein he states that the respondent did not provide him with evidence to support his application for asylum (Motion at F). Counsel further states that the respondent told him that he did not fear returning to Guatemala and instead wished to depart the United States voluntarily (Motion at F). The respondent disputes the veracity of former counsel’s statement (Respondent’s Br. at 6-8).
 
There is no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not establish that his former counsel denied him due process by not filing an asylum application on his behalf. The Immigration Judge found the statement of the respondent’s former counsel to be credible, and we are unable to discern clear error in that determination. Hence, it appears that the respondent chose not to apply for asylum in the United States and has not established that he is a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
In addition, we decline to reopen these proceedings sua sponte (Respondent’s Br. at 9-12). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s and the Immigration Judge’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship). The respondent, as stated, has not established that he is a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We are also unable to identify any additional circumstances to warrant the sua sponte reopening of these proceedings.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward F. Kelly
FOR THE BOARD
2019 WL 2464429 (BIA)
End of Document

2019 WL 2160112 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GIOVANNY CAMACHO FLORES
File: AXXX XX0 686 - Orlando, FL
February 1, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Daniela Hogue, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Chelsea Rubin
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. On May 21, 2015, the Immigration Judge issued an order of removal in absentia. On October 22, 2015, and April 25, 2018, the Immigration Judge denied motions to reopen. The respondent filed a timely appeal from the Immigration Judge’s April 25, 2018, order. On May 16, 2018, the Board denied the respondent’s motion for an emergency stay and the respondent was removed on May 23, 2018. The Department of Homeland Security requests that the Board summarily affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent challenges the Immigration Judge’s April 25, 2018, order denying his second motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of former counsel. The respondent contends that his former counsel apparently received notice of his hearing, but did not advise the respondent of the hearing date. The respondent also alleges that former counsel did not advise him that an in absentia order had been issued or that former counsel was filing a motion to reopen. Since former counsel did not appear at his hearing and did not notify the respondent of the hearing, the respondent contends that his failure to appear was due to ineffective assistance of former counsel.
 
Since notice to counsel is notice to the respondent, the respondent is deemed to have notice. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2). See also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). Therefore the respondent was required to file his motion to reopen within 180 days of the in absentia order of removal and demonstrate exceptional circumstances as to why he did not appear. Section 240(b)(5) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
The respondent’s first motion to reopen was timely filed, but he did not demonstrate exceptional circumstances. The respondent did not file a Notice of Appeal with respect to the Immigration Judge’s October 22, 2015, order denying the first motion to reopen.
 
*2 The respondent’s second motion, the denial of which is currently before us on appeal, was not timely filed. The respondent contends that equitable tolling of the time and number bar should apply due to ineffective assistance of former counsel. See Avila-Santoyo v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 713 F.3d. 1357, 1362 n. 3, 1364-65 (11th Cir. 2013) (equitable tolling applies to in absentia orders); Montano-Cisneros v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 514 F.3d 1224 (11th Cir. 2008). The respondent has complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). There is no evidence that former counsel responded to the complaint.
 
The Immigration Judge properly declined to equitably toll the time and number bars based on ineffective assistance of counsel. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not demonstrate that he pursued his rights diligently. See Avila-Santoyo v. Atty Gen., 713 F.3d. at 1363 n. 5 (11th Cir. 2013). The respondent was aware of his in absentia order of removal as of January 2016, but his second motion was not filed until April 17, 2018, about 15 months later (IJ Attachment, April 25, 2018; Motion to Reopen, Tab A at paras. 14-20, April 17, 2018).
 
Soon after becoming aware of the in absentia order of removal, the respondent discussed his case with his former lawyer and then Catholic Charities (Motion to Reopen, Tab A at paras. 14-21), but there is no evidence that he engaged his former lawyer or Catholic Charities to formally review or further pursue his case. He avers that he reached out to a couple of other lawyers but this general assertion does not demonstrate that he was diligently pursuing his rights. Further, he did not contact his current firm until February 2017, over a year after learning about the in absentia order of removal. Considering these circumstances, the Immigration Judge correctly denied the respondent’s motion.
 
We decline to exercise our sua sponte discretionary authority to reopen proceedings because the respondent has not demonstrated an exceptional situation justifying the exercise of our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2019 WL 2160112 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOAQUIN HERNANDEZ
File: AXXX-XX5-140 - El Paso, TX
January 31, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ivan Yocub, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on March 16, 2005. On January 3, 2018, the Board upheld an Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen these proceedings to seek asylum and related relief based on changed country conditions in El Salvador. On April 3, 2018, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen. He later filed a supplemental motion to reopen, with an accompanying request for a stay of removal. The Board denied his stay request on October 2, 2018. The motion to reopen and supplemental motion, which remain pending and to which the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded, will be denied.
 
The respondent asserts that reopening is warranted because he received ineffective assistance of counsel from the attorney who represented him on his first motion to reopen and on appeal before the Board. The respondent faults counsel for filing “a subpar motion to reopen and brief in support of appeal.” (Motion to Reopen at 1). Specifically, he argues that he was denied reopening because counsel (1) provided only minimal information regarding country conditions in El Salvador in 2005; (2) made no meaningful comparison between conditions that existed at that time and current conditions; and (3) failed to argue that evidence of changed country conditions was material to his claims for relief (Id. at 7).
 
The respondent also argues that reopening is warranted because he did not receive notice of his 2005 hearing (Motion to Reopen at 23-27). The respondent argues that his Notice to Appear was deficient because it did not provide a date and time of the hearing, and that he never received a Notice of Hearing (Id. at 26). He further contends that no immigration officer told him that he had to go to court or provide the court with an updated address (Id. at 27 & Exh. A ¶ 18). The respondent claims he was told he was “free to travel throughout the country with the papers[,], that he cannot read Spanish or English and that he had no idea what the Notice to Appear said (Id., Exh. A ¶ 18).
 
The respondent also seeks sua sponte reopening pursuant to our limited discretionary authority under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a) (Motion to Reopen at 1, 22).
 
In his supplemental motion to reopen, the respondent argues that proceedings should be reopened and terminated for lack of jurisdiction based on Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S.Ct. 2105 (2018) (Supplemental Motion to Reopen at 2-7). According to the respondent, Pereira establishes that his Notice to Appear was invalid because it lacked a date and time of hearing, and that, consequently, the Immigration Court never acquired jurisdiction over his proceedings. The respondent claims that service of a subsequent notice of hearing does not “cure” a deficient Notice to Appear (Id.). Finally, and in the alternative, the respondent reiterates that his proceedings should be reopened based on lack of notice (Id. at 7-9).
 
*2 To the extent the respondent seeks reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel, the instant motion is time and number-barred. See sections 240(c)(7)(A) and 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A), 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i). Even if the motion was not so barred, the respondent is not entitled to reopening on this basis. To prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, a respondent must show that the claimed ineffective representation resulted in substantial prejudice. See Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 n.2 (5th Cir. 2008); Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162, 165 (5th Cir. 2006). Proving prejudice requires making a prima facie showing that the alleged violation affected the outcome of the proceedings. See Okpala v. Whitaker, 908 F.3d 965, 971 (5th Cir. 2018). The respondent has made no such showing here.
 
The respondent has not made a prima facie showing of changed country conditions in El Salvador that are material to his claim and that would render him eligible for relief. We acknowledge that the country conditions evidence proffered with the motion reflects that there has been an increase in gang violence in El Salvador since the respondent’s in absentia hearing (Motion to Reopen, Exhs. C, D). However, the respondent has not meaningfully shown that he may become a victim of such violence, or that any violence he may encounter would be on account of a protected ground under the Act.
 
The respondent fears that he will be targeted by gang members upon his repatriation based on his membership in his family because gang members threatened his nephew’s wife and her family after she refused their extortion efforts and refused to “turn over” her son to collaborate with the gang (Id. at 3, 16). The only pieces of evidence he has proffered in support of his fear, however, are his own asylum application and supporting declaration (Motion to Reopen, Exh. A). We afford limited weight to the respondent’s statements regarding his family’s experiences in El Salvador. The respondent does not appear to have firsthand knowledge of these events, which occurred years after he came to the United States (Id., Exh.A). The respondent has also offered no explanation of how he came to be aware of these events (Id.).
 
Moreover, the respondent avers that his nephew’s wife came to the United States in 2014 (Motion to Reopen, Exh. A ¶ 7). The respondent has not provided any corroborating evidence from her or from anyone else who would have had firsthand knowledge of these events. At this time and on this record, the respondent has not shown that he presently may face persecution or torture in El Salvador.1
 
*3 Based on the foregoing, we find that the respondent has failed to demonstrate substantial prejudice. We therefore find no basis to grant this untimely and number-barred motion to reopen.
 
The respondent also has not shown that he is eligible for reopening based on lack of notice of his hearing. We are satisfied that the respondent received adequate notice for the reasons articulated in our last decision.
 
We find the respondent’s argument that no one told him to come to court or to provide an address unpersuasive. His Notice to Appear provides a certification from a border patrol agent that he was given oral notice in the Spanish language of the consequences of his failure to appear for a hearing and of the need to provide the court with an address (Admin. Record, Exh. 1). The respondent’s statement to the contrary, without more, is unconvincing.
 
Moreover, even assuming the respondent cannot read Spanish or English, he has made no showing that, in the period between his initial apprehension in January 2005 and his apprehension by immigration officials in August 2014, he made any effort to discover the contents of the “papers.” The respondent does not dispute that the Notice to Appear was in his possession in that period of over 9 years. Given the entirety of the circumstances, we remain persuaded that the respondent received legally sufficient notice of his hearing.
 
The respondent’s reliance on Pereira v. Sessions is misplaced. In Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. 441 (BIA 2018), which post-dated Pereira v. Sessions and the date of filing of the instant motion, the Board held that a Notice to Appear that does not specify the time and place of an alien’s initial removal hearing vests an Immigration Judge with jurisdiction over the removal proceedings and meets the requirements of section 239(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a), so long as a notice of hearing specifying this information is later sent to the alien. Here, as we have already discussed, the respondent received adequate notice of his hearing.
 
We further note that the Act states that any alien who, after receiving the written notice required under sections 239(a)(1) or 239(a)(2) of the Act, fails to attend his hearing he shall be ordered removed in absentia. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act. Moreover, the relevant regulations pertaining to vesting of a charging document with the Immigration Court; defining the essential contents of a Notice to Appear (which does not mention time and place of the hearing); the alien’s obligation to provide an address, and the conduct of in absentia removal proceedings, were all complied with here. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.14(a), 1003.15(c), 1003.26(c), & 1003.26(d).
 
*4 We acknowledge that the Pereira case did involve a case conducted in absentia and included a discussion of the operation of sections 239(a)(1) and 239(a)(2) of the Act within that context. See Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S.Ct. at 2117-18. However, Pereira involved the applicability of the stop-time rule for cancellation of removal, which is not at issue here. Moreover, unlike the respondent, Pereira provided an address to the DHS prior to the issuance of his in absentia order. See id. at 2112.
 
For all of the foregoing reasons, we decline to reopen and terminate these proceedings based on Pereira. Finally, we do not find exceptional circumstances that would warrant sua sponte reopening of these proceedings. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and supplemental motion to reopen are denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent relies on Hernandez-Avalos v. Lynch, 784 F.3d 944 (4th Cir. 2015) to support his eligibility for asylum (Motion to Reopen at 15-19). Besides being nonbinding, the Fourth Circuit’s decision in Hernadez-Avalos is distinguishable. The alien in Hernandez-Avalos established her eligibility for asylum based on events that she had personally experienced in El Salvador. See id. The respondent in this case has no evidence of having received any direct threats.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BARTOLO LUCAS-CARMELO
File: AXXX-XX6-125 - New York, NY
January 17, 2019
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrea A. Saenz, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Caroline Clark
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals an Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 31, 2018, which denied his request for a continuance.1 The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed a brief in opposition to the appeal. The respondent indicated in his Notice of Appeal that he would be filing a brief on appeal, but has not done so.2 The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s request for a continuance so that he could apply for special immigrant juvenile (“SIJ”) status (IJ at 2-3). We agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent has not shown “good cause” for a continuance (IJ at 2-3). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29 (an Immigration Judge may grant a motion for continuance for good cause shown). In determining whether good cause exists to continue removal proceedings to await the resolution of a collateral matter, an Immigration Judge should consider: (1) the likelihood that the alien will receive the collateral relief, and (2) whether the relief will materially affect the outcome of the removal proceedings. See Matter of L-A-B-R-, 27 I&N Dec. 405, 413 (BIA 2018). The Immigration Judge should also consider ““whether the alien has exercised reasonable diligence in pursuing that relief, DHS’s position on the motion, the length of the requested continuance, and the procedural history of the case.” Matter of L-A-B-R-, 27 I&N Dec. at 413 (stating that the “good-cause standard in section 1003.29 requires consideration and balancing of all relevant factors in assessing a motion for continuance to accommodate a collateral matter”).
 
The respondent was granted one continuance before the Immigration Judge from June 20, 2018, to July 31, 2018 (IJ at 2; Tr. at 1-2, 5). The respondent did not provide evidence that he filed a Form I-360 petition for SIJ status with the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (IJ at 2; Tr. at 12). Although the respondent contends in his Notice of Appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in stating that he was not eligible for SIJ status because he was over 18, the respondent did not submit evidence that he was prima facie eligible for SIJ relief, either before the Immigration Judge, or on appeal (Notice of Appeal; IJ at 2). Thus, based on the aforementioned, we agree that the respondent did not make a persuasive showing of good cause for his requested continuance (IJ at 2-3).
 
*2 The respondent contends in his Notice of Appeal that his former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel, presumably by not filing for SIJ status (Notice of Appeal). However, the respondent has not shown that he complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Further, to show ineffective assistance of counsel prejudice must be shown. Omar v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 647 (2d Cir. 2008). The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent has not made this showing inasmuch as he has not shown that he is eligible for SIJ relief.
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD

1

The Immigration Judge also found that the respondent had conceded removability (IJ at 2). Inasmuch as the respondent has not appealed that determination, we deem it waived. See Matter of R-A-M-, 25 I&N Dec. 657, 658 n.2 (BIA 2012).

2

The respondent filed a motion on November 8, 2018, requesting more time to file his brief pending the return of the respondent to the United States after he had been wrongfully removed. The Board sent the respondent’s attorney a letter dated November 13, 2018, informing her that the briefing schedule would not be suspended. A Form I-830 (Notice to EOIR: Alien address) shows that the respondent is now being detained at the Bergen County Jail in New Jersey and thus has presumably been returned.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ILIR HOPE A.K.A. ILIR OSMANI A.K.A. ILIR WALES
File: AXXX-XX8-702 - Kansas City, MO
December 28, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Matthew L. Hoppock, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mohammad Abdelaziz
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on August 13, 2018, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to terminate and finding him removable as charged. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief in opposition to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent argues in the motion that his proceedings should be reopened because he is the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel by the attorney who represented him on appeal before the Board. Specifically, he alleges that prior counsel failed to raise the most important issues in his appeal brief, failed to cite to controlling Board and Circuit Court precedent, and failed to apprise the Board of the bases for the appeal (Motion at 1).
 
As an initial matter, we agree with the DHS that the respondent has not substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and ineffective assistance of counsel is not clear on the face of the record. See Habchy v. Gonzales, All F.3d 858 (8th Cir. 2006). Although respondent’s current counsel sent his former counsel an email advising him that a disciplinary complaint had been filed, prior counsel was not given an opportunity to respond to the allegations in advance of the filing of the complaint or the respondent’s motion with the Board. The aforementioned email correspondence is dated October 8, 2018, and acknowledges that a bar complaint had already been filed with the Pennsylvania Disciplinary Authority (Motion at Tab A). Moreover, the respondent’s motion to reopen was filed with the Board on October 9, 2018, one day after the email was sent to prior counsel. We do not find this was sufficient time for respondent’s counsel to respond to the allegations. Further, we note that the respondent has submitted a letter from former counsel dated August 28, 2018, regarding his representation of the respondent before the Board and discussions with the respondent regarding possible representation of the respondent in conjunction with a Petition for Review before the United States Court of Appeals for the Eighth Circuit (Motion at Tab B). However, this letter was drafted and sent out prior to current counsel being obtained and over a month before the allegations of ineffectiveness were alleged.
 
Even assuming that the respondent properly complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, the motion is denied because the respondent has not demonstrated prejudice. Valencia v. Holder, 657 F.3d 745, 749 (8th Cir. 2011), citing Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 638. The Eighth Circuit has held that, in order to prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, aliens must establish that they were prejudiced by their counsel’s performance, which in turn requires a showing that their attorney’s performance was so inadequate that it “may well have resulted in a deportation that would not otherwise have occurred.” Obleshchenko v. Ashcroft, 392 F.3d 970, 972 (8th Cir. 2004); Al-Khouri v. Ashcroft, 362 F.3d 461, 466 (8th Cir. 2004). The court further elaborated that this standard is akin to the requirement under the Sixth Amendment that there be “a reasonable probability that, but for counsel’s unprofessional errors, the result of the proceeding would have been different.” Obleshchenko v. Ashcroft, 392 F.3d at 972.
 
*2 Despite the contentions in the respondent’s motion, the appeal brief contained the arguments that the respondent is now alleging where not raised. The Board considered the arguments regarding whether the foreign conviction was obtained in absentia and thus was inconsistent with United States standards of due process, whether the foreign conviction statute was divisible, and whether the DHS had established the respondent was the same person as the defendant in the criminal proceedings (See Decision of the Board, dated August 13, 2018, at 2-3). We considered all of these arguments and found them unpersuasive when we dismissed his appeal. Specifically, as the DHS notes in its opposition to the current motion, the respondent was not convicted in absentia, was represented by counsel, and only absconded before the final appeal in his case was resolved. Thus, none of the case law cited or additional arguments raised in the respondent’s motion to reopen convince us of error in our prior decision. As such, the respondent has not established prejudice from prior counsel’s actions or inactions warranting reopening of his proceedings.
 
Accordingly, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.1
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

The parties should advise the Eighth Circuit of the Board’s decision.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDGAR ALBERTO LOPEZ-SANCHEZ

December 17, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Myong O. Chung, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s June 26, 2018, decision denying the respondent’s motion for a continuance and ordering him removed to Mexico. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and, as the respondent did not appear eligible for any form of relief, he ordered the respondent removed (IJ at 3-4). On appeal, the respondent claims that the Immigration Judge erred in not granting him a continuance (Notice of Appeal). An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance “for good cause shown,” within his sound discretion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Matter of L-A-B-R-, 27 I&N Dec. 405 (A.G. 2018); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785, 790-93 (BIA 2009); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 355-56 (BIA 1983); Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987). “[A]n Immigration Judge’s decision denying [a] motion for continuance will not be reversed unless the alien establishes that the denial caused him actual prejudice and harm and materially affected the outcome of his case.” Matter of Villarreal, 23 I&N Dec. 886, 891 (BIA 2006).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s request for a continuance (IJ at 2-4). The Immigration Judge did not err in not granting another continuance as the respondent did not demonstrate good cause (IJ at 3-4). In the instant case, the respondent requested a continuance so that he could pursue an S visa application with DHS. However, as noted by the Immigration Judge, the DHS has sole jurisdiction over such applications. Thus, we find that the respondent has not demonstrated any prejudice as a result of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his continuance request. See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. at 354; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. Accordingly, we find no error in the decision of the Immigration Judge to deny a continuance. Therefore, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
*2 During the pendency of the appeal, the respondent filed a motion to remand alleging ineffective assistance by his former attorney. We take note that the respondent has not complied with any of the evidentiary requirements for asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988).1 See also Rodriguez-Manzano v. Holder, 666 F.3d 948, 953 (5th Cir. 2012) (court requires strict Lozada compliance). Moreover, the respondent has not established that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s actions. See De Zavala v. Ashcroft, 385 F.3d 879, 883 (5th Cir. 2004) (noting that “an alien must make an initial showing of substantial prejudice” to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
To the extent that the respondent has submitted new evidence with his appeal, we generally cannot consider any new evidence proffered on appeal. See Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv). The additional documentation here is not sufficiently material as to warrant a remand. Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed and the motion is denied.
 
Edward F. Kelly
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 637, a motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel should be supported by an affidavit, reflect whether a complaint had been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and include a response to the allegations by former counsel or report counsel’s failure or refusal to respond. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639.

2018 WL 8062949 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FROILAN ALVAREZ GARCIA A.K.A. FROILAN GARCIA ALVAREZ
File: AXXX-XX5-292 - Lumpkin, GA
December 14, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lynn M. Burke, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jessica C. Wallace
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision of June 21, 2018, which denied his motion for a continuance in his removal proceedings and ordered him to be removed. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review all other issues under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. He did not apply for any relief from removal but instead requested a continuance in his removal proceedings to petition the state criminal court to modify his conviction based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel in his criminal proceedings, specifically that he did not have a Spanish translator during his guilty plea. The Immigration Judge found that such a petition may take an indeterminate amount of time and that it is speculative as to whether it will be successful.1 He therefore denied the respondent’s request for a continuance, and ordered him to be removed.
 
We are not persuaded by the respondent’s argument on appeal that, because his right to effective assistance of counsel in his criminal proceedings is a constitutionally protected right, the Immigration Judge should have granted his request for a continuance to allow for the adjudication of his pending motion for appropriate relief in state court. The respondent claims that he filed his motion in state court on July 23, 2018, but his evidence indicates that it was actually filed on July 27, 2018. See Brief at 4, Exh. J. As both dates are more than a month after the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request for a continuance, it was not pending in state court at the time that the Immigration Judge denied the continuance.
 
Upon our de novo review, we find that the Immigration Judge correctly denied the respondent’s continuance request. An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance in his discretion if good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Zafar v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 461 F.3d 1357, 1360 (11th Cir. 2006) (a request for a continuance to await the approval of the pending labor certification which was speculative was properly denied because it did not demonstrate good cause); Matter of L-A-B-R-, 27 I&N Dec. 405 (A.G. 2018) (an Immigration Judge may grant a motion for a continuance of removal proceedings only for good cause shown, and when a respondent requests a continuance to pursue collateral relief, the Immigration Judge must consider primarily the likelihood that the collateral relief will be granted and will materially affect the outcome of the removal proceedings); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009).
 
*2 Further, an Immigration Judge’s decision denying a continuance will not be reversed unless the alien establishes that the denial caused him actual prejudice and harm and materially affected the outcome of his removal proceedings. See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433, 434 (BIA 1987); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 355-56 (BIA 1983) (a decision to deny a continuance will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondents were deprived of a full and fair hearing). The respondent has not shown that the Immigration Judge failed to consider the relevant factors for a continuance in his removal proceedings, or that he abused his discretion in denying a continuance to pursue collateral relief in state court. See Matter of L-A-B-R-, 27 I&N Dec. at 405; Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807 (BIA 2012). He has not shown that the denial of the continuance caused him actual prejudice in his removal proceedings or materially affected the outcome of his removal proceedings. The respondent’s conviction has not been vacated by the criminal court and remains valid for immigration purposes. See Matter of L-A-B-R-, 27 I&N Dec. at 405.
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

1

The record includes the opposition of the State of North Carolina to the respondent’s motion for relief in the criminal court.

2018 WL 8062953 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KARLA MONTES A.K.A. PERLA R. OCHOA ANDRADE
File: AXXX-XX1-109 - Phoenix, AZ
December 13, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Laura Perez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the Immigration Judge’s November 27, 2017, decision denying her motion to reopen her removal proceedings.1 The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent filed a motion to reopen and rescind her in absentia removal order, arguing that her failure to appear at her missed hearing was the result of exceptional circumstances (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen). This motion to reopen is untimely, as it was filed more than 5 years after the respondent was ordered removed on July 19, 2012. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C) of the Act (stating that an in absentia removal order may be rescinded upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances). Additionally, because the respondent previously filed a motion to reopen in 2012, her present motion to reopen is number barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) (stating that an alien may file only one motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia removal order).
 
The respondent argues that the time and numerical limitations on her motion to reopen should be equitably tolled based on the ineffective assistance rendered by attorney Carlos, whom she hired to file her first motion to reopen (Respondent’s Br. at 16-18)2. Specifically, the respondent alleges that attorney Carlos did not comply with the Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, resulting in the denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen (Respondent’s Br. at 16-17).
 
The respondent has not, however, demonstrated that she acted with due diligence in discovering attorney Carlos’s alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672, 679 (9th Cir. 2011) (holding that equitable tolling of the deadlines and numerical limits for a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel requires that an alien act with reasonable diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error). The respondent first had reason to suspect attorney Carlos’s purported deficient representation when the Immigration Judge denied her motion to reopen, in part, because she did not comply with the Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 637, requirements (IJ, Aug. 16, 2012). See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d at 679 (noting that in determining whether an alien exercised due diligence, an adjudicator must first determine if and when a reasonable person in the alien’s position would suspect the specific fraud or error underlying the motion to reopen). The respondent has not shown that she took any affirmative steps-- including consulting with an attorney--to investigate the suspected deficient representation during the approximately 5 years between when her motion to reopen was denied in 2012 and when she hired her present counsel in 2017 (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 21, Tab C). See id. at 680-81. We therefore conclude that the respondent has not exercised due diligence, and is not entitled to equitable tolling of the time and numerical limitations on her motion to reopen.
 
*2 Even if the respondent’s motion to reopen were not time and number barred, she has not demonstrated that her failure to appear at the hearing was the result of exceptional circumstances beyond her control. See section 240(e)(1) of the Act. The respondent claims that she did not attend her July 19, 2012, hearing because her prior attorney did not timely inform her that she needed to attend the hearing (Respondent’s Br. at 13-16). She further argues that this constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and excuses her failure to appear at her missed hearing. See Monjaraz-Munoz v. INS, 327 F.3d 892, 896 (9th Cir. 2003) (noting that if an alien fails to appear because of his actual and reasonable reliance on erroneous advice of counsel, it can constitute an exceptional circumstance beyond the alien’s control), amended by 339 F.3d 1012 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
The respondent was given oral notice of her July 19, 2012, hearing by the Immigration Judge on March 31, 2011 (IJ, Nov. 27, 2017; IJ, Aug. 16, 2012). The record further indicates that the respondent was personally served with the notice of hearing for her July 19, 2012, hearing, and that the notice of hearing warns her of the consequences for failing to appear (Notice of Hearing, Mar. 31, 2011). Even if the respondent’s prior attorney did not attempt to timely remind her of the hearing date--a claim the prior attorney disputes--the respondent does not allege in her affidavit or on appeal that she attempted to ask her attorney or the Immigration Court the date of her next hearing (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Tabs C, Q). While we recognize that the respondent previously attended two hearings before the Immigration Judge and filed her first motion to reopen less than 2 weeks after she was ordered removed, we nonetheless conclude that the totality of the circumstances do not establish that her failure to appear at her missed hearing was the result of exceptional circumstance warranting reopening of her removal proceedings. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act; Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503, 509 (BIA 1996).
 
We further conclude that the respondent’s case does not warrant sua sponte reopening (Respondent’s Br. at 20-22). Sua sponte authority is invoked sparingly and reserved for truly exceptional circumstances. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-35 (BIA 1999); see also Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (explaining that sua sponte reopening is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations where enforcing them might result in hardship). While we are sympathetic to the harm the respondent purportedly suffered as a child in Mexico and her fears of return, we do not find that the particular facts in this case demonstrate truly exceptional circumstances warranting sua sponte reopening. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. at 1133-35. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Daniel Morris

All citations to the respondent’s brief refer to the December 12, 2017, brief, unless otherwise noted.

IN RE: JUAN CARLOS DIAZ-JAIMES

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gabriela Parra, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, on April 16, 2018, filed an untimely motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of prior counsel.1 The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. We find that the respondent has properly asserted a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel (Motion at Tab D). See generally Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-640 (BIA 1988). The respondent asserts that but for ineffective assistance of counsel, which he asserts caused him to be removed in absentia, he would have been given the opportunity to voluntarily depart, and would have chosen to do so. Considering the totality of circumstances presented in the motion, and the lack of any opposition to the motion from the Department of Homeland Security, the motion will be granted and the record will be remanded to allow the respondent the opportunity to seek any relief for which he may be eligible. Given the disposition of this matter, we need not further address the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Accordingly, the motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to terminate proceedings is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings not inconsistent with this order and entry of a new decision.
 
Edward R. Grant

By a supplemental motion filed on July 9, 2018, the respondent seeks the termination of proceedings in light of Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S.Ct. 2105 (2018). However, a subsequent notice informed the respondent of the date and time of his hearing, September 2, 2015, which is not a matter of dispute. See Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. 441, 441 (BIA 2018) (Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S. Ct. 2105 (2018), distinguished). Therefore, the motion to terminate these proceedings will be denied.

2018 WL 7572449 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NARI CUELLO-VELOZ A.K.A. VICENTE CORCINO
File: AXXX-XX4-632 - Guynabo, PR
November 30, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Susana Abarca, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jorge Ramos
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board entered the final administrative decision on August 7, 2015, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s May 30, 2014, written decision denying a previous motion to reopen proceedings in which the respondent was ordered removed to the Dominican Republic in December 1999 because he did not comply with a voluntary departure order. The respondent has filed another motion to reopen, averring that his prior counsel deficiently represented him by not seeking to apply for a provisional unlawful presence waiver of inadmissibility (Form I-601A) on his behalf in the May 2014 motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland ‘ecurity opposes the motion, which will be denied, as the respondent has not demonstrated that an exception to the time and number limits applies. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The respondent has not meaningfully raised his ineffective assistance of counsel claim, where he has not shown compliance with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), including, inter alia, notification to prior counsel of the alleged deficient representation. Current counsel’s assertion that the respondent contacted prior counsel several unspecified times and informed him of the alleged deficient representation is insufficient. Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980) (statements of counsel are not considered evidence). Current counsel has provided no explanation why she has not notified prior counsel of the alleged deficient performance. Asaba v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 9, 12 (1st Cir. 2004) (emphasizing importance of Lozada notification requirement, to afford prior counsel an adequate opportunity to respond to the alleged deficient performance). Nor is the purported State of New York Grievance Committee complaint, dated April 10, 2018, adequate notification to the respondent’s former counsel. Taveras-Duran v. Holder, 767 F.3d 120, 123-24 (1st Cir. 2014) (recognizing Board’s discretionary authority to deny motions to reopen for failure to comply with Lozada requirements).
 
The respondent also has not demonstrated the requisite diligence to pursue an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Neves v. Holder, 613 F.3d 30, 36 (1st Cir. 2010) (the equitable tolling doctrine extends statutory deadlines only in extraordinary circumstances, where parties were prevented from complying through no fault or lack of diligence of their own); Chedid v. Holder, 573 F.3d 33, 37 (1st Cir. 2009) (observing that equitable tolling should be sparingly invoked and is foreclosed where aliens do not exercise due diligence).1 The respondent should have inquired about his potential eligibility for adjustment of status when the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services approved a visa petition on his behalf in 2002 (Respondent’s Mot., tab C). If he had done so then he would have known that a provisional unlawful presence waiver of inadmissibility was necessary to apply for adjustment of status when prior counsel filed his motion to reopen in May 2014. Because the respondent waited an additional 4 years since the motion to allege ineffective assistance of counsel, we are not persuaded that he has diligently pursued the claim. Chedid v. Holder, 573 F.3d at 37 (affirming Board’s finding that alien did not act with due diligence, where he waited approximately 1 year to pursue ineffective assistance of counsel claim).
 
*2 Further, we are not persuaded that prior counsel’s allegedly deficient representation prejudiced the respondent. Muyubisnay-Cungachi v. Holder, 734 F.3d 66, 72 (1st Cir. 2010) (observing that an alien must demonstrate a reasonable probability that counsel’s deficient performance prejudiced him or deprived him of a fundamentally fair hearing). Considering then-applicable regulations, the respondent has not shown that he was eligible for an unlawful presence waiver when prior counsel filed the motion to reopen in May 2014, given the respondent’s final removal order. 8 C.F.R. § 212.7(e)(4)(vi) (2013).
 
Finally, the respondent has not established an exceptional situation warranting sua sponte reopening. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). Although we sympathize with his wife’s health issues, we do not have the authority to grant relief from removal solely on equitable grounds. Matter of Silva-Rodriguez, 20 I&N Dec. 448 (BIA 1992).
 
Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

The United States Court of Appeals for the First Circuit has not decided whether equitable tolling applies to the time and number limitations on motions to reopen. Chedid v. Holder, 573 F.3d at 37.

2018 WL 7435824 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN MANUEL ESPITIA-CORTES
File: AXXX-XX3-309 - Chicago, IL
November 8, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven Laxton, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Casey J. Johnson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; termination
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on October 4, 2000, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. On August 16, 2018, almost 18 years after the Board’s order, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, remand, and terminate. The Department of Homeland Security has opposed the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
We will first address the respondent’s argument that his proceedings should be reopened, his removal order rescinded, and his proceedings terminated, or in the alternative remanded to the Immigration Judge, in light of the Supreme Court’s decision in Pereira v. Sessions 138 S. Ct. 2105 (2018). The respondent argues that, because the Notice to Appear in his case did not contain a specific date and time of hearing, under Pereira, this document does not constitute a Notice to Appear, jurisdiction over his case did not vest with the Immigration Court, and therefore the removal proceedings against him should be terminated (Moton at 1-2).
 
However, in the respondent’s case, the Notice to Appear did specify the date and time of his hearing, as “February 4, 1998[,] at 9:00 a.m.” as well as the place of his hearing, i.e., the Chicago Immigration Court (Exh. 1). Inasmuch as the Notice to Appear satisfied the date and time requirement addressed in Pereira v. Sessions, the respondent’s arguments seeking termination or remand of the proceedings based on that decision are inapposite.
 
We will next address the respondent’s arguments regarding ineffective assistance of counsel. Specifically, the respondent argues that attorney Gary Spraker, who represented him at hearing and on appeal, provided ineffective assistance because in 1996 he filed an unmeritorious application for adjustment of status for the respondent, for the purpose of obtaining an employment authorization, which was denied by the former Immigration and Naturalization Service (INS), and which resulted in the initiation of the removal proceedings against him. The respondent argues that attorney Spraker was disciplined by bar authorities in 2001 for such actions (Motion at 2-4).
 
However, the respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was not filed within 90 days of the Board’s final administrative decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent acknowledges that attorney Spraker was disciplined in 2001, shortly after the completion of the respondent’s proceedings.1 The respondent has not explained how he exercised due diligence in filing a motion to reopen based on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim nearly 18 years after the alleged ineffective assistance. See El-Gazawy v. Holder, 690 F.3d 852, 859-860 (7th Cir. 2012).
 
*2 In any event, attorney Spraker’s action now complained of by the respondent was conduct prior to the issuance of the Notice to Appear and therefore outside the removal proceeding, rather than during the removal proceeding itself. Therefore, this conduct did not affect the fairness or outcome of the proceeding itself. To establish a due process violation based on ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent must show, inter alia, that the alleged ineffective assistance prevented him from receiving a fair hearing and that the lack of a fair hearing actually had the potential for affecting the outcome of the proceedings. El-Gazawy v. Holder, 690 F.3d at 860; see also Rapheal v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 521, 533 (7th Cir. 2008); Hamid v. Gonzales, 417 F.3d 642, 646-47 (7th Cir. 2005) (applicant’s failure to allege excluded testimony that would potentially affect outcome of hearing was fatal to due process claim).
 
To the extent the purported deficiencies in the attorney’s representation in the case did not pertain to the actual substance of the hearing (e.g., evidence presented or omitted, arguments raised or overlooked), or call the hearing’s fairness into question, the respondent has not shown that reopening is warranted based on the ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See also Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004), amended sub nom. Lara-Torres v. Gonzales, 404 F.3d 1105 (9th Cir. 2005). Furthermore, despite the acknowledgement in the motion regarding Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (Motion at 4), the respondent has not shown that he has complied with the requirements set forth in that decision. Based on the above, the motion based on ineffective assistance of counsel will be denied.
 
The respondent also urges us to sua sponte reopen his proceedings under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), based on a number of factors in his case, including the approved Form I-130 visa petition filed by his United States citizen daughter, his long period or residence in the United States and minimal criminal record, and other factors. We do not find that the respondent’s case presents an exceptional situation that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the power to reopen sua sponte “is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). Based on the above, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent’s motion states that he may have been aided by a class action settlement (Motion at 4, fn. 3; Motion Exh. L). However, this settlement appears to have been entered in or about 2005, long before the current motion was filed.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YOSY ADALI LOPEZ-BENITEZ
File: AXXX-XX3-402 - New York, NY
November 2, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Perham Makabi, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Honduras, appeals a March 1, 2018, decision by an Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security has not filed a response. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
In an October 17, 2017, decision, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent voluntary departure until on or before February 14, 2018, and issued an alternate order of removal. The respondent filed his motion to reopen on February 13, 2018. The respondent contends that proceedings should be reopened because his prior attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel by both advising him to request voluntary departure and failing to apply for Special Immigrant Juvenile (“SIJ”) status. He asserts that he is eligible for SIJ status.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel necessary to reopen proceedings by accepting voluntary departure and not pursuing the respondent’s asylum claim (IJ at 2-3). An alien is bound by the reasonable tactical decisions of his counsel. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986); Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 310-11 (BIA 1988). The respondent’s attorney’s determination to accept voluntary departure qualifies as a reasonable tactical decision inasmuch as the respondent’s asylum application only refers to gang violence, and the respondent has not shown that he would have been eligible for asylum based on this fear (IJ at 2).
 
As to the respondent’s claim that his attorney failed to pursue SIJ status, the respondent has not shown that he revealed enough information to his attorney to establish that he was potentially eligible for this status (IJ at 3). The respondent’s current counsel indicates at one point that the respondent never told his former counsel about the abuse (Notice of Appeal). However, he also indicates that the respondent told his former counsel that he left Honduras because of the abuse (Motion to Reopen at 2). The respondent’s declaration itself is not clear regarding exactly what he told his prior counsel (Declaration with Motion to Reopen).
 
The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in stating that he had not filed an action in family court that would result in the issuance of the order necessary for an I-360 petition (Petition for Amerasian, Widow(er) or Special Immigrant) (Respondent’s Br. at 7; IJ at 3). While the respondent correctly notes that he submitted evidence with his motion showing submission of an action in family court, it is speculative that this request will be approved (Respondent’s Br. at 7; Motion to Reopen Exh. C (Petition for Appointment as Guardian of a Person); IJ at 3). Moreover, the respondent’s petition does not ask the family court whether the respondent not returning to Honduras would be in his best interests, and it is otherwise not clear the petition complies with all the regulatory standards. 8 C.F.R. § 204.11(c)(3)-(6). Therefore, it is not apparent that an action by the family court will necessarily support an I-360 petition.
 
*2 Further, in the respondent’s brief submitted on June 7, 2018, he indicated that the family court proceeding would be finalized in a few weeks, but he has not provided an update as to the outcome of that proceeding (Respondent’s Br. at 4). Thus, based on the aforementioned, the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by any action of his prior attorney by not filing for SIJ status. See Omar v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 647 (2d Cir. 2008) (to establish ineffective assistance of counsel, prejudice must be shown); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (the term “prejudice” refers to actual prejudice).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 7435840 (BIA)
End of Document

2018 WL 7435855 (BIA)

IN RE: ADORA NUDO GAMOLO
File: AXXX-XX8-813 - Los Angeles, CA
October 29, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Daniel P. Hanlon, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jessica Soley
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATIONS: Reopening; remand; termination of proceedings
 
*1 This case was last before us on November 15, 2011, when we denied the respondent’s second motion to reopen her removal proceedings. On April 19, 2018, the respondent submitted the instant motion to reopen and remand pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. She requests termination of her removal proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion. The motion exceeds both the time and number limitations for motions to reopen, and it will be denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); section 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229a(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i)(an alien may file only one motion to reopen and, with certain exceptions, it shall be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order). The respondent’s termination request will also be denied.
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of the Philippines. On October 26, 2005, the Immigration Judge pretermitted her application for adjustment of status based on an employment-based visa petition and ordered her to be removed from the United States. The respondent previously sought to have her proceedings reopened based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and to apply for adjustment of status. She now seeks reopening, a remand of the record, and termination of her proceedings to apply for adjustment of status based on an approved visa petition on her behalf filed by her adult United States citizen son. She contends that reopening sua sponte is warranted.
 
The number-barred motion to reopen has also been filed out of time, and has not been shown to come within any exception to the filing requirements for motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). A new claim of eligibility for adjustment of status is generally not a circumstance warranting the grant of an untimely or number-barred motion to reopen. We have considered the argument and evidence presented with the motion. However, the respondent has not shown the exceptional circumstances necessary to warrant our granting this untimely motion in the exercise of our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). We find no basis to remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings, or to terminate the respondent’s removal proceedings. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen and remand, and request for termination of her proceedings, are denied.
 
*2 Edward R. Grant

2018 WL 7435862 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDINA FAGUNDES-GOMES
File: AXXX-XX3-703 - Harlingen, TX
October 26, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Luciano B. Park, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carolyn Abdenour
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent was ordered removed in absentia in 2005, and the Board affirmed the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen on March 16, 2016. On April 20, 2018, the respondent filed the present motion asking the Board to sua sponte reopen these removal proceedings to allow her to apply for a provisional unlawful presence waiver (Form I-601A) in conjunction with an application for adjustment of status. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is both time and number-barred and will be denied on that basis. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Moreover, the power to reopen sua sponte allows reopening in exceptional situations not present here. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). The respondent urges that the hardship to her United States citizen spouse upon her removal warrants sua sponte reopening. We acknowledge the challenges that the respondent’s spouse may face, but there appears to be no basis for reopening outside of the time and numerical constraints imposed by law given the present record and the evidence submitted in support of the motion.
 
We only separately note that it is undisputed that the respondent was personally served with the Notice to Appear, which contains the date, time, and place of her scheduled hearing. The respondent’s claim that she did not receive proper notice of her hearing was fully considered and addressed by both the Immigration Judge and the Board.1
 
Finally, pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 212.7(e)(4)(iv), the respondent does not need to have her removal proceedings reopened to be able to pursue a Form I-601A waiver with the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS). In addition, she may request an administrative stay of removal from the DHS while any waiver, or provisional waiver, applications are pending before the USCIS. See 8 C.F.R. § 241.6(a). Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

To the extent the respondent contends that she received ineffective assistance from former counsel in connection with these removal proceedings below, the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements for advancing such a claim, including the requirement that she show prejudice resulting from former counsel’s alleged deficient performance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 n.2 (5th Cir. 2001) (noting that to establish ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must demonstrate that counsel’s performance resulted in substantial prejudice); Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F. 3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994) (proving prejudice requires the petitioner to make a prima facie showing that, absent counsel’s deficient performance, he would have been entitled to the relief the sought).
