** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FLOR CRUZ-RUEDA DE LEON
File: AXXX-XX8-421 - Denver, CO
October 22, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Orlando Mondragon, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Geoffrey D. Rieman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the Immigration Judge’s November 16, 2017, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen her removal proceedings. While this appeal was pending, the respondent also filed another motion to reopen with this Board. The appeal will be dismissed and the respondent’s second motion to reopen is denied.
 
We review the Immigration Judge’s factual findings for clear error, including findings as to the credibility of testimony. We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of immigration judges de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
On March 2, 2011, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s request for voluntary departure prior to the conclusion of her removal proceedings (IJ at 1, 5-6). See section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b). The respondent did not depart the United States on or before the expiration of her voluntary departure period (IJ at 6). Six years later, on October 5, 2017, the respondent filed a motion to reopen sua sponte. The respondent sought reopening based on her argument that the Immigration Judge failed to ask her if she had a fear of persecution in Mexico. The respondent claimed that she feared persecution in Mexico based on the particular social group of her family. The respondent also sought reopening so that she could submit applications for a U visa and for relief under the Violence Against Women Act (VAWA). The respondent claimed that her lawful permanent resident husband was emotionally, psychologically, and physically abusive to her and to her children (IJ at 2, 3). See Motion to Reopen Sua Sponte; Addendum to Motion to Reopen at 2.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen on November 16, 2017. The respondent now appeals that decision. Despite indicating on her Notice of Appeal that she intended to file an appeal brief, the respondent did not do so. Instead, she filed another Motion to Reopen and to Terminate Removal proceedings based on the Supreme Court’s decision in Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S. Ct. 2105 (2018).1
 
With regard to the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of her first motion to reopen, in her Notice of Appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred by finding that the Digital Audio Recording of the respondent’s March 2, 2011, hearing is intelligible and provides a full and accurate record of the hearing (IJ at 4). The respondent states that she has not been able to “find a playable track” of the hearing. Notice of Appeal at 2. In the Immigration Judge’s November 16, 2017, decision, however, the Immigration Judge set forth a detailed summary of the March 2, 2011, hearing (IJ at 4-6). The respondent was present at the hearing and has not indicated that the Immigration Judge’s summary is inaccurate. In any event, the respondent has not shown that she was prejudiced by this alleged error. See Molina v. Holder, 763 F.3d 1259, 1263 (10th Cir. 2014) (respondent cannot succeed on a due process claim without showing that the alleged error materially affected the outcome of her case).
 
*2 The respondent also argues in her Notice of Appeal that her proceedings should be reopened because she has requested that the Vermont Service Center issue an expedited decision on her U visa application. Notice of Appeal at 2. This Notice of Appeal was filed on November 24, 2017, however, which was almost 1 year ago. The respondent has not provided any indication since then that the Vermont Service Center has issued a decision. Nonetheless, the Board generally lacks authority to reopen proceedings of aliens who are under a final order of removal and who seek relief, such as a U visa, over which the Board has no jurisdiction. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 107 (BIA 2009).
 
Further, the respondent appears to argue that her proceedings should be reopened based on ineffective assistance of counsel. Notice of Appeal at 2. To the extent that the respondent raised this argument before the Immigration Judge in her motion to reopen, she has not attempted to comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009); Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260, 261 n.1 (BIA 2007) (matters not raised before the Immigration Judge are waived on appeal).
 
Moreover, the respondent also argues that the Immigration Judge erred by denying reopening so that she can file a self-petition under the VAWA. She claims that she was living with her lawful permanent resident boyfriend and ““being abused by him at the time of her hearing” before the Immigration Judge. Notice of Appear at 2; Addendum to Motion to Reopen at 2-3. Because the respondent alleges that her boyfriend abused her at the time of her hearing, she has not shown that these allegations were unavailable and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The respondent also has not presented sufficient evidence to demonstrate prima facie eligibility for VAWA relief and that she merits relief as a matter of discretion (IJ at 8). See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314, 323 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 106 (1988); Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413, 419 (BIA 1996). For these reasons, we uphold the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s first motion to reopen.
 
In addition, as explained above, during the pendency of this appeal, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen based on the Supreme Court’s decision in Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S. Ct. 2105 (2018). In that case, the Supreme Court addressed the narrow issue of whether a Notice to Appear that does not designate a specific time or place of a removal proceeding triggers the stop-time rule which ends the accrual of the alien’s period of continuous physical presence in the United States for purposes of cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act. See 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1).
 
*3 Here, the respondent relies on Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S. Ct. at 2119, and argues that, because her September 22, 2009, Notice to Appear did not contain the time or place of a removal hearing, her proceedings should be reopened and she should be permitted to file an application for cancellation of removal (Exh. 1). See Second Motion to Reopen at 3-4.
 
Generally, an alien may file one motion to reopen and it must be filed no later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceedings sought to be reopened. See sections 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Act. In this case, the respondent has filed two motions to reopen, and the second motion was filed on September 20, 2018, which was not within 90 days of the Immigration Judge’s November 16, 2017, decision. In order to avoid the time and numeric limitations, however, the respondent argues that these limitations should be equitably tolled, or that the Board should reopen the proceedings sua sponte. See Second Motion to Reopen at 5-8.
 
Contrary to the respondent’s argument, this Board recently decided Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. 441, 447 (BIA 2018). In that case, the Board held that a Notice to Appear that does not specify the time and place of a removal proceedings vests jurisdiction with the Immigration Judge over the removal proceedings and satisfies section 239(a) of the Act as long as a Notice of Hearing setting forth this information is subsequently sent to the alien. See Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. at 447; see also section 239(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a). In this case, it is undisputed that the respondent appeared for her master calendar hearing on November 24, 2010, and for subsequent hearings. Therefore, the respondent received notice of the time and place of her removal hearings. For these reasons, consistent with Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. at 447, the respondent has not established that reopening is warranted on account of a lack of notice. Moreover, the respondent has not demonstrated that an exceptional situation exists which calls for sua sponte reopening. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). For these reasons, we deny the respondent’s second motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s Motion to Reopen and Terminate Removal Proceedings is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent states in her most recent motion to reopen that she was removed from the United States, re-entered illegally on June 5, 2018, and was convicted for illegal re-entry. She also states that she is being held in detention awaiting a credible fear interview. Motion to Reopen at 2.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HERMINIA ROSADO

October 17, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Cheryl Lin, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The Board entered the final administrative order of removal in the respondent’s case on February 5, 2008. The respondent now moves the Board to reconsider our March 5, 2018, decision denying her untimely, number-barred motion to reopen. See section 240(c)(6) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). She specifically challenges the Board’s decision not to reopen the proceedings sua sponte to enable her to apply for adjustment of status. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The motion will be denied.
 
This Board has limited authority to reopen and reconsider cases sua sponte in truly exceptional situations. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999). However, “[t]he power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship.” Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). The respondent asserts that she did not seek sua sponte reopening to cure filing defects. Rather, she requested reopening sua sponte “because it took may years for [her] to become eligible to adjust status [] because [h]er son [] became a permanent resident in 2007 and a naturalized citizen in 2011” (Respondent’s Mot. at 8). We emphasize that becoming potentially eligible for relief many years after the entry of a final order of removal is not an “exceptional situation” warranting sua sponte reopening.
 
The respondent also asserts that the Board should reconsider our decision because we noted in a footnote that she could apply for a provisional unlawful presence waiver before the USCIS. She essentially asserts that she is not prima facie eligible for such relief because she is separated from her husband who is her sole qualifying relative for the waiver. We find no error in our footnote to warrant to reconsideration. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006).
 
In sum, the respondent has not identified an error of fact or law in our prior order to warrant reconsideration of our denial of her untimely, number-barred motion to reopen.1 See section 240(c)(6)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1). We recognize that the respondent has acquired equities in the United States, and that her removal will likely result in hardship to the respondent and her family. Nevertheless, the respondent’s prior motion did not demonstrate an exceptional situation to persuade the Board to exercise its sua sponte discretion in this matter.2 Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

To the extent that the respondent offers evidence with the instant motion, such evidence has not been shown to comply with the regulations at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1).

2

The respondent now asserts that she is not removable as charged in the Notice to Appear. The respondent, however, did not raise this issue on appeal or in a prior motion to reopen. Motions to reopen and reconsider are not opportunities to present previously available evidence or to raise arguments that could have been raised on appeal. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 n.2 (BIA 1991) (discussing the characteristics of motions to reopen and motions to reconsider). To the extent that the respondent implies that her prior attorney incorrectly conceded the charge set forth in the NTA, she has not established that the filing restrictions imposed on motions to reopen before the Board should be equitably tolled. The respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, nor demonstrated that she exercised due diligence in bringing this matter before the Board. See Fadiga v. Attorney Gen. U.S., 488 F.3d 142, 157 (3d Cir. 2007); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

IN RE: MACJHAY YAGAO
File: AXXX-XX6-445 - San Diego, CA
October 16, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrew M. Knapp, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This case was previously before the Board on July 29, 2016, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal, denied his motion to administratively close proceedings, and denied his motion to terminate proceedings. On July 20, 2018, the respondent filed an untimely motion to reconsider this decision, and a motion to terminate proceedings. 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(b).1 The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be remanded to the Immigration Court.
 
The respondent first contends that his proceedings should be terminated in light of the Supreme Court’s recent decision in Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S. Ct. 2105 (2018). In particular, the respondent claims that his Notice to Appear (Form I-862) was defective because it failed to designate the date and time of his hearing as required under section 239(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a). Accordingly, the respondent argues, the filing of the Notice to Appear with the Immigration Court in his case was insufficient to vest jurisdiction with the Immigration Court.
 
The decision in Pereira v. Sessions involved the “stop-time” rule in cancellation of removal cases. The respondent asserts that the decision should not be limited to such cases, but should be applied to all Notices to Appear that do not include the time and place of the hearing. However, as this Board recently explained in Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. 441 (BIA 2018), a Notice to Appear that does not specify the time or place of an alien’s initial removal hearing vests an Immigration Judge with jurisdiction over the removal proceedings, and meets the requirements of section 239(a) of the Act, so long as a notice of hearing specifying this information is later sent to the alien. The record indicates that the respondent subsequently received proper notices of his removal hearings, which specified the dates, times, and locations of his scheduled hearings, and that he attended these hearings (IJ at 2). Consequently, the requirements of section 239(a) of the Act were satisfied in this case, and termination is unwarranted.
 
However, in supplemental pleadings, the respondent has submitted evidence that the Court of Appeals, Fourth Appellate District, Division One, State of California, has sustained the respondent’s appeal from his denial of post-conviction relief. Therein, the California appellate court found that the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel because he was not properly informed of the immigration consequences of pleading guilty to a violation of California Health and Safety Code § 11360(a). The appellate court remanded the respondent’s criminal proceedings so that he would be allowed to withdraw his guilty plea to this offense.
 
*2 While the respondent has not proffered post-conviction relief which, as yet, directly affects his removability, we find the appellate court’s decision to be an exceptional situation which warrants a remand of this record for further proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (Board will exercise its sua sponte authority over an untimely motion only in an “exceptional situation”); Matter of Jean, 23 I&N Dec. 373 (A.G. 2002). The respondent’s sole charge of removability rests upon his conviction under Cal. Health and Safety Code § 11360(a), and it appears that, upon remand, the California trial court will vacate the conviction based on due process concerns. See Matter of Adamiak, 23 I&N Dec. 878 (BIA 2006); Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003). As such, we will remand the record to allow both parties to submit further evidence related to the respondent’s removability, and the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The request for administrative closure is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The record of proceeding also contains a pleading by the respondent, certified to the Board by the Immigration Judge on March 22, 2017, which requests administrative closure of these proceedings. The respondent’s request will be denied based on the recent guidelines set forth in Matter of Castro-Tum, 27 I&N Dec. 271 (A.G. 2018).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DIEGO LUCAS-MOLINA
File: AXXX-XX7-340 - Phoenix, AZ
October 2, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eric G. Bjotvedt, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Edith M. Lucero
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Administrative closure; remand; termination
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 17, 2017. The respondent has submitted a motion to remand and a motion to terminate. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has submitted a motion for summary affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s decision and has opposed the respondent’s motion to terminate. The appeal will be dismissed and the motions will be denied.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
In the Notice of Appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his request for administrative closure, and suggests that he should have been granted administrative closure because he was not an enforcement priority and there was a new administration at the time of his final hearing (Notice of Appeal at 2). However, the respondent has not explained or shown, before the Immigration Judge or on appeal, that these are sufficient reasons for administrative closure under Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 2012), overruled by Matter of Castro-Tum, 27 I&N Dec. 271 (A.G. 2018). In any event, since the Immigration Judge’s decision, the Attorney General ruled in Matter of Castro-Tum, 27 I&N Dec. 271, that Immigration Judges and the Board do not have the general authority to suspend indefinitely immigration proceedings by administrative closure, subject to certain exceptions that are not applicable here. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent has submitted a motion to remand, showing that he has married a spouse who has filed for a U nonimmigrant status and therefore he may qualify as a derivative beneficiary of a U visa if this application is approved (Motion to Remand Exhs. A-D). However, the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) has sole jurisdiction over all petitions for U nonimmigrant status, rather than the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(1). Furthermore, the respondent is not precluded from seeking a U visa from the USCIS by the fact that he is the subject of a final order of removal.1 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(1)(ii). Accordingly, the motion to remand based on the U visa application will be denied.
 
*2 In the motion to remand, the respondent has submitted an asylum application and argues that the right to apply for asylum was not explained to him at hearing (Motion to Remand Exh. E). The respondent states that he fears returning to Guatemala because he is a native Mayan and has experienced social discrimination and limited educational, economic, and other opportunities (Motion to Remand Exh. E, Form I-589 at 5). However, the respondent was represented at hearing by an attorney, who indicated that the respondent was advised of his immigration rights (Tr. at 14). The respondent has submitted a declaration stating that he was never advised of the possibility of asylum (Motion to Remand Exh. F). However, the respondent has not made a valid ineffective assistance of counsel claim by complying with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and he has not explained why he could not do so. Accordingly, the motion to remand for asylum application will be denied.2
 
In the motion to terminate, the respondent argues that his proceedings should be reopened, his removal order rescinded, and his proceedings terminated, in light of the Supreme Court’s decision in Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S. Ct. 2105 (2018). The respondent argues that, because the Notice to Appear in his case did not contain a specific date and time of hearing as the Notice to Appear in Pereira, this document does not constitute a Notice to Appear, jurisdiction over his case did not vest with the Immigration Court, and therefore the removal proceedings against him should be terminated.
 
However, the Supreme Court’s decision did not hold that a Notice to Appear that did not contain a specific date and time of hearing was invalid for all purposes or did not validly initiate removal proceedings. Rather, the Supreme Court focused on the specific question whether such a Notice to Appear triggers the stop-time rule for purposes of cancellation of removal. Section 240A(d)(1)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(d)(1)(B). Pereira v. Sessions, 138 S. Ct. at 2113 (stating that “the dispositive question in this case is much narrower ...: Does a “notice to appear” that does not specify the “time and place at which the proceedings will be held” . . . trigger the stop-time rule?”). Therefore, the Supreme Court’s decision does not warrant termination of the respondent’s proceedings.
 
In Matter of Bermudez-Cota, 27 I&N Dec. 441 (BIA 2018), the Board held that a notice to appear that does not specify the time and place of an alien’s initial removal hearing vests an Immigration Judge with jurisdiction over the removal proceedings and meets the requirements of section 239(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a), so long as a notice of hearing specifying this information is later sent to the alien. For the reasons set forth in Matter of Bermudez-Cota, we will deny the respondent’s motion to terminate.
 
*3 The Immigration Judge granted a 60-day period of voluntary departure to the respondent. The respondent submitted proof on appeal that the voluntary departure bond has been posted, therefore the period of voluntary departure will be reinstated. Based on the above, the following orders shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motions are denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the DHS. See section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
*4 Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

While the pendency of an application for a U visa has no effect on the government’s authority to execute a removal order, an applicant for a U visa is permitted to file a request for a stay of removal with the DHS. 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(1)(ii). If the USCIS approves the respondent’s application, the regulation allows him to file a motion to reopen and terminate proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(5)(i).

2

The respondent also has not argued or shown that the past or future social discrimination and lack of opportunities he describes may rise to the level of persecution or torture.

2018 WL 5921038 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAVIER ELIUD MORALES-MARTINEZ
File: AXXX-XX3-328 - Chaparral, NM
September 28, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Orlando Mondragon, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 27, 2018. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged.1 On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
At the hearing on April 26, 2018, the respondent requested a continuance of proceedings to enable him to pursue post-conviction relief with regard to the conviction that serves as a basis for removal and renders him ineligible for relief from removal. The respondent alleged that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of this conviction, and he has filed a motion to vacate it (Tr. at 59-60).2 The respondent was granted continuances for that and other purposes from June 27, 2017, to April 27, 2018 (Tr. at 12, 17-19, 24, 29, 41-42, 50-51, 60). The respondent’s post-conviction motion to challenge his criminal conviction on constitutional grounds was still pending on that date, and the IJ denied further continuance (IJ at 2; Tr. at 62). On appeal, the respondent challenges denial of the continuance. The respondent does not indicate that the conviction has been vacated.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of L-A-B-R-, 27 I&N Dec. 405 (A.G. 2018); Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009). We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We conclude that good cause was not shown.
 
While the respondent is free to pursue the post-conviction motion, such a motion does not justify a continuance of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of L-A-B-R- at 417 (observing that collateral attacks on criminal convictions are often too speculative to warrant a continuance). The respondent’s post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter Acosta, 27 I&N Dec. 420 (BIA 2018).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Joan B Geller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Removability and relief from removal are not at issue on appeal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010), the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. 342 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky does not apply retroactively).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CLAUDIA SANCHEZ A.K.A. EBLIN IBANIA MURILLO CASTRO
File: AXXX-XX0-471 - Harlingen, TX
September 21, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mo Idlibby, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Guillermo Rey De La Garza
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 5, 2018, denying her motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for her failure to appear for a hearing on June 27, 2005. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We find no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s findings of fact. The Immigration Judge found that the Notice to Appear (NTA) was personally served on the respondent on November 13, 2004, and contained the date, time, and location of the respondent’s June 27, 2005, hearing (IJ at 1; Exh. 1). Further, the Immigration Judge observed that the certificate of service on the NTA indicates that the respondent was provided oral notice in her native language of Spanish of the consequences of a failure to appear, and the respondent’s right index fingerprint is affixed thereto (Id.). Moreover, the NTA bears the respondent’s signature. Consequently, inasmuch as the respondent was personally served with the NTA containing the date, time, and location of her hearing, we conclude that the respondent received proper notice of her hearing. See Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 181, 185 (BIA 2001) (stating that the entry of an in absentia order is authorized when the alien has been given written notice of the removal hearing); see Cruz-Diaz v. Holder, 388 F. App’x 429 (5th Cir. 2010); see also Chavez v. Holder, 343 F. App’x 955 (5th Cir. 2009); see Ojeda-Calderon v. Holder, 726 F.3d 669, 675 (5th Cir. 2013) (“Due process allows notice of a hearing to be given solely in English to a non-English speaker if the notice would put a reasonable recipient on notice that further inquiry is required.”). Nevertheless, the respondent did not appear, and the Immigration Judge ordered her removed in absentia.
 
On appeal, the respondent claims she has demonstrated exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear based upon ineffective assistance of counsel. She claims that her attorney advised her not to appear at the immigration hearing and that he would take care of things for her (Respondent’s Brief).
 
*2 We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an ““exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. A motion to reopen alleging exceptional circumstances for a failure to appear at a removal hearing must be filed within 180 days of the order of removal, and only one such motion is allowed. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(i) & 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The respondent’s motion claiming that she did not appear at her hearing because of ineffective assistance by her former counsel, was untimely, as it was filed well beyond 180 days after the removal order was entered. However, the U.S. Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this case lies, has recognized that equitable tolling may apply in “rare and exceptional circumstances,” United States v. English, 400 F.3d 273, 275 (5th Cir. 2005), and that a party seeking to toll a filing deadline must demonstrate diligence, Pacheco v. Rice, 966 F.2d 904, 906-07 (5th Cir. 1992).1
 
In Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337, 344 (5th Cir. 2016), the Fifth Circuit instructed the Board to apply the same equitable tolling standard that the court uses in other contexts, stating that under this standard, “a litigant is entitled to equitable tolling of a statute of limitations only if the litigant establishes two elements: (1) that he has been pursuing his rights diligently; and (2) that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way and prevented timely filing.” Id. The Fifth Circuit explained further that the first element requires the litigant to establish that he pursued his rights with “reasonable diligence” and the second element requires the litigant to establish that an “extraordinary circumstance” beyond his control prevented him from complying with the applicable deadline. Id.
 
The respondent is not entitled to equitable tolling because she has not established that she has pursued her rights with reasonable diligence and that an extraordinary circumstance beyond her control prevented her from complying with the applicable deadline. The respondent has not indicated that she attempted to determine the status of her case with the Harlingen Immigration Court during the passage of over 13 years after she was ordered to appear in court in June 2005, nor that she consulted with any other attorneys between 2005 and 2018. The respondent also has not established that an extraordinary circumstance beyond her control prevented her from complying with the applicable deadline. We conclude that the respondent has not established that she is entitled to equitable tolling in this matter. See Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d at 344.
 
*3 Further, we also conclude that the respondent has not established an exceptional circumstance herein to warrant the sua sponte reopening of these proceedings. The respondent’s purported eligibility for adjustment of status does not constitute an exceptional situation. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that “the power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). The respondent was personally served with a NTA listing the time and place of her hearing and she was provided oral notice of her hearing date and the consequences of failing to appear (Exh. 1). The respondent has not established what efforts she took to determine the status of her removal proceeding after receiving the NTA almost 13 years before she filed her motion to reopen. We therefore decline to reopen proceedings sua sponte. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward F. Kelly
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note on appeal that while the respondent has complied with the procedural prerequisites outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), the respondent did not expressly argue in her brief that her motion should be equitably tolled.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARMEN GUAMAN-LEMA
File: AXXX-XX1-443 - Harlingen, TX
September 21, 2018
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rev. John J. Garkowski, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Guillermo Rey de la Garza
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Ecuador, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s October 11, 2017, decision. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen filed on August 29, 2017, seeking rescission of an in absentia order of deportation that was entered on March 22, 1996. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) filed an opposition to the motion, and has filed a brief opposing the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
An order of deportation issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to former section 242B(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(c), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed with the Immigration Judge either within 180 days after the date of the order of deportation if the alien demonstrates that he failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances (8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(iii)(A)(1)) or at any time if the alien demonstrates she did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because she was in Federal or State custody and failed to appear through no fault of her own (8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(iii)(A)(2)).
 
The Order to Show Cause (OSC) was personally served on the respondent on December 31, 1995; the notice of hearing (NOH) was served by certified mail, and the record contains a signed receipt for the NOH (IJ (unpaginated); Exhs. 1, 3). Because the respondent received notice of her hearing, her motion to rescind was subject to the 180-day time limit, and the motion was untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(iii)(A)(1). Moreover, the respondent took no action during the last 20 years to assert this claim for reopening and she has not explained why she waited until 2017 to file a motion to reopen. Her lack of diligence supports the conclusion that reopening is not warranted as a matter of discretion.
 
On appeal, the respondent also maintains that she is eligible to adjust her status because her U.S. citizen spouse has filed a petition on her behalf, which has been approved (Respondent’s Br. at 1). The formal rescission requirements need not be satisfied in certain circumstances where the respondent is eligible for relief that was unavailable at the time of the in absentia hearing and did not receive oral warnings in his/her native language regarding the bar to future discretionary relief that arises from a failure to appear. See generally Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). However, the respondent received oral warnings in her native Spanish language (Exh. 1). In addition, the respondent’s in absentia deportation order was entered on March 22, 1996, and therefore, the motion was also filed long past the 90-day deadline for the filing of a motion to reopen seeking new relief. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b).
 
*2 Additionally, the DHS has amended the rules regarding provisional unlawful presence waivers to permit individuals with final removal orders to apply for provisional unlawful presence waivers in certain instances. See Expansion of Provisional Unlawful Presence Waivers of Inadmissibility, 81 Fed. Reg. 50,244, 50,275-76 (July 29, 2016); 8 C.F.R. § 212.7(e) (4) (iv). Thus, the respondent does not need to have her removal proceedings reopened to be able to apply for a provisional unlawful presence waiver with the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS). The respondent may request an administrative stay of removal from the DHS while any waiver or provisional waiver applications are pending before USCIS. 8 C.F.R. § 241.6(a).
 
The respondent argues that she relied upon the advice of counsel telling her that once she paid bond and was released, immigration authorities were no longer interested in her (Respondent’s Br. at 1). However, the respondent has not identified that attorney; nor has she complied with any of the requirements to state a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
We find without merit the respondent’s appellate contention that she did not understand the proceedings because she did not understand English (Respondent’s Br. at 1). The OSC and Notification Requirement for Change of Address each contained instructions in both English and Spanish (Exhs. 1, 4).
 
While the respondent contends that her country conditions have worsened (Respondent’s Br. at 2), she has proffered no evidence to support that assertion. See Matter of J-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 161 (BIA 2013) (holding that removal proceedings conducted in absentia may be reopened where the alien seeks asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions); see also Matter of S-Y-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 247, 258 (BIA 2007) (explaining that it is the alien’s heavy burden to show that the proffered evidence is material, reflects changed conditions arising in the country of nationality, and supports a prima facie case for a grant of asylum).
 
While we sympathize with the respondent for wanting to remain in the United States after living here for many years, the Board is not empowered to grant relief from removal solely on equitable or humanitarian grounds. See Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734, 742 (BIA 1988).
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in her decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
*3 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Thomas G Snow
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 5921057 (BIA)

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SERGIO ARREOLA-ARREOLA
File: AXXX-XX4-117 - El Centro, CA
September 14, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert B. Jobe, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent’s case is before the Board on remand from the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. Arreola-Arreola v. Sessions, 713 F. App’x 675 (9th Cir., Feb. 26, 2018). The Department of Homeland Security has not offered a brief on remand to the Board. Upon reconsideration of the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s March 5, 2013, decision, we will sustain the appeal, and terminate the proceedings.
 
The respondent is a 61-year-old native and citizen of Mexico who entered the United States as a lawful permanent resident on April 7, 1959, at the age of two. On October 6, 1998, the respondent was ordered removed to Mexico pursuant to section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii) (1995) (alien convicted of an aggravated felony), based on his convictions for driving under the influence of alcohol in violation of sections 23152(b) and 23175 of the California Vehicle Code. The respondent waived appeal.
 
On December 26, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and terminate before the Immigration Judge. The respondent sought the exercise of the Immigration Judge’s authority to reopen and terminate the proceedings sua sponte in view his equities, claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, and a change in law whereby the 1996 crime underlying his removability was determined not to be an aggravated felony. See United States v. Trinidad-Aquino, 259 F.3d 1140 (9th Cir. 2001); Montiel-Barraza v. INS, 275 F.3d 1178 (9th Cir. 2002). On March 13, 2015, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s March 5, 2013, decision denying his motion. The Ninth Circuit granted the respondent’s petition, finding that the Board erred in dismissing the appeal, and remanded the record to the Board.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under the de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge determined that the respondent’s motion did not demonstrate an exceptional situation to warrant sua sponte reopening. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(a)(1). However, a fundamental change in law may qualify as an exceptional situation that merits the exercise of discretion to reopen a case sua sponte. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999). In view of the particular circumstances of this case, including the change in law affecting the basis of the respondent’s removability, we find that reopening sua sponte reopening was warranted. See also Sessions v. Dimaya, 138 S. Ct. 1204 (2018). Therefore, the Board’s March 13, 2015, decision will be vacated and the respondent’s appeal will be sustained. The motion to reopen will be granted, and the proceedings will be terminated without prejudice.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The Board’s March 13, 2015, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained and the motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The removal proceedings are terminated without prejudice.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA A. GONZALEZ A.K.A. MARIA ANTONIA GONZALEZ RODRIGUEZ A.K.A. ALFREDO GONZALEZ
File: AXXX-XX5-691 - Houston, TX
September 11, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert Kenneth Hoffman, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This case was last before us on February 28, 2018, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings due to ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reconsider this decision on March 30, 2018. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion.
 
In his October 27, 2016, decision, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable pursuant to section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), as an aggravated felon defined in section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(F) (Exh. 1). Specifically, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent’s conviction under Texas Penal Code § 38.04 did not qualify as a crime of violence under 18 U.S.C. § 16(a), because it does not have as an element, “the use, attempted use, or threatened use of physical force” (IJ at 10). See United States v. Sanchez-Ledezma, 630 F.3d 447, 450 (5th Cir. 2011). However, the Immigration Judge found, pursuant to the modified categorical approach, that the respondent’s conviction for evading arrest under Texas Penal Code § 38.04 created her removability under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) because, by its nature, the respondent’s offense “involves a substantial risk that physical force may be used” pursuant to 18 U.S.C. § 16(b) (IJ at 11).
 
In her motion to reconsider, the respondent correctly argues that the United States Supreme Court recently held that 18 U.S.C. § 16(b), as incorporated into the Immigration and Nationality Act, is unconstitutionally vague. See Sessions v. Dimaya, 138 S. Ct. 1204 (April 17, 2018). The respondent argues that the record should be remanded because it is now apparent that her previous counsel failed to properly contest her removability, and because she is now eligible for relief from removal. We need not address this issue, because we find, alternatively, that due to the Supreme Court’s holding in Dimaya, the respondent’s sole ground of removability is no longer sustainable. Inasmuch as the respondent is no longer removable as charged, we will terminate the respondent’s proceedings without prejudice, and the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is granted, and the respondent’s proceedings are terminated without prejudice.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

2018 WL 4692839 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ELIZABETH MARIA ESTEVEZ DE MARTINEZ A.K.A. ELIZABETH M. LANDA
File: AXXX-XX1-305 - New York, NY
August 27, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael A. Areizaga, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On February 26, 2018, the respondent submitted a motion to reopen proceedings in which the Board dismissed her appeal on August 25, 2009. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely because it was filed more than 8 years after the Board’s final administrative decision. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). It is also number-barred, as it is the respondent’s second motion to reopen. However, the respondent contends that the attorney who represented her before the Immigration Judge provided her with ineffective assistance because he did not inform her that she could pursue a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(d)(11) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(d)(11), or that her husband could file a visa petition on her behalf, and because he did not provide briefing or evidence in this regard (Motion, Tab A at 2-3). Matter of Compean, 21 I&N Dec. 51 (BIA 1995). She also seeks sua sponte reopening. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
The noted statutory time and number limitations may be excused if a respondent demonstrates that she had ineffective assistance of counsel. Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 130 (2d Cir. 2008); Jin Bo Zhao v. INS, 452 F.3d 154, 159-60 (2d Cir. 2006); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent contends that she has substantially complied with the procedural requirements for such a claim, as enumerated in Matter of Lozada (Motion at 7; Tab A). However, to warrant equitable tolling the respondent also must “demonstrate ... that [she] has exercised due diligence in pursuing [her claim].” Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 135 (2d Cir. 2000). In addition, she must demonstrate that she was prejudiced by counsel’s actions; i.e., that ““counsel’s performance was so ineffective ... [that it] impinged upon the fundamental fairness of the hearing in violation of the Fifth Amendment due process clause.” Rabiu v. INS, 41 F.3d 879, 882 (2d Cir. 1994).
 
In determining whether the respondent exercised due diligence, we consider ““whether and when the ineffective assistance ‘[was], or should have been, discovered by a reasonable person in the situation.’ Then, petitioner bears the burden of proving that [she] has exercised due diligence in the period between discovering the ineffectiveness of [her] representation and filing the motion to reopen.” Jian Hua Wang v. BIA, 508 F.3d 710, 715 (2d Cir. 2007) (quoting Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d at 134). The respondent must demonstrate that she exercise “due diligence” in pursuing her claim “during the entire period [she] ... seeks to toll.” Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d at 132.
 
*2 The respondent has not demonstrated that she exercised the requisite due diligence. The record reflects that the Immigration Judge continued proceedings on April 9, 2007, so that the respondent could file a visa petition package with the court (Tr. at 184-87). However, when the hearing convened almost 5 months later, no visa petition had been filed (Tr. at 191-92). Moreover, the record reflects that there was extensive discussion at her hearing about the respondent’s eligibility to file for adjustment of status, and pursue a waiver of inadmissibility (Tr. at 192-211). As such, the respondent was aware that she could pursue both adjustment and a waiver as of the time of her December 20, 2007, hearing.
 
In arguing that she exercised due diligence, the respondent notes that she retained a different attorney to file and brief her appeal. Although this attorney did not raise any arguments pertaining to her eligibility for a waiver of inadmissibility, the respondent has neither alleged, nor perfected, a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against him. Twum v. INS, 411 F.3d 54 (2d Cir. 2005). This is true despite the fact that the DHS specifically raised the issue of whether the respondent was eligible for a section 212(d)(11) waiver of inadmissibility in its brief on appeal (DHS Br. at 18). Although she indicates that this attorney did file a visa petition on her behalf which was ultimately granted, the respondent has not detailed the representational agreement she had with him, as required (Motion, Tab C). Id. She also has not explained what, if any, actions she took with respect to her claim following the Board’s 2009 adverse decision.1 As such, we find that she has not demonstrated the requisite due diligence. Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d at 135.
 
In addition, the respondent has not demonstrated that she was prejudiced by counsel’s actions, as required. To demonstrate prejudice, or to otherwise succeed on a motion to reopen, the respondent must establish a prima facie case for the underlying substantive relief sought; i.e., adjustment of status and a section 212(d)(l 1) waiver of inadmissibility. See Rabiu v. INS, 41 F.3d at 882-83; see also INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104 (1988). In dismissing the respondent’s appeal, the Board upheld the Immigration Judge’s alternate basis for denying her application for adjustment of status, in the exercise of discretion (BIA at 3, ftnt. 1; IJ at 14). The record reflects that the Immigration Judge found the respondent not credible, and that she had lied to federal agents, a District Court judge, and the Immigration Judge (IJ at 7-14). The respondent has neither addressed, nor rebutted, the Immigration Judge’s findings. As both adjustment of status and a waiver of inadmissibility are discretionary forms of relief, the respondent has not shown that she was prejudiced by former counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance.
 
*3 In closing, we recognize that the respondent has alternatively asked that her proceedings be reopened sua sponte, citing various humanitarian factors in this regard (Motion at A, pgs. 1-5). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). However, an individual’s acquisition of equities after remaining in the United States for many years, and the resulting hardships to that person spouse or children, following a removal order are, unfortunately, common situations. The authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte, however, is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations where enforcing them might result in hardship. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The respondent bears the burden to show exceptional circumstances supporting sua sponte reopening. See Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216 (BIA 2000). As the respondent has not met her burden in this case, sua sponte reopening is not warranted.
 
Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent also argues that she was diligent in pursuing her claim as the visa petition filed on her behalf remained pending until it was approved on July 2, 2015. However, the fact that a visa petition was pending, without more, is insufficient to demonstrate due diligence. We also note that there is no evidence that respondent’s second counsel was involved in the filing of this visa petition, as the notice was addressed to the petitioner, her husband, and not former counsel. Furthermore, the respondent had not explained what action she undertook since the visa petition was approved, until the filing of the instant motion, with respect to her claim.

2018 WL 4692855 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROBERTO PEREZ CHAVEZ
File: AXXX-XX9-095 - Seattle, WA
August 23, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steve Tanijo, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Termination of proceedings
 
*1 This case is before the Board pursuant to a March 13, 2018, order of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, which granted the Government’s motion to remand. In its motion, the Government sought remand for the Board to reconsider the respondent’s removability in light of the subsequent vacating of the respondent’s conviction by the state court. Attached to the Government’s motion is a copy of the May 23, 2017, court order from the King County Superior Court, Washington, vacating the respondent’s conviction due to ineffective assistance of counsel resulting in a constitution violation pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010).
 
Subsequently, the respondent filed a motion to dismiss to which the Department of Homeland Security has filed no response. Attached to the respondent’s motion is a copy of the May 23, 2017, court order. The respondent’s conviction is no longer valid for immigration purposes. Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003) (holding that a conviction vacated by the criminal court based upon a procedural or substantive defect in the underlying proceedings is no longer a valid conviction for immigration purposes). As the respondent was charged with removability solely based upon this now vacated conviction, the motion will be granted and proceedings terminated.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered:
 
ORDER: The motion to dismiss is granted and the respondent’s removal proceedings are hereby terminated.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 4692855 (BIA)
End of Document

2018 WL 4692861 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TRANG THI XUAN PHUNG A.K.A. TRANG THI-XUAN PHUNG A.K.A. THI XUAN TRANG PHUNG A.K.A. XUAN TRANG THI PHUNG
File: AXXX XX4 835 - Houston, TX
August 21, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lawrence E. Rushton, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Vietnam, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 7, 2017, denying her motion to reopen removal proceedings. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on September 13, 2016. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) did not file a response to the appeal. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be reopened and remanded for further proceedings.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C), an order of removal entered in absentia may be rescinded only (1) upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances, or (2) upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice of the hearing in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act, or that the alien was in Federal or State custody and did not appear through no fault of the alien. Sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and (ii) of the Act; see also Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722, 722-23 (BIA 1999).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that she demonstrated a lack of notice and exceptional circumstances for not attending the scheduled removal hearing due to ineffective assistance by her prior attorney (Respondent’s Br. at 5-7). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The respondent claims that her prior counsel did not inform her that she was placed in removal proceedings and that she was required to appear at her removal hearing, scheduled to be held on September 13, 2016 (Id.). Ineffective assistance of counsel may constitute such an exceptional circumstance if the ineffective assistance results in an alien’s failure to appear at her hearing. Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 647 (5th Cir. 2014); Gutierrez-Morales v. Homan, 461 F.3d 605, 609 (5th Cir. 2006).
 
*2 The Notice to Appear, and the Notice of Hearing which scheduled her removal hearing for September 13, 2016, were mailed to the respondent’s prior attorney (Exhs. 1-3). The respondent did not appear at the hearing and was ordered removed in absentia. However, the record shows that respondent’s prior attorney entered an appearance to represent the respondent before the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) only - the Notice of Entry is the Form G-28, and only the first page of the Form is in the record (Exh. 3). The incomplete Form G-28 contains the respondent’s prior attorney’s address, 11310 Bellaire, Suite 148, Houston, Texas 77072, but does not reflect whether the respondent’s address was also provided (Id.). Furthermore, the Notice of Hearing was mailed to an incorrect address, i.e., suite #14 and not #148 as provided on the Form G-28 and on the Notice to Appear (Exhs. 1-3).1 While notice to counsel is sufficient to establish notice to the respondent, her prior attorney did not represent her in removal proceedings. Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 259 (BIA 1985) (notice to the alien’s attorney constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). We conclude that the mailing of the Notice of Hearing to the respondent’s prior counsel in proceedings before USCIS (not removal proceedings), combined with the mailing of the notice to an incorrect address establishes that she did not receive notice of the hearing in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act. Therefore, we will sustain the appeal, rescind the in absentia order of removal, reopen proceedings, and remand the record for further proceedings.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Notice to Appear references the suite number 148 and “Blvd.” as part of the prior counsel’s address. In contrast, the Form G-28 does not reference “Blvd” in providing the counsel’s address.

2018 WL 4692860 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRANCIS ANTONIO COLLADO REYES
File: AXXX-XX2-347 - Boston, MA
August 21, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey B. Rubin, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Termination of proceedings
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, appeals from the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated July 19, 2017, finding the respondent removable as charged. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent became a lawful permanent resident of the United States in 1994. In May of 2011, he left the United States for the Dominican Republic due to the death of his mother. He argues he intended to stay there 1 month, but due to grief and depression he remained there over 1 year, attempting to return on September 14, 2012. At this time, he attempted to enter the United States with a fraudulent stamp in his passport indicating he had not been absent from the United States for this length of time. The Immigration Judge found the respondent lacked credibility and had abandoned his lawful permanent resident status (IJ at 9-10). The respondent and his attorney were provided a deadline to file any applications for relief and they did not meet it (IJ at 11). On appeal, the respondent argues he was credible and appeared less so due to memory issues and that he did not abandon his lawful permanent resident status (Respondent’s Br. at 7-11). He also claims he did not commit fraud and did not abandon his applications for relief. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994).
 
Generally, to gain admission into the United States, an immigrant must present a valid, unexpired immigrant visa as well as a valid, unexpired passport or other travel document. See section 211(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1181(a). If a person fails to produce such documents, he or she is deemed inadmissible under section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I).
 
When an applicant for admission has a colorable claim to returning resident status, the Department of Homeland Security bears the burden to establish by clear and convincing evidence that the applicant’s status has changed and he or she is not eligible for admission. See Katebi v. Ashcroft, 396 F.3d 463 (1st Cir. 2005); Matter of Huang, 19 I&N Dec. 749, 753 (BIA 1988); see also Woodby v. INS, 385 U.S. 276 (1966); Matter of Rivens, 25 I&N Dec. 623 (BIA 2011). In order to qualify as a returning resident alien, an alien must be returning to an unrelinquished lawful permanent residence after a temporary visit abroad. Matter of Huang, 19 I&N Dec. at 749. A temporary visit abroad is one that is “relatively short, fixed by some early event, or” that “will terminate upon the occurrence of an event having a reasonable possibility of occurring within a relatively short period of time.” Katebi v. Ashcroft, 396 F.3d at 466, quoting Chavez-Ramirez v. INS, 792 F.2d 932, 936-37 (9th Cir. 1986).
 
*2 The required intent is not one of ultimately returning to the United States, but, rather, an intent to return to a residence in the United States within a relatively short period of time from a temporary visit abroad. Katebi v. Ashcroft, 396 F.3d at 466. The definition of “temporary” is not inflexible and varies in application depending upon the facts and circumstances of each particular case and is not defined in terms of elapsed time alone. See Katebi v. Ashcroft, 396 F.3d at 466. Rather, the intention of the alien, when it can be ascertained, will control. Id.; Matter of Kane, 15 I&N Dec. 258 (BIA 1975). We have in the past ascertained an alien’s intention by examining the location of family ties, property holdings, and employment. Matter of Muller, 16 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1978); Matter of Quijencio, 15 I&N Dec. 95 (BIA 1974); see also Katebi v. Ashcroft, 396 F.3d at 710.
 
The Immigration Judge’s findings regarding both the respondent’s intent in relation to his return to the United States and his life in the Dominican Republic are factual in nature. Thus, we review them for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We conclude the Immigration Judge’s factual findings are not clearly erroneous. The respondent first testified that he had no memory of when he left the United States and then stated that he left the country for, at the most, 1 month (Tr. at 42-71). Over a year later, he again appeared in Immigration Court, but this time he testified that he had left the United States for Puerto Rico due to the death of his mother and had remained there for over 1 year (Tr. at 110-18). He also recalled giving a friend his passport to have a date stamped in it indicting that he had been out of the United States for a shorter length of time (Tr. at 121-24). The Immigration Judge found this directly contradictory testimony to support an adverse credibility determination (IJ at 9-10).
 
The respondent argues he has memory problems, and was suffering from depression and stress due to the death of his mother (Respondent’s Br. at 7-9). He has submitted some documents supporting this claim and he takes medicine for the depression and memory (Exhs. 6, 7). The Immigration Judge considered this evidence but concluded it was not sufficient to overcome the adverse credibility determination (IJ at 9-10). The respondent’s mother died in 2011 and the hearing, when he first testified to not remembering his trip, was in 2014.1 The Immigration Judge is permitted to make reasonable inferences among the plausible possibilities and explanations for discrepancies in the record and he did so. See Matter of D-R-, 25 I&N Dec. 445, 455 (BIA 2011) (explaining that an Immigration Judge may make reasonable inferences from direct and circumstantial evidence in the record as a whole and is not required to accept respondent’s account where other plausible views of the evidence are supported by the record). There is no clear error in the Immigration Judge adverse credibility determination.
 
*3 Given this lack of credibility, the Immigration Judge did not credit the respondent’s claim that he had no intention of remaining in the Dominican Republic for as long as he did (IJ at 10). Although the respondent has children and grandchildren in the United States, he also has many relatives in the Dominican Republic. He testified to having no steady employment or property in the United States and to having paid no taxes since 2007 (IJ at 10). The record provides clear and convincing evidence that the respondent abandoned his lawful permanent resident status in the United States. See Katebi v. Ashcroft, 396 F.3d at 466. Although the respondent testified that he intended to return to the United States within 1 month (Tr. at 111), an alien’s professed intent to return to the United States without more is not sufficient to support his claim. Matter of Huang, 19 I&N Dec. at 755. We acknowledge the respondent’s argument that his situation is different than the one found in Katebi (Respondent’s Br. at 11). Each case must be reviewed on its own facts, and, here, the Immigration Judge applied the appropriate standard.
 
In 2015, the respondent attempted to enter the United States with a fraudulent stamp in his passport indicating that he was not out of the country for as long as he had been (IJ at 10-11). He testified that a friend took his passport away from him for several days and obtained the stamps (Tr. at 121-23). No clear error exists in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent was not credible when he claimed he did not know the stamp was fraudulent (IJ at 10-11). We affirm the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent is removable as charged.
 
We further affirm the Immigration Judge determination that the respondent abandoned any applications for relief by not filing them in a timely manner (IJ at 11). The respondent was represented by an attorney from 2013 (Tr. 15), and both the respondent and his attorney were clearly informed of the August 1, 2016, deadline for filing any applications for relief (Tr. at 180-90). A new attorney took over representation on August 1, 2016, but no applications for relief were filed (IJ at 11). Although, at a hearing represented by the new attorney, the parties discussed the applicability of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (Tr. at 211), no attempt has been made to comply with the requirements it lays out for motions based on ineffective assistance of counsel (IJ at 11). The respondent testified that his conflict with his previous attorney was over the fees he was being charged (Tr. at 217). The respondent has not presented good cause for failing to timely file any applications for relief (Respondent’s Br. at 13-14).
 
The Immigration Judge properly found that the respondent, by not complying with the filing deadline provided, abandoned his opportunity to apply for relief (IJ at 11). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c); Moreta v. Holder, 723 F.3d 31, 34-35 (1st Cir. 2013); Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547, 549 (BIA 1992); see also Matter of Interiano-Rosa, 25 I&N Dec. 264, 265-66 (BIA 2010) (concerning late filed documents). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*4 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Joan B Geller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

A hearing regarding competence to testify was discussed, but declined by the respondent and his attorney (Tr. at 75-79). See Matter of M-A-M-, 25 I&N Dec. 474 (BIA 2011).

IN RE: ANA YURI AGUILAR ROMERO
File: AXXX XX4 609 - Charlotte, NC
August 10, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Aimee E. Deverall, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James A. Jones
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s November 28, 2017, derision ordering her removed to Mexico. The respondent has also filed a motion to remand. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) moves for summary affirmance. The record will be remanded.
 
We review the Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We find it necessary to remand the record for the preparation of a new decision as we conclude that the Immigration Judge’s November 28, 2017, order does not contain sufficient specificity for adequate review. See generally Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). On November 8, 2017, the respondent filed a motion for administrative closure.1 On November 13, 2017, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion in a form order and indicated that she could appear telephonically for the next hearing. According to the transcript, the respondent then appeared in person for her hearing on November 28, 2017 and renewed her request for administrative closure or in the alternative, for a continuance (Tr. at 11-18). The Immigration Judge denied said requests and ordered the respondent removed to Mexico in a form order (Tr. at 16-18). We conclude that this order does not contain sufficient findings of fact or conclusions of law for us to adequately review the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion for a continuance.2 We will therefore remand the record for the entry of a new decision.
 
Moreover, the respondent has filed a motion to remand on appeal, alleging she was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel below and seeks the opportunity to apply for new relief. Considering the facts and circumstances of this, we will remand the record for the entry of a new decision. Upon remand, the Immigration Judge should consider the arguments raised in and the evidence submitted with the respondent’s motion to remand and afford the respondent the opportunity to apply for any available relief.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for the entry of a new decision.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

The Immigration Judge previously administratively closed these proceedings on October 27, 2016. On September 11, 2017, the DHS moved to re-calendar proceedings, which the Immigration Judge granted on September 12, 2017.

2

The Attorney General recently determined that Immigration Judges and the Board do not have the general authority to administratively close cases. See Matter of Castro-Tum, 27 I&N Dec. 271, 287 n.9 (A.G. 2018) (stating that 8 C.F.R. § 212.7(e)(4)(iii) cannot be an independent source of authority for administrative closure because only the Attorney General may expand the authority of Immigration Judges or the Board).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
