IN RE: GENESIS DOLLANA CIFUENTES-LOPEZ
File: AXXX XX6 032 - Dallas, TX
July 27, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Christi J. Giddeon, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s January 11, 2018, decision denying her December 21, 2017, motion to reopen proceedings. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia after she did not appear for a scheduled removal hearing on July 18, 2017. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
An in absentia removal order may be rescinded only if the alien files a motion to reopen: (1) within 180 days after the date of the removal order in which he establishes that the failure to appear was because of “exceptional circumstances,” as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(e)(1); or (2) at any time where the alien establishes that she did not have proper notice of the hearing at which the removal order was entered. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act.
 
The respondent concedes that her attorney of record received proper notice of her July 18, 2017, hearing. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 2. The respondent states in an affidavit that she did not receive a notification of the hearing that her attorney sent to her by mail and that she learned of the hearing at which she was ordered removed when she appeared at the Immigration Court on August 3, 2017, which she thought was her current hearing date. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 8. The respondent filed her motion to reopen on December 21, 2017.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings. Service on the respondent’s attorney of record constitutes proper service of a hearing notice. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.26(c)(2), 1292.5(a); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985). The Immigration Judge therefore correctly concluded that reopening under section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act is not warranted as notice was proper in this case. The respondent’s motion to reopen suggests that the attorney may have sent the hearing notice to an incorrect address. See Motion to Reopen at 2 ¶ 3 (stating that the attorney erred in sending the motion to 3608 rather than 3608½ NW 13th Street). However, the respondent has not alleged ineffective assistance of counsel or complied with any of the procedural requirements for establishing such a claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent alleges in her affidavit that she frequently experiences problems with her mail, but did not submit any evidence of this assertion and did not promptly file a motion to reopen upon learning that she had been ordered removed in absentia. Cf. Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 666 (BIA 2008). We affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen where the respondent did not establish that notice was improper or that exceptional circumstances existed excusing her failure to appear. See sections 240(b)(5)(C), (e)(1) of the Act.
 
*2 We further affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s request that proceedings be reopened sua sponte as the respondent has not shown that exceptional circumstances are present in this case. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (stating that sua sponte reopening is an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional circumstances). We note that the respondent may continue to seek an immigration benefit based on an approved visa petition from outside of the United States with the appropriate waivers. For the aforementioned reasons, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Thomas G Snow
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 4611504 (BIA)
End of Document

2018 WL 4611510 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DWIGHT LOWELL MORGAN
File: AXXX XX9 226 - New York, NY
July 26, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jaclyn Brooke Aruch, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ada G. Guillod
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, has appealed from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated January 12, 2018, denying his motion to reopen proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
In a decision dated January 8, 2015, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed from the United States to Jamaica. On that same date, the respondent married a United States citizen. On October 11, 2017, the respondent filed a motion to reopen to allow him an opportunity to file an application for adjustment of status based on the May 3, 2017, approval of the visa petition filed on his behalf by his wife.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen, filed 2 years and 9 months after the final administrative decision was rendered, is untimely (IJ at 2). Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent asserts that the filing deadline should be tolled because he received ineffective assistance of counsel. In this regard, the respondent states that former counsel neglected to inform him that he could file a motion to reopen the removal proceedings within 90 days as a result of his marriage to a United States citizen who had filed an immediate relative visa petition on his behalf. The respondent reported in his affidavit that he told former counsel of the marriage the following day, and former counsel, retained by the respondent’s wife, filed a visa petition based on that marriage on February 26, 2015. The respondent provided evidence that a complaint was filed with New York’s Attorney Grievance Committee and states that a copy of such complaint was sent to his former attorney.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion because he found that even if the respondent’s former counsel had acted to his detriment, the respondent did not act with due diligence (IJ at 3-4). Ali v. Gonzales, 448 F.3d 515, 517 (2d Cir. 2006); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2d Cir. 2006). The respondent had already received numerous continuances, and he was not eligible for relief from removal when the Immigration Judge issued his January 8, 2015, decision. The visa petition filed on the respondent’s behalf was approved on May 3, 2017. Although the respondent was aware a final order of removal had been entered against him, he did not file the motion to reopen until October 11, 2017, after he had been taken into immigration custody (IJ at 4).
 
*2 The respondent asserts on appeal that the time from January 8, 2015, when he was ordered removed until May 3, 2017, when his visa petition was approved, should be equitably tolled (Respondent’s Br. at 12). However, even if such time period were tolled, the respondent waited another 161 days to file his motion to reopen. The respondent has not shown that he was diligent during this time period when he believed his proceedings could be reopened as a result of the approval of his visa petition. Accordingly, even if ineffective assistance of counsel were found and the period for filing a motion to reopen is tolled from January 8, 2015, to May 3, 2017, reopening is not warranted because the respondent waited another 161 days to file his motion to reopen.
 
Nor has the respondent shown that application of the extraordinary remedy of sua sponte reopening is warranted. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The respondent has proffered additional evidence on appeal. However, we do not consider evidence submitted for the first time on appeal, and a remand is unwarranted where the additional evidence would not change the result in the case. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(iv), 1003.2(c)(1); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 4611510 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.




2018 WL 4611514 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROSA CANDIDA MENDOZA-REYES
File: AXXX XX3 834 - New York, NY
July 25, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert K. Lacy, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s January 10, 2018, decision denying her motion to reopen removal proceedings and rescind an October 24, 2006, in absentia removal order. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board must defer to the Immigration Judge’s factual findings unless they are clearly erroneous. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated that her untimely motion to reopen should be granted due to ineffective assistance of counsel (IJ at 1-2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) (an order of removal entered in absentia may be rescinded upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances). The respondent concedes that she received the notice of hearing informing her of the October 24, 2006, master calendar hearing (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 4, Tab F). She also concedes that she was in New York City on October 24, 2006, ready to attend her hearing, but received faulty advice from the American Immigrant Federation (AIF) that she would be detained and removed if she appeared (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 4-5; Tab H).
 
We acknowledge the evidence of record that the New York Attorney General shut down AIF in 2010 because it defrauded immigrants by providing, inter alia, unauthorized legal advice (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Tab G). However, even accepting that this constituted ineffective assistance, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent did not act with due diligence in pursuing her immigration case (IJ at 1-2). See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127 (2d Cir. 2008). The respondent turned 18 in 2008, and the action against AIF occurred in 2010. The respondent was aware of her removal proceedings, but did not seek current counsel until April 2017, after her marriage to a United States citizen. As noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent has not adequately addressed her efforts to follow up on her immigration case in the 10 years between the 2006 in absentia order and 2017, when she filed her motion to reopen (IJ at 1-2). Under these circumstances, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not exercise due diligence in such a way that tolls the filing deadline for motions to reopen.
 
*2 We also agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s request to reopen her removal proceedings pursuant to his sua sponte authority (IJ at 2). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (explaining that proceedings may be sua sponte reopened where exceptional circumstances exist). We acknowledge that the respondent is the mother of three United States citizen children and the beneficiary of an approved I-130 petition filed on her behalf by her United States citizen husband (Respondent’s Br. at Tab G). However, U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) has exclusive jurisdiction over the respondent’s application for adjustment of status because she is an arriving alien and does not qualify for the limited circumstances in which an Immigration Judge retains jurisdiction over adjustment of status applications. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1.1(q), 1001.1(q) (defining the term “arriving alien”); 8 C.F.R. §§ 245.2(a)(1) (setting forth the jurisdiction of the USCIS over adjustment applications for arriving aliens), 1245.2(a)(1)(ii) (setting forth the limited circumstances in which an Immigration Judge will have jurisdiction over the adjustment application of an arriving alien). Further, the respondent is not prohibited from pursuing her applications for adjustment of status and a provisional unlawful presence waiver (provided she is granted consent to reapply for admission) before USCIS even with an unexecuted administratively final order of removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 212.7(e)(3)-(4).
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Blair O’Connor
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 4611514 (BIA)
End of Document

2018 WL 4611528 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LOURDES SUYAPA PENA-MEJIA
File: AXXX XX6 202 - Harlingen, TX
July 18, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDEN
 
Tina Y. Song, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rod Wolthoff
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Honduras, has appealed the Immigration Judge decision dated March 5, 2018, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for her failure to appear for the hearing on October 31, 2002. The Department of Homeland Security has opposed the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The record reflects that the Notice to Appear was served personally on the respondent on August 14, 2002, and the respondent received oral, in the Spanish language, and written notice of the consequences of a failure to appear and of the requirement to notify the Immigration Court immediately by using Form EOIR-33 whenever she changed her address or telephone number during the proceeding (Exh. 1). The notice of hearing was sent to the address provided by the respondent and it was not returned to the Immigration Court as undeliverable by the postal service. The respondent did not appear for the hearing on October 31, 2002, and the Immigration Judge issued an in absentia order of removal.
 
There is a receipt presumption for notices sent by regular mail, which “is weaker than that accorded to notice sent by certified mail.” Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 673 (BIA 2008). We consider all relevant evidence, including circumstantial evidence, offered to rebut the presumption. Relevant factors include the following: “(1) the respondent’s affidavit; (2) affidavits from family members or other individuals who are knowledgeable about the facts relevant to whether notice was received; (3) the respondent’s actions upon learning of the in absentia order, and whether due diligence was exercised in seeking to redress the situation; (4) any prior affirmative application for relief, indicating that the respondent had an incentive to appear; (5) any prior application for relief filed with the Immigration Court or any prima facie evidence in the record or the respondent’s motion of statutory eligibility for relief, indicating that the respondent had an incentive to appear; (6) the respondent’s previous attendance at Immigration Court hearings, if applicable; and (7) any other circumstances or evidence indicating possible nonreceipt of notice.” Matter of M-R-A, 24 I&N Dec at 673-74.
 
*2 The Immigration Judge correctly determined that based on all of the evidence in this matter, the respondent has not rebutted the weaker presumption of delivery (IJ at 2).1 See Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. at 674-75; see also Li v. Holder, 402 Fed. Appx. 923, 924 (5th Cir. 2010) (holding that an alien’s failure to provide a current address precludes the alien from obtaining rescission of an in absentia order of removal). Specifically, as noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent did not provide an affidavit or any other evidentiary material stating that she did not receive the notice of hearing (IJ at 2). Moreover, the respondent did not show that she had an incentive to appear at her hearing. In addition, the notice of hearing was sent to the most recent address provided by the respondent and was not returned by the postal service as undeliverable. Furthermore, the respondent waited over 15 years, until after she became eligible for an alternative form of relief, to file a motion to reopen. These factors outbalanced her claim that she did not receive any notice.
 
Regarding the respondent’s request to reopen to adjust her status based on an approved I-130 visa petition, in Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998), the Board held that an alien may reopen for purposes of pursuing previously unavailable relief in circumstances where she was not given oral warnings in her native language of the consequences of failing to appear at a deportation hearing and where the motion complies with the general time limits governing motions to reopen. See also Matter of Monges, 25 I&N Dec. 246 (BIA 2010). Neither condition is satisfied here.
 
To the extent that the respondent is alleging ineffective assistance by her former attorney, we note that she has not complied with any of the evidentiary requirements for asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988).2 See also Rodriguez-Manzano v. Holder, 666 F.3d 948, 953 (5th Cir. 2012) (court requires strict Lozada compliance). Moreover, the respondent has not established that she was prejudiced by her former counsel’s actions. See De Zavala v. Ashcroft, 385 F.3d 879, 883 (5th Cir. 2004) (noting that “an alien must make an initial showing of substantial prejudice” to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
To the extent that the respondent has submitted new evidence with her appeal, we generally cannot consider any new evidence proffered on appeal. See Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv). The additional documentation here is not sufficiently material so as to warrant a remand for its consideration.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Thomas G Snow
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

On appeal the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred by applying a strong presumption of the delivery of the notice of hearing (Respondent’s Br. at 3). However, a review of the record indicates that the Immigration Judge properly determined that the respondent did not rebut the weaker presumption of delivery (IJ at 2).

2

Under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 637, a motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel should be supported by an affidavit, reflect whether a complaint had been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and include a response to the allegations by former counsel or report counsel’s failure or refusal to respond. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALBA CELEDONIA MELGAR-BRIZUELA ASHLI ANAHI ORELLANA-MELGAR
Files: AXXX XX1 679 - San Antonio, TX AXXX XX1 680
July 17, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
CM Boston Cote, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on January 16, 2018, when we dismissed the respondents’ appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of their applications for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture and declined to remand their proceedings for voluntary departure or adjustment of status. The respondents have filed a timely motion to reconsider and request for a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion. The motion and stay will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, the respondents’ motion is both a motion to reconsider and a motion to reopen. A motion to reconsider contests the correctness of the original decision based upon the previous factual record, whereas a motion to reopen seeks a new hearing based on new or previously unavailable evidence. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 57-58 (BIA 2006). Here, the respondents challenge the decisions of the Board and the Immigration Judge and additionally allege ineffective assistance of their former counsel.
 
We first address that aspect of the motion requesting reconsideration. A party seeking reconsideration requests that the original decision be reexamined in light of alleged legal or factual errors, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. at 56; Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). Here, the respondents largely raises the same or similar arguments in the motion to reconsider as were raised in their prior appeal brief. We considered those arguments and found them unpersuasive when viewed within the record as a whole before we dismissed their appeal and denied their motion to remand. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. at 58 (stating that “a motion to reconsider is not a process by which a party may submit, in essence, the same brief presented on appeal . . .”). Disagreement with the result is not sufficient. Therefore, the motion to reconsider is denied.
 
The respondents’ motion is also in the nature of a timely motion to reopen. Applicable regulations provide that a motion to reopen “shall state the new facts that will be proven at a hearing to be held if the motion is granted.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Moreover, a movant must satisfy the “heavy burden” of establishing that if the proceedings were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). To prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, an alien must show that competent counsel would have acted otherwise and that the alien was prejudiced. See Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F.3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
*2 To the extent that the respondents are attempting to raise a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, they have not complied with any of the Lozada1 procedural requirements. See Matter of Zmijewska, 24 I&N Dec. 87, 94-95 (BIA 2007) (finding that the alien satisfied the concerns underlying the Lozada requirements by complying with the first two steps). Regardless, the respondents have not demonstrated that they were prejudiced by their prior counsel’s actions or inactions. See Gutierrez-Morales v. Homan, 461 F.3d 605, 609 (5th Cir. 2006) (rejecting claim of ineffective assistance of counsel based on failure to pursue a discretionary waiver of relief from removal).
 
The respondents seek reopening to apply for adjustment of status or voluntary departure. However, the respondents are not eligible to adjust under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1155(a), as they were not inspected and admitted into the United States (see Exh. 1; IJ at 1-2). Moreover, despite the assertions in their motions, the respondents are not charged as arriving aliens (Compare Motion at with Exh. 1). Therefore, even if the visa petition is ultimately approved, the respondents would need to seek a provisional waiver of their unlawful presence in anticipation of consular processing. As we noted in our previous decision, such a waiver could be sought without reopening the proceedings. 8 C.F.R. § 212.7(e)(4)(iv). Further, as we mentioned in our prior decision, the respondents are not eligible for voluntary departure because they were not present in the United States for 1 year immediately preceding the issuance of the Notice to Appear (Decision of the Board, at 1). See section 240B(b)(1)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b)(1)(A); Matter of Arguelles, 22 I&N Dec. 811, 816 (BIA 1999).
 
As there is no relief immediately available to the respondents in reopened removal proceedings, they have not demonstrated prejudice from counsel’s actions or omissions such that they have established a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. As such, the respondents’ motion is insufficient to meet their “heavy burden” of showing that it is likely that the result would change if the proceedings were reopened. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. at 464. Therefore, we do not find that they have met their burden of showing that reopening of these proceedings is warranted.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Matter of Lozada, 19 I & N Dec. at 637, requires a successful ineffective assistance of counsel claim be accompanied by: “(1) an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement with former counsel concerning what action would be taken and what counsel did or did not represent in this regard; (2) proof that the alien notified former counsel of the allegations of ineffective assistance and allowed counsel an opportunity to respond; and (3) if a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities is claimed, a statement as to whether the alien filed a complaint with any disciplinary authority regarding counsel’s conduct and, if a complaint was not filed, an explanation for not doing so.”).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PETER KIGANO GATHIRU
File: AXXX XX2 112 - Seattle, WA
July 17, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tom Youngjohn, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mark Hardy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on February 25, 2015, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s September 15, 2014, decision that denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order entered against him on October 10, 2006. On February 22, 2018, the respondent filed a motion with the Board requesting reopening based upon a claim that he received ineffective assistance from the attorney who represented him before the Immigration Judge. He specifically alleges that former counsel failed to provide effective representation with regard to a motion to reopen the proceedings that was filed with Board. The respondent claims that the motion was granted and the case remanded to the Immigration Judge. However, former counsel failed to notify him of the remand, resulting in an in absentia order of removal.
 
The record of proceedings does not reflect that the Board previously reopened these proceedings, and a review of internal agency records confirms that no such order was issued. The procedural history of this case, as reflected in the previous decisions of the Immigration Judge and this Board, is that the respondent was aware of the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order of removal because prior counsel requested and was permitted to review the record of proceedings in 2009 to confirm entry of the order in contemplation of the filing a motion to reopen. The respondent eventually filed the motion to reopen five years later, on August 22, 2014, claiming that he never received notice of his removal hearing. The Immigration Judge and this Board both concluded that, even assuming lack of notice, the respondent failed to act with due diligence in pursuing reopening. The Immigration Judge found, and this Board agreed, that there was no evidence to corroborate the respondent’s claim of non-receipt in any event, and reopening based upon lack of notice was therefore not warranted. The respondent’s appeal was dismissed and nothing further occurred until the filing of the instant motion.
 
The respondent did not seek reconsideration of the Board’s decision affirming the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent failed to establish non-receipt of the hearing notice for his 2006 removal hearing. We find no reason to revisit the issue.
 
The respondent’s current motion based on former counsel’s alleged failure to inform him that this Board ordered the proceedings reopened is without merit because we never issued such an order. Inasmuch as the respondent cannot demonstrate prejudice arising from circumstances that never occurred, the respondent’s motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance will be denied. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (an alien asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must demonstrate prejudice); see also Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785, 793-94 (9th Cir. 2005) (stating that an alien must demonstrate that prior counsel’s performance may have affected the outcome of the proceedings).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 4002256 (BIA)

2018 WL 4002273 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BRAULIO JUAREZ-CHILEL
File: AXXX XX9 535 - Baltimore, MD
July 11, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Theodore J. Murphy, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals the Immigration Judge’s July 9, 2014, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings, in which he was ordered removed in absentia.1 The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review issues of law, discretion, or judgment de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. We concur with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not show that he is the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel (IJ at 1). Notwithstanding the respondent’s allegations on appeal, the Immigration Judge appropriately gave significant weight to prior counsel’s detailed refutation of the respondent’s allegations of error as well as the Disciplinary Counsel of the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania’s dismissal of the respondent’s complaint against his prior counsel (IJ at 1; Motion at Tab H). While the respondent continues to assert he was not given adequate notice of his hearing, we find no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s reliance on the attorney’s detailed discussion of his interactions with the respondent (IJ at 1). Nor do we find clear error with the Immigration Judge’s determination that prior counsel directly notified the respondent of the time and location of the hearing (IJ at 1). Under the circumstances, we are not persuaded of any error with the representation, much less prejudice.
 
Additionally, the respondent did not meet his heavy burden of showing that his motion fell within the exception to the time limit for motions to reopen to apply for asylum or withholding of removal based on changed circumstances or changed country conditions “arising in the country of nationality.” Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 473 (BIA 1992); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii). In order to prevail on his motion, the respondent was required to produce evidence that was material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at his previous hearing before the Immigration Judge. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of Act, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). He was also required to demonstrate his prima facie eligibility for asylum and withholding of removal, to wit, that his new evidence would likely alter the result of his case. See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104-05 (1988). The respondent failed to demonstrate that reopening was warranted. Instead, the motion included evidence of past and ongoing conditions of crime in Guatemala.
 
*2 Even if the motion were deemed to have established changed country conditions, the motion would not succeed since it did not establish the respondent’s prima facie eligibility for relief. See Matter of Gutierrez, 21 I&N Dec. 479, 482 (BIA 1996). Specifically, the respondent indicates in his affidavit that he fears that gangs or people from the neighboring municipality will target him for persecution in Guatemala because they think he has money or want his family’s land (Motion at Tab C). Being targeted for mistreatment and extortion by criminal elements in Guatemala would not constitute persecution on account of his political opinion or membership in a particular social group. See Matter of A-B-, 27 I&N Dec. 316, 335, 338-39 (A.G. 2018); Matter of A-M-E- & J-G-U-, 24 I&N Dec. 69, 76 (BIA 2007) (recognizing that being a victim of criminal extortion is not persecution on account of a protected ground under the Act); see also Matter of C-A-, 23 I&N Dec. 951 (BIA 2006), clarified by Matter of W-G-R, 26 I&N Dec. 208 (BIA 2014), and Matter of M-E-V-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 227 (BIA 2014). Nor did the motion establish prima facie eligibility for protection under the CAT, i.e., that it is more likely than not that the respondent will face torture by or with the acquiescence (to include the concept of willful blindness) of a member of the government of Guatemala upon return to that country. Therefore, the respondent’s motion for reopening for the purpose of applying for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the CAT was properly denied.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

On December 23, 2014, we remanded this matter back to the Immigration Court for the recovery of the record of proceedings. Once the record of proceedings was finally located, the matter was administratively returned to the Board on August 24, 2016.

2018 WL 7572470 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SALEH ALI AL-ASHMALI
File: AXXX XX9 378 - Charlotte, NC
July 11, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Fleta Christina Sabra
Accredited Representative
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Susan Lecker
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 In a decision dated January 9, 2018, the Board upheld an Immigration Judge’s decision denying in discretion the respondent’s application for a fraud waiver under section 237(a)(1)(H) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(H). The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen these proceedings. To the extent that he challenges our last decision, his motion is also in the nature of a timely motion to reconsider. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 1991) (providing that the nature of a motion to reconsider is that the original decision was defective in some regard). The respondent has also filed a request for a stay of removal, which we denied in an order issued on June 26, 2018.1 The motions to reopen and reconsider, which remain pending and are opposed by the Department of Homeland Security, will be denied.
 
In the instant motion, the respondent argues that the Board and the Immigration Judge overlooked evidence pertaining to conditions in his native country of Yemen that would be adverse to his family, medical evidence of one of his United States citizen children, and other factors relevant to discretion (Motion to Reopen at 8-15, 36-44). He also accuses the Immigration Judge of bias by “t[aking] over much of the questioning,” and claims that the Immigration Judge unfairly “orchestrated the consequence where [his] and his ex-wife’s cases would be heard [together].” (Id at 19, 51).2
 
The respondent further argues that reopening is warranted based on ineffective assistance of former counsel, and that the Board erred in finding that he failed to comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for raising such a claim on his direct appeal (Motion to Reopen at 29-36, 52-55). He accuses former counsel of (1) committing an ethical violation by discussing his case “ex parte” with the Immigration Judge and DHS counsel outside of his presence; (2) coaching him to lie at his hearing and then ridiculing him to the Immigration Judge; (3) failing to submit evidence pertaining to one of his United States citizen child’s Attention Deficit Disorder (ADD); and (4) refusing to make the respondent’s criminal attorney accountable for allowing him to plead guilty to a crime that, unbeknownst to him, would have adverse immigration consequences (Id. at 15-25, 27-39).
 
Ultimately, the respondent wishes to seek reopening to apply for cancellation of removal for certain permanent residents under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(a), as well as protection under the Convention Against Torture, 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16-1208.18.3
 
*2 To the extent the respondent seeks reconsideration of our last decision, he has failed to identify any error of fact or law in our decision that would warrant its reconsideration. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 56-58 (BIA 2006). We are satisfied that the Immigration Judge and the Board fully and properly reviewed the respondent’s case under the applicable legal precedents. While we acknowledged his numerous positive equities in our last decision, we remain persuaded that the respondent’s significant adverse factors - namely, his numerous false statements to the DHS and to the Immigration Court to obtain immigration benefits over a 15-year period, outweigh his positive equities (Board decision dated Jan. 9, 2018, at 2-3). The respondent does not merit discretionary relief.
 
Moreover, upon review of the record, we are unpersuaded that the Immigration Judge was biased against the respondent or otherwise conducted the hearing in a manner that did not comport with due process. See section 240(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(1) (giving immigration judges authority to ““interrogate, examine, and cross-examine the alien and any witnesses”); Rusu v. INS, 296 F.3d 316, 321-22 (4th Cir. 2002) (providing that alien must be ““accorded an opportunity to be heard at a meaningful time and in a meaningful manner, i.e., ... [to] receive a full and fair hearing on [her] claims”). The Immigration Judge’s conduct during the hearing fell short of displaying a ““deep-seated favoritism or antagonism that would make fair judgment impossible [.]” Liteky v. United States, 510 U.S. 540, 555 (1994).
 
We are also unpersuaded that reopening is warranted based on ineffective assistance of counsel. To prevail on such a claim, in addition to complying with certain procedural requirements, an alien must demonstrate that he was prejudiced as a result of his counsel’s ineffectiveness. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 640 (holding that alien must also show that she was prejudiced by counsel’s actions). The respondent has made no such showing here.
 
Initially, we are unpersuaded that the respondent’s former counsel committed any ethical violations by discussing the case with the Immigration Judge, in DHS counsel’s presence, at the conclusion of the proceedings. As an officer of the court, the respondent’s former counsel would have been subject to discipline for knowingly and with reckless disregard offering false evidence. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.102(c). The respondent continued to perjure himself throughout these proceedings, after the original fraud he committed before the DHS during his adjustment and naturalization processes (I.J. at 18; Tr. at 29.58. 222). Apparently, he perjured himself without former counsel’s urging or knowledge that he would do so (See Motion to Reopen. Attached E-Mail from Patrick Taurel dated Dec. 8. 2016. in which Taurel responded to the allegations against him). Once the respondent admitted to his continued perjury, we find it not only reasonable, but also incumbent upon former counsel, to have notified the Immigration Judge that he was unaware that his client was making false statements during his testimony. It is also not uncommon for an Immigration Judge to discuss an alien’s case with his counsel, along with DHS counsel, outside of the respondent’s physical presence.
 
*3 We also do not find that the respondent was prejudiced by former counsel’s failure to offer evidence of his child’s ADD. There is no indication that this evidence would likely have affected the outcome of these proceedings. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464. 473 (BIA 1992).
 
Moreover, the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s alleged failure to confront the respondent’s criminal counsel about any substantive or procedural defects of his plea in his criminal case. There is no indication that former counsel in these proceedings was retained for that purpose or agreed to assist the respondent in that regard. The respondent has also admitted in these proceedings that he committed the immigration fraud to which he ultimately pled guilty in his criminal proceedings.
 
As a final matter, we decline to reopen these proceedings to permit the respondent to pursue cancellation of removal for certain permanent residents or protection under the Convention Against Torture. The respondent has not shown his prima facie eligibility for either form of relief, such that reopening of these proceedings would be warranted.
 
Because the respondent committed fraud to procure adjustment of status, he is not an alien lawfully admitted for permanent residence for cancellation purposes. See 240A(a)(1) of the Act; see also Matter of Koloamatangi, 23 I&N Dec. 548, 551 (BIA 2003) (holding that an alien was ineligible for cancellation of removal for lawful permanent residents because he was “deemed, ab initio, never to have obtained lawful permanent resident status once his original ineligibility therefor [was] determined in [removal] proceedings”D’).
 
The respondent has also failed to make a prima facie showing that he will more likely than not face harm amounting to torture by or with the consent or acquiescence (including willful blindness) of a public official or other person acting in an official capacity, in the Yemeni government upon his repatriation. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16-1208.18. The respondent generally alleges that he will be tortured in Yemen because his brother and cousin were killed by Houthi rebels after the respondent informed United States immigration officials that Houthi rebels were attending his mosque (Petition For Convention Against Torture filed Feb. 9, 2018, Attached Form I-589 & Accompanying Affidavit). The respondent’s generalized and conclusory statements in this regard are insufficient, without more, to make out a prima facie showing that he is eligible for protection under the Convention Against Torture. We acknowledge the difficult country conditions in Yemen, but evidence of adverse country conditions, without more, is insufficient to show that the respondent himself may more likely than not be tortured upon his repatriation. See generally 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16-1208.18.
 
*4 In view of the foregoing, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent has also filed a separate “motion to expedite” wherein he complains about conditions in his current detention facility that are adversely impacting his state of health. However, the Board and the Immigration Judges have limited jurisdiction and can grant only those forms of relief from removal that are expressly authorized by Congress. See Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734 (BIA 1988). We do not have any authority to instruct the DHS how to operate their detention facilities. To the extent that the respondent may be seeking a bond determination, we note that bond proceedings are separate and apart from removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.19(d). Any request for bond would have to be made in a separate proceeding.

2

The respondent’s ex-spouse, Hana Al-Barati (AXXX XXX XXX), is presently in separate removal proceedings. However, she testified on his behalf in these proceedings.

3

The respondent also alleges that he is eligible for suspension of deportation under former section 244(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1254 (Brief in Support of Cancellation of Removal filed Feb. 16, 2018, at 9-13). However, the respondent is ineligible for suspension of deportation, which was abolished in 1996, because such relief is unavailable for aliens who, like the respondent, are in removal proceedings.

2018 WL 4002277 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AIWU CHEN
File: AXXX XX0 428 - Pompano Beach, FL
July 10, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Armineh Ebrahimian, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Evagelia Stella Solomos
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of China, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s February 7, 2018, decision denying his motion to continue and deeming his application for asylum abandoned and requests that the record be remanded to the Immigration Judge to file his application for asylum. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge properly deemed the respondent’s claim for asylum abandoned (IJ at 3-4). See Makir-Marwil v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 681 F.3d 1227, 1235 (11th Cir. 2012) (“If an application for relief is not filed by the deadline set by the IJ, that ground for relief is deemed waived.”); Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547, 549 (BIA 1992); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c). The Immigration Judge adequately informed the respondent of the opportunity to file for asylum and the consequences of failure to timely file the application (IJ at 3-5; Tr. at 3-4, 7). As further discussed below, the respondent did not establish a persuasive basis for not timely filing his application.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion for a continuance. An Immigration Judge “may grant a motion for continuance for good cause shown.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. The respondent sought a continuance to find a new attorney (Tr. at 8-9). The Immigration Judge noted that the Department of Homeland Security opposed a continuance (IJ at 5). Despite the respondent’s counsel’s failure to appear for the hearing, the respondent, through a Mandarin interpreter, was advised of the consequences of not filing his asylum application by February 7, 2018, and confirmed that he understood (Tr. at 3-4, 7).1 The respondent was also served with a hearing notice advising him of the same (IJ at 3-4; Jan. 10, 2018, Hearing Notice). The respondent appeared at his February 7, 2018, hearing without an application for relief. While the respondent claims he was unaware of his former counsel’s motion to withdraw, he indicated that he was looking for another attorney, and the Immigration Judge noted that a letter from his sponsor indicated the same (IJ at 4; Tr. at 7-8). Moreover, the respondent acknowledged his understanding of the consequences for failure to file an asylum application by February 7, 2018 (IJ at 3-5; Tr. at 3-4, 7). Under these circumstances, the respondent had not established that “good cause” existed to further continue these removal proceedings (Id). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807, 815 (BIA 2012) (recognizing that a continuance should not be granted where it is being sought “as a dilatory tactic to forestall the conclusion of removal proceedings”); see also Matter of Castro-Turn, 27 I&N Dec. 271, 287 (A.G. 2018) (noting regulatory authorization for continuances).
 
*2 Moreover, the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by the continuance denial, as he has not shown that he is prima facie eligible for asylum, and has not submitted an application for asylum on appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (a motion to reopen proceedings for the purpose of submitting an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documentation). Therefore, the respondent has not demonstrated that the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny him a continuance was in error or that he experienced prejudice as a result. See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 356-57 (BIA 1983) (noting that an alien must show that the denial of the continuance caused actual prejudice).
 
Likewise, the respondent’s request for a remand to apply for asylum is denied. Despite his claim of ineffective assistance of former counsel, the respondent does not persuasively explain why he could not timely filed his application before the Immigration Judge. Moreover, as noted above, he has not supported his request for a remand with his asylum application, and thus, he has not established prima facie eligibility for asylum. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c); see also INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104-05 (1988); Najjar v. Ashcroft, 257 F.3d 1262, 1303 (11th Cir. 2001); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). Therefore, we are unable to conclude whether any application for asylum is reasonably likely to succeed on the merits. See Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349, 357 (BIA 1998). Thus, the record will not be remanded.
 
Finally, to the extent the respondent asserts that his due process rights were violated in the proceedings below, we are unpersuaded. The respondent did not have a constitutionally protected liberty interest in the admission of his application for asylum after the court-ordered deadline had run. See Tang v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 578 F.3d 1270, 1276 (11th Cir. 2009). Further, the respondent has not established that he suffered prejudice such that the outcome of these proceedings may have been affected by the Immigration Judge’s decision. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We do not address the merits of the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel because the respondent did not comply with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005); Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1221-22 (11th Cir. 2003). We note that, despite the respondent’s claim of not receiving the Immigration Judge’s decision, the record shows that the respondent was served with the Immigration Judge’s summary order deeming the asylum application abandoned and ordering him removed to China, as well as being informed of his right to appeal the decision (Tr. at 10-12).

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZOILA YOJANA MARTINEZ-ZELAYA
File: AXXX XX0 313 - Dallas, TX
June 28, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Honduras, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 4, 2017, denying her motion to reopen removal proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security has not filed a response to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on May 12, 2014. On August 11, 2014, the respondent through her former counsel, Mr. Mario Caballero, filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge alleging that she did not receive notice of her May 12, 2014, hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion in a decision dated November 28, 2014, finding that the respondent’s failure to appear at the May 12, 2014, hearing was due to her failure to provide an updated address with the Immigration Court. On June 28, 2017, the respondent, through new counsel, filed a second motion to reopen alleging that her new motion was subject to equitable tolling based on exceptional circumstances in the form of Mr. Caballero’s ineffective assistance of counsel. The Immigration Judge denied the motion in a decision dated August 4, 2017, finding that while the respondent may have pursued her rights diligently, extraordinary circumstances were not present in this case because the respondent did not meet her burden of proof to show that Mr. Caballero’s representation was ineffective (IJ at 3).
 
On appeal, the respondent specifically contends that her former attorney, Mr. Caballero, provided ineffective assistance of counsel to her because he based the respondent’s first motion on an improper ground, i.e. a lack of notice claim (Respondent’s Br. at 4-5, 9-10). The respondent therefore argues that her second motion, filed more than 90 days after the entry of the final administrative order on May 12, 2014, should be equitably tolled based on extraordinary circumstances in the form of ineffective assistance of counsel (Respondent’s Br. at 7-10).
 
Generally, an alien may file one motion to reopen, which must be filed within 90 days after the entry of the final administrative decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). Under the doctrine of equitable tolling the 90-day filing deadline can be excused if the respondent establishes that: 1) she has been pursuing her rights diligently, and 2) some extraordinary circumstance stood in her way and prevented a timely filing. Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337, 344 (5th Cir. 2016).
 
*2 Ineffective assistance of counsel may constitute extraordinary circumstances. See, e.g., Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996). However, we agree with the Immigration Judge that equitable tolling is not warranted in this case because no extraordinary circumstances exist in the form of ineffective assistance of counsel (IJ at 3). The respondent did not fully comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), in order to reopen her proceedings based upon her claim of Mr. Caballero’s ineffective representation (IJ at 4-5). Specifically, the respondent’s affidavit did not set forth in detail the representation agreement she had with her former counsel (id.). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639 (requiring a motion to reopen to be “supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel...”) (emphasis added). The documents that the respondent alludes on appeal to do not attest in detail to the facts of the representation agreement at issue and only summarily explain that she retained Mr. Caballero to file a motion to reopen (Respondent’s Br. at 11-13; Respondent’s Second Motion to Reopen-Tabs E, J).
 
Furthermore, even if the respondent had met the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, to demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel in an immigration proceeding, the respondent must demonstrate “substantial prejudice” resulting from counsel’s performance. Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 n. 2 (5th Cir. 2001). Proving prejudice requires that the petitioner make a prima facie showing that, absent counsel’s deficient performance, she would have been entitled to the relief she sought. Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F.3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994). The respondent has not met her burden of showing that she was prejudiced by actions from his prior attorney.
 
Although the respondent contends that her prior attorney did not submit an application for withholding of removal with the respondent’s motion to reopen, the respondent has not shown that she was prima facie eligible for withholding of removal (Respondent’s Br. at 10). To that end, although the respondent submitted an application for withholding of removal with her Second motion to reopen, the application is blank in a number of places, does not indicate upon what basis she is seeking withholding of removal, and did not include an affidavit from the respondent setting forth the basis of her claim for relief (IJ at 6; Respondent’s Second Motion to Reopen-Tab K).
 
*3 The respondent contends that a reasonable attorney would not have based a motion to reopen on the respondent not having received notice of the May 12, 2014, hearing as it should have been based on exceptional circumstances or on the Immigration Judge’s power to reopen a case sua sponte (Respondent’s Br. at 12). However, the respondent does not advance what those exceptional circumstances would have been. The respondent did not appear for her May 12, 2014, hearing because she did not update her address with the Immigration Court, which led to her not receiving notice of the hearing (IJ at 5; Respondent’s Br. at 4, 10)). As the Immigration Judge noted, this does not constitute an exceptional circumstance and the respondent’s motion is untimely (IJ at 3, 5). Further, we agree with the Immigration Judge that based on the totality of the circumstances, the respondent had not presented a truly exceptional situation that would have warranted sua sponte reopening (IJ at 5-6). Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
We disagree with the respondent’s contention that the Immigration Judge conflated his legal analysis regarding equitable tolling with the procedural and substantive requirements of a Lozada claim (Respondent’s Br. at 8). The Immigration Judge separately considered whether the respondent had complied with the Lozada requirements, and then considered whether the respondent had made out a prima facie case for relief.
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Keith E. Hunsucker
FOR THE BOARD

APPLICATION: Administrative closure
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. This matter was last before us on October 14, 2016, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s April 24, 2015, decision denying her motion to administratively close proceedings; and reinstated the Immigration Judge’s grant of the privilege of voluntary departure. This case is presently before us pursuant to a July 27, 2017, order of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit granting a motion to remand.1 The respondent has filed a post-remand brief. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2018). We review de novo all other issues, including issues of law, judgment, or discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The motion to remand provided that the Board would “further consider its conclusion that the respondent’s argument regarding administrative closure was moot because the Ninth Circuit vacated the district court’s preliminary injunction in Puente Arizona v. Arpaio, 821 F.3d 1098 (9th Cir. 2016).” Specifically, the motion noted that since the Board’s October 14, 2016, decision in the respondent’s case, the district court declared that Arizona is preempted from using documents submitted solely as part of the federal employment verification process for purposes of investigating or prosecuting violations of certain Arizona statutes, including the one under which the respondent was charged and convicted. See Puente Arizona v. Arpaio, No. CV-14-01356-PHX-DGC, 2017 WL 1133012, at *16 n.7, 17 (D. Ariz. Mar. 27, 2017).
 
With her post-remand brief, the respondent submitted evidence that her conviction for criminal impersonation was vacated pursuant to Puente Arizona v. Arpaio, No. CV-14-01356-PHX-DGC, 2017 WL 1133012 (Respondent’s Br. at 4, Tabs F, G). The respondent asserts on appeal that she would not be in removal proceedings if it were not for the conviction that has since been vacated, and the Immigration Judge did not properly consider all relevant factors outlined in Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 2012), in denying her motion to administratively close her removal proceedings (Respondent’s Br. at 11-12).2
 
*2 Notably, Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. at 688, has been overruled since this case has been remanded, and we are without authority to grant administrative closure under the facts presented here (Respondent’s Br. at 11-12). See Matter of Castro-Turn, 27 I&N Dec. 271 (A.G. 2018). Furthermore, to the extent that the respondent seeks prosecutorial discretion, we observe that the record demonstrates that the DHS has declined to exercise prosecutorial discretion in the respondent’s case, and neither the Board nor the Immigration Judge has jurisdiction over issues of prosecutorial discretion. See Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 349-50 (BIA 1982).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the DHS. See section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event she fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, she shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c)(3)(iii), (e)(1)).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if she files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, she will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if she provides to the DHS such evidence of her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence the DHS deems sufficient that she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
*3 Anne J. Greer

APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Haiti, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of December 4, 2017, denying the respondent’s November 1, 2017, motion to reopen removal proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia on February 9, 2001. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a reply opposing the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent’s November 1, 2017, motion was the second one filed in these proceedings, it was filed over 16 years after she was ordered removed in absentia, and the Immigration Judge correctly found that the motion was time and numerically barred (IJ at 2). See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C) (providing that there is no deadline for rescinding an order entered in absentia where the issue is notice, and that an alien has 180 days to file a motion where the nonappearance was based on exceptional circumstances).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that she is eligible for equitable tolling of the time limits because she was misled by a person posing as a lawyer. However, the respondent has not shown that she complied with the requirements for bringing such a claim, as laid out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (1988). In particular, nothing in the record indicates that the respondent: (1) filed an affidavit setting forth the agreement that was entered into with this person with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations this individual did or did not make to her in this regard; (2) informed the former attorney or person of the allegations leveled against him/her; or (3) filed a complaint with any appropriate disciplinary authority. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1273 & n.6 (11th Cir. 2005) (explaining the Lozada requirements).
 
We find no merit to the respondent’s contention that since it was a non-attorney involved, she need not fulfill the Lozada requirements. See Montano Cisneros, 514 F.3d 1224, 1227 (11th Cir. 2008). Moreover, as the respondent raised the same issue of ineffective assistance of counsel in her October 29, 2001, motion and in his March 13, 2002, decision, the Immigration Judge found the respondent had not fulfilled the Lozada requirements, we agree with the DHS that the law of the case doctrine applies to the respondent’s repeated ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See United States v. Amedeo, 487 F.3d 823, 829 (11th Cir. 2007) (courts may readily dispose of repetitive, meritless claims that present the same claim already denied in a prior claim by applying the law of the case where appropriate).
 
*2 For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in her decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: YERICIA NICOL ORTIZ-NUNEZ
File: AXXX XX4 227 - Miami, FL
June 21, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Beresford A. Landers, Jr., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Angel L. Fleming
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s October 29, 2017, decision denying her motion to reconsider. The respondent sought reconsideration of the Immigration Judge’s October 2, 2017, decision denying her motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order dated August 24, 2017. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2). The Department of Homeland Security opposes the appeal. The appeal will be sustained.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On December 2, 2016, the respondent, 13 years of age at the time, appeared before the Immigration Judge with present counsel. At the conclusion of that hearing, the Immigration Judge rescheduled the respondent’s case for a later date and waived the respondent’s appearance at future proceedings so that she could attend school (Dec. 2, 2016, Hearing Tr.). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.25(a). Counsel’s presence was not waived. The respondent’s case was ultimately rescheduled for August 24, 2017 (Mar. 7, 2017, Notice of Hearing). The respondent’s counsel did not appear for this hearing, and the Immigration Judge entered an in absentia removal order. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i).
 
The respondent filed a motion to reopen on August 29, 2017, claiming that reopening is warranted based on “exceptional circumstances.” Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. Although not explicitly framed as such, the exceptional circumstance relied on by the respondent is ineffective assistance of counsel. See Montano Cisneros v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 514 F.3d 1224, 1226 (11th Cir. 2008) (providing that ineffective assistance of counsel may amount to an exceptional circumstance). The respondent states that the proceedings should be reopened and the removal order rescinded due to counsel’s failure to appear at the hearing (Respondent’s Br.), which counsel claims resulted from unforeseen travel delays on the date of the hearing.
 
In an October 2, 2017, decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, concluding, without additional explanation, “no exceptional circumstance for the respondent’s failure to appear.” (Emphasis added). On October 19, 2017, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider pointing out the fact that the respondent herself was not required to attend the hearing because her presence had been excused.1 See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.25(a). In an October 29, 2017, decision, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reconsider, explaining that “neither the respondent nor counsel were present” at the hearing and that “no exceptional circumstance” had been established. The present appeal ensued.
 
*2 A motion to reopen or reconsider based on ineffective assistance of counsel requires “(1) an affidavit . . . detail[ing] the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken . . ., (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities . . . and if not, why not.” Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 637 (BIA 1988). The United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit has found it reasonable to demand an alien claiming ineffective assistance of counsel to substantially comply with these procedural requirements. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005); Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222-23 (11th Cir. 2003).
 
The motion to reopen is supported by an affidavit from counsel, detailing the circumstances surrounding his failure to appear at the hearing. Furthermore, counsel’s failure to appear at the hearing, which amounts to deficient performance, is plain on the face of the administrative record. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir. 2000) (“[W]here ‘the facts are plain on the face of the administrative record, the requirements of Lozada are not dispositive.”D’ (citation omitted)), as quoted in Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d at 1222. There is no dispute that counsel missed a hearing he was scheduled to appear at and, in doing so, caused the respondent to be ordered removed in absentia at a hearing she herself was not required to attend. While we share in the dissent’s point that counsel should have self-reported his conduct to relevant bar authorities to satisfy the bar complaint requirement under Matter of Lozada, there is no indication of collusion between the minor respondent and counsel in this case. See Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599, 604 (BIA 1996) (noting that the purpose of the bar complaint requirement is to “protect against possible collusion between counsel and the alien client”). On this record, the purpose of Matter of Lozada is sufficiently satisfied.
 
The ineffectiveness of the respondent’s counsel in this case constitutes an exceptional circumstance and was the cause of the respondent’s failure to appear. The respondent, thus, met her burden of establishing that rescission of the in absentia order and reopening of proceedings were warranted.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The August 24, 2017, in absentia order is rescinded, this matter is reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.
 
Joan B Geller
FOR THE BOARD
DISSENTING OPINION: Garry D. Malphrus, Board Member
The issue addressed by the majority--whether counsel’s absence from the hearing constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel--was neither presented to nor considered by the Immigration Judge. As this argument was not advanced below, the proper procedure for raising a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel has not been followed. See generally Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 637 (BIA 1988). In particular, there is no indication that relevant bar authorities have been alerted of counsel’s conduct in missing the hearing. See id. (providing that a motion claiming ineffective assistance of counsel must “reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities . . . and if not, why not”).
 
This bar complaint requirement serves the important purpose of “protect[ing] against possible collusion between counsel and the alien client.” Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599, 604 (BIA 1996). I do not believe, even in jurisdictions that permit only substantial compliance with the requirements under Matter of Lozada, that an attorney should be permitted to bypass this requirement by simply admitting error. See Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222 (11th Cir. 2003) (providing that substantial compliance with the requirements under Matter of Lozada is permissible only “where their purpose is fully served by other means”).
 
For these reasons, I respectfully dissent. I would remand the record for the Immigration Judge to address the issue of ineffective assistance of counsel in the first instance under Matter of Lozada.
 
Footnotes

1

The DHS continues to represent on appeal, as it did in its opposition to the motion to reopen, that it “has no record of the respondent’s presence being waived” (DHS’s Br. at 3). The digital audio recording of the December 2, 2016, hearing clearly reflects that the respondent’s presence was waived.
