U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LAUDICEIA VENTURA DE SOUZA ALVES KELITA YALI DE SOUZA
Files: AXXX XX9 940 - Philadelphia, PA AXXX XX9 941
June 21, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Margaret W. Wong, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondents, natives and citizens of Brazil, were ordered removed from the United States in absentia on October 6, 2016, after not appearing at a hearing. They filed a motion to reopen on November 13, 2017, and appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated December 18, 2017, denying the motion. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondents argue that “exceptional circumstances” prevented their appearance at the hearing. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C) (providing that a motion to reopen based on “exceptional circumstances,” as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1), must be filed within 180 days of the removal order). The respondents filed their motion to reopen more than a year after the Immigration Judge ordered their removal from the United States in absentia, and it is therefore untimely.
 
In addition, we decline, as requested on appeal, to equitably toll the 180-day period to file the motion (Respondent’s Br. at 7). See Alzaarir v. Att’y. Gen, of U.S., 639 F.3d 86, 90 (3d Cir. 2011) (stating that an Immigration Judge may equitably toll the period to file a motion if ineffective assistance of counsel prevented the filing, and the alien has exercised due diligence for the entire period requested to be tolled). In the affidavit, the lead respondent, hereafter respondent, states that an unknown individual, a Portuguese speaker, and possibly an alien smuggler, advised her to remove her ankle bracelet and not attend her hearing because it would result in her removal to Brazil. He further advised her that she did not have the money to afford an attorney and her best option was not to appear at her hearing. The respondent states that she followed the individual’s instructions because she did not understand the American immigration system and was afraid to return to Brazil.
 
However, the respondent’s decision to rely on the individual’s advice is not tantamount to ineffective assistance of counsel especially after an immigration officer personally served her with the Notice to Appear and advised her in her native language, Portuguese, of the consequences of not appearing at a hearing (Exh. 1). In addition, the respondent has not established that she exercised due diligence during the entire period that she requests the Board to toll. Hence, we decline to equitably toll the 180-day period to file the motion and find it to be filed untimely.
 
*2 In addition, the respondents have not established an exceptional situation to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s and the Immigration Judge’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship). The respondent received the Notice to Appear, was advised by an immigration officer of the consequences of not attending a hearing, and made the calculated decision not to attend. Under these circumstances, we decline to reopen these proceedings sua sponte and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the motion.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Thomas G Snow
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: MARLON JOSE MORENO-SUNSIN
File: AXXX XX2 645 - Miami, FL
June 20, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Irving Joseph Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Evagelia Stella Solomos
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On January 9, 2008, an Immigration Judge entered an in absentia order of removal. On December 6, 2017, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. On January 8, 2018, an Immigration Judge denied the motion. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.1
 
In his motion to reopen, the respondent argued that he relied upon the advice of someone at his former attorney’s office that he should not appear at the January 2008 hearing (IJ at 2). The Immigration Judge found that the respondent had received notice of the hearing, but was arguing that he had failed to appear due to ineffective assistance of counsel (IJ at 3 & n.3). The respondent does not challenge this characterization of events on appeal. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent had not complied with any of the three requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) to establish ineffective assistance of counsel (IJ at 3). On appeal, the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge relied on the lack of a bar complaint in rejecting his ineffective assistance ot counsel claim (Respondent’s Br.).2
 
However, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent did not fulfill any of the three requirements, not only the third requirement relating to the filing of a bar complaint. The respondent does not address the other two requirements, i.e., that he provide an affidavit detailing the agreement entered into with counsel and that counsel be informed of the allegations against him and given an opportunity to respond. As the respondent does not identify any error in the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent did not comply with each part of Matter of Lozada, we conclude there is no reason to disturb that portion of the decision. The respondent’s motion is untimely and unexcused. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The respondent emphasizes his desire for sua sponte reopening. In 2013, the respondent married a United States citizen and he argues that he has an exceptional situation in that his wife has documented mental health problems (Respondent’s Br.). Moreover, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge should have reopened and administratively closed the proceedings so that the respondent could adjust his status through a waiver before the agency (Respondent’s Br.).
 
Administrative closure is no longer appropriate in most circumstances. Matter of Castro-Tum, 27 I&N Dec. 271 (A.G. 2018). Moreover, the couple married after the respondent was in removal proceedings and had an order entered against him, and thus this outcome cannot be said to be an extraordinary circumstance. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (Board’s discretion to reopen a case sua sponte is “an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”). For the above reasons, we decline to exercise our sua sponte authority. See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 107 (1988) (“[M]otions to reopen are disfavored in deportation proceedings . . . [because] [t]here is a strong public interest in bringing litigation to a close as promptly” as possible.).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Anne J. Greer
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: ASHABEN VISHAL PATEL

MOTION

Joanna Golding, Esquire

Luciana Dubuc

APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on October 15, 2015, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision on July 28, 2014, which denied her motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered removed in absentia on November 29, 2011. On February 8, 2018, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen and reconsider our prior decision. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) filed a response in opposition to the respondent’s motion to reopen. The motion to reopen will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely. The Board entered the final administrative order in these proceedings on October 15, 2015. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), with certain exceptions not pertinent here, a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion to reopen until February 8, 2018 (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen and Reconsider (Motion)). The respondent has not shown that an exceptional situation exists that would warrant the Board’s exercise of discretion to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
The respondent contends that she filed the instant motion to reopen because she identified substantial issues relating to proper notice and improper representation by prior counsel (Motion). The respondent asserts that she intends to pursue all possible relief and to supplement the motion with evidence of her eligibility for relief warranting reopening in this matter (Id.).
 
On October 15, 2015, the Board affirmed the Immigration Judge determination that the respondent received proper notice of her hearing held on November 29, 2011, and that she had neither alleged nor demonstrated ineffective assistance of counsel (Board Dec. at 1-2). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-40 (BIA 1988). The respondent’s instant motion raises the same issues that we considered and decided in our decision affirming the Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to reopen proceedings following the entry of in absentia order of removal (Id.; Motion). See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and (ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i), (ii).
 
*2 The respondent did not demonstrate any error in our prior decision (Motion). She did not establish that her failure to appear at her scheduled immigration hearing was due to ineffective assistance of counsel or that she was prejudiced by her prior counsel’s performance (Id.). In her motion, the respondent states that she intends to submit new evidence to support her motion and general contention that she is eligible for relief (Id.). However, she has only submitted one new document, a Form I-797, Notice of Action, dated February 22, 2016, which indicates that she filed a Form I-918 petition for U nonimmigrant status. She has not submitted any other applications for relief. The respondent has not established that she is currently eligible for any relief from removal. The respondent has not established she did not receive proper notice of her hearing in 2011 or that her failure to appear at the hearing was beyond her control. See Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337, 344 (5th Cir. 2016) (an alien must establish that he pursued his or her rights with “reasonable diligence” and that his failure to appear was beyond his control); Matter of J-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 33, 36 (BIA 1998) (even where the exercise of due diligence is not a statutory requirement for reopening an in absentia deportation order, an alien’s lack of diligence may be a “factor that undercuts” the proffered explanation for the alien’s failure to appear at a scheduled immigration hearing).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Roger Pauley

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IRVIN JOSIMAR URBINA-MORALES
File: AXXX XX9 928 - Atlanta, GA
June 7, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William Rounds, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of October 20, 2017, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia on February 7, 2017. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a response to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Contrary to the respondent’s appellate contention, notice on the respondent’s attorney constitutes notice to the respondent. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.26(c)(2), 1292.5(a); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien).
 
On appeal, the respondent maintains that he never received notice of his hearing from his former counsel. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), are satisfied. Here, however, the respondent in his motion to reopen did not allege ineffective assistance by his former counsel. To the extent the respondent now on appeal alleges ineffective assistance by former counsel, he has not complied with any of the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639. Specifically, the respondent has not submitted an affidavit describing the facts and his agreement with his former counsel, nor has he provided any evidence that his former counsel has been made aware of the allegations against him and given an opportunity to respond, either after or before he filed his brief to the Board, nor did he indicate that a disciplinary complaint had been filed against his prior attorney. Consequently, the respondent has shown no error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen.
 
We also agree with the Immigration Judge that no exceptional circumstances have been presented to warrant sua sponte reopening. The respondent has not explained why his previous counsel was unable to contact the respondent, he has conceded in his motion that he moved to California, and while he alleges he tried to call the Immigration Court, there is no evidence in the record that the respondent filed a written change of address with the Immigration Court.1 Pursuant to our holding in Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), the power to reopen sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations where enforcing them might result in hardship. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
*2 For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent is fully aware of the process because he filed a Form EOIR-33 in Immigration Court on November 20, 2013, informing the Immigration Judge that he had moved to Norcross, GA.

IN RE: LIVIU BURTEA MARIANA IANCU
Files: AXXX XX1 414 - Los Angeles, CA AXXX XX1 415
June 7, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Sanjay Sobti, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Andrea Siebert
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening, rescission
 
*1 This case was last before us on September 27, 2017, when we denied the respondents’ motion to reconsider the denial of their motion to reopen. On October 6, 2017, the respondents filed a motion to reopen and rescind claiming that they received ineffective assistance of counsel when filing their first motion to rescind the in absentia order entered against them.1 The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the respondents’ motion. The respondents’ motion to reopen and rescind will be denied.
 
The respondents are a husband and wife and are natives and citizens of Romania. An Immigration Judge ordered them removed in absentia on October 5, 2011, when they failed to appear for their removal hearing. The respondents now seek rescission of that order on the ground that they did not receive notice of their hearing.2 In support of their motion, the respondents have submitted a signed declaration explaining the lack of notice, why they did not pursue rescission before 2015, and the ineffective assistance they received from their former attorney (Respondents’ Motion, Exhibit A). The respondents also have provided copies of documents they received in Canada, asylum applications, proof that they have appeared for their check-ins with the DHS, copies of the change of address forms they sent to the Immigration Court, and copies of the letter they sent to their former attorney and the bar compliant they filed against her (Respondent’s Motion, Exhibits B-F).
 
A motion to rescind an in absentia order based on a claim of lack of notice may be filed at any time. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(ii). The respondents’ motion therefore is not untimely.
 
The motion, however, is the respondents’ second motion to rescind and therefore could be numerically barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) (stating that an alien may only file one motion to rescind an in absentia order). The respondents nevertheless claim they are entitled to equitable tolling of the numeric limit in light of the errors of their former attorney and their diligence in pursuing relief. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897-98 (9th Cir. 2003) (stating that the court recognizes equitable tolling of deadlines and numerical limits on motions to reopen or reconsider during periods when a petitioner is prevented from filing because of deception, fraud, or error, as long as the petitioner acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error). Even if we accept the respondents’ arguments regarding equitable tolling of the numeric limit, the respondents have not met their burden of establishing that rescission of the in absentia order is warranted due to lack of notice. We therefore will deny their motion.
 
*2 To obtain rescission of their in absentia order, the respondents must submit sufficient evidence to rebut the presumption that they received proper notice of their hearing. See Matter of C-R-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 677 (BIA 2008); Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 674-76 (BIA 2008); Sembiring v. Gonzales, 499 F.3d 981 (9th Cir. 2007) (discussing considerations in determining whether alien has overcome the weaker presumption of delivery when notice sent by regular mail). The record establishes, and the respondents do not dispute, that the respondents were served with the Notice to Appear in person (Exh. 1). The respondents also do not dispute that they received the notice for their original hearing scheduled in Harlingen, Texas, on August 2, 2011. The respondents, however, have submitted a signed statement explaining that they did not receive notification that their request for a change of venue to Los Angeles was granted or notice of their hearing in Los Angeles on October 5, 2011 (Respondent’s Motion, Exhibit A). The respondents state that they notified the Harlingen Immigration Court several times of their change in address, but they did not receive anything from the court. They indicate that they became concerned but could not afford to hire an attorney. The respondents claim that friends advised them to go to Canada to pursue asylum and that they took this advice and left the United States in late August or early September 2011 (Respondent’s Motion, Exhibit A). The respondents assert that they did not receive the notice regarding their hearing in Los Angeles before they left.
 
The documents in the record support the respondents’ statements. The record contains two change of address forms that the respondents filed with the Harlingen Immigration Court on January 10, 2011, and July 18, 2011 (Exhibits 2 and 4). The record also contains a handwritten note, filed on June 30, 2011, that appears to request a change of venue to Los Angeles, California. These filings indicate that the respondents were attempting to pursue their case in Immigration Court and support the respondents’ assertion that they were concerned because they did not receive any correspondence from the Immigration Court.
 
Nevertheless, the respondents have not adequately explained why, if they had not heard anything from the Immigration Court in Harlingen, they did not appear at their August 2, 2011, hearing in Harlingen or contact the court. Further, the respondents’ statements indicate that they could have been on their way to Canada when the notice of their October 5, 2011, hearing arrived by mail. The respondents’ failure to receive notice of their October 5, 2011, hearing therefore could have been their own fault. Cf. Vukmirovic v. Holder, 640 F.3d 977 (9th Cir. 2011) (finding no exceptional circumstances to excuse failure to appear at hearing when alien had not kept the government informed of his address); Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 674 (BIA 2008) (a respondent cannot evade delivery of a properly sent notice of hearing by relocating without providing the required change of address and then request reopening of the in absentia proceeding on the basis of a claim of lack of notice). Given these facts, we cannot conclude that the respondents have met their burden of establishing that rescission of the in absentia order entered against them is warranted, and we will deny their motion to rescind.3
 
*3 In addition, the respondents have not established that sua sponte reopening of their proceedings is appropriate. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen and their motion to rescind the in absentia order entered against them are denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondents also filed a response to the DHS’ opposition to their motion. We have considered the arguments raised both in their motion and their response in rendering this decision.

2

As part of their lack of notice claim, the respondents assert that they were not given oral notice of the consequences of failing to appear at their hearing in a language they understood. The statute, however, only requires that individuals receive written notice of the proceedings against them. See section 239(a)(1) and (2) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1) and (2). Whether an alien received proper oral notice of the consequences of failing to appear at a hearing is only relevant to a determination regarding the bars to certain forms of discretionary relief for failure to appear. See section 240(b)(7) of the Act. Accordingly, the respondents’ claimed lack of oral notice is not relevant to their motion to rescind the in absentia order. Further, their claim does not affect the validity of the Notices to Appear issued in their case. Finally, the Form I-213 prepared in their case contradicts their oral notice claim. The document states that all forms were explained to the respondents in Romanian (Exh. 7).

3

The evidence of record shows that the hearing notice and the Immigration Judge’s decision granting a change of venue were mailed to the respondents’ address of record. The respondents’ only evidence of non-receipt is a joint statement (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Exhibit A). Given the other circumstances of the respondents’ case, including their departure from the United States, this statement is not sufficient to establish that the respondents did not bear some responsibility for the lack of notice. Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. at 674.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

IN RE: YOLVER ESTRADA-MENDOZA YOLBER MENDOZA ESTRADA
File: AXXX XX9 566 - San Diego, CA
May 31, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Douglas D. Nelson, Esquire
 
AMENDED DECISION
 
*1 ORDER:
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 21, 2017, which found that he had abandoned his opportunity to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1), but granted his request for voluntary departure. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion denied.
 
The record shows that during the September 8, 2016, hearing the Immigration Judge explained to the respondent and counsel that he set a deadline of December 7, 2016, for the filing of the application (IJ at 1-2; Tr. at 31). During the next hearing, the Immigration Judge extended the deadline and advised the respondent and counsel that his application must be filed no later than March 17, 2017 (IJ at 1-2; Tr. at 39-40). In his June 21 2017, decision, the Immigration Judge observed that no application had been filed and deemed it abandoned (IJ at 1-4). For the reasons provided in the written decision, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination to deem the application abandoned (IJ at 1-4). 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1003.31(c). On appeal, the respondent, through counsel, concedes that the application was not filed with the Court, but explains that “counsel has committed a mistake,” and counsel seeks to take full responsibility for the failure to file (Respondent’s Brief at 5). To the extent the respondent is alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, we find no “substantial compliance” with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Castillo-Perez v. I.N.S., 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000), Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.1
 
Turning to the respondent’s motion to reopen, we construe it as a motion to remand because it was filed during the pendency of this appeal. Although the respondent has finally submitted a copy of the application with his motion, the motion is denied. This Board is an appellate body charged with reviewing determinations made by Immigration Judges. We discern no error by the Immigration Judge in deeming the application abandoned. Further, the respondent has not met his heavy burden or demonstrated prima facie eligibility for the relief sought. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472-73 (BIA 1992) (explaining that a party who seeks a remand or to reopen proceedings to pursue relief bears a “heavy burden” of proving that if proceedings before the Immigration Judge were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case).
 
*2 FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248 and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
*3 Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2018 WL 3416232 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARK MAITLAND A.K.A. MARK C. MAITLAND
File: AXXX XX2 019 - Orlando, FL
May 24, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sanjay Sethi, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated January 29, 2018, denying his motion to reopen. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent was ordered removed from the United States on June 8, 2005. His motion to reopen, filed on January 2, 2008, was untimely. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). While, on appeal, the respondent refers to ineffective assistance of counsel, he does not meaningfully argue that, between the entry of the order of removal and the filing of his motion, he exercised the requisite due diligence needed to equitably toll the applicable 90-day filing deadline. Avila-Santoyo v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 713 F.3d 1357, 1363 n.5 (11th Cir. 2013). To the extent that the respondent is seeking reopening in order to apply for asylum and related forms of relief, an alien who has been ordered removed cannot file a successive asylum application except as part of a timely and properly filed motion to reopen or one that claims that the late motion is excused because of changed country conditions. Chen v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 565 F.3d 805, 810 (11th Cir. 2009).
 
The respondent has not established changed country conditions in Jamaica should excuse the untimeliness of his motion. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i). In determining whether evidence accompanying a motion to reopen demonstrates a material change in country conditions that would justify reopening, we compare the evidence of country conditions submitted with the motion to those that existed at the time of the merits hearing below. Matter of S-Y-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 247, 253 (BIA 2007). However, the respondent did not present any remarkable evidence of country conditions in Jamaica with his motion to reopen. On appeal, the respondent, who claims that he is a homosexual, has presented numerous documents concerning the treatment of homosexuals in that country. The Department of State Country Report on Human Rights Practices which was previously presented to the Immigration Judge indicates that, at the time of his ultimate removal hearing, brutality against homosexuals in Jamaica was reportedly “widespread” (Exh. 5). While the evidence presented on appeal confirms that homosexuals are still targeted for violence in Jamaica, the evidence does not establish a change, as opposed to a continuation, of conditions since the time of his removal hearing on June 8, 2005.
 
*2 Finally, to the extent that the respondent’s arguments on appeal can be construed as a request for sua sponte reopening, such measure is not warranted. The respondent’s arguments on appeal, cumulatively considered, do not persuade this Board that an exceptional situation exists which would justify sua sponte reopening. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, as we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Thomas G Snow
FOR THE BOARD

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZOLTAN ZEMLENYI
File: AXXX XX7 854 - Los Angeles, CA
May 21, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Maria Janossy, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s December 28, 2017, decision that denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent reiterates that he demonstrated exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear based upon ineffective assistance of counsel. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “““exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), are satisfied. Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 596 (9th Cir. 2004) (considering an alien’s claim that ineffective assistance of counsel qualifies as an exceptional circumstance warranting rescission of an in absentia order); Monjaraz-Munoz v. INS, 337 F.3d 892 (9th Cir. 2003) (finding that alien established that his failure to appear was due to his reasonable and justifiable reliance on the advice of his attorney’s agent, such that alien was entitled to rescission of in absentia removal order on basis of exceptional circumstances). Upon our de novo review, we conclude that based upon the totality of the circumstances in this case the respondent has met the burden of demonstrating exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear and thereby his November 16, 2017, in absentia order of removal is rescinded. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i).
 
Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
*2 Edward F. Kelly

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KARLA MARINA QUIJANO-DE VASQUEZ
File: AXXX XX9 877 - San Antonio, TX
May 14, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lisette Cardo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Todd Keller
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
 
*1 The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s December 6, 2017, decision denying her motion to reopen the removal proceedings and to rescind an in absentia removal order issued on March 24, 2006. In conjunction with the appeal the respondent has filed documentary evidence relating to her asylum claim under section 208(a)(1)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1158(a)(1)(B), which we construe as a motion to remand. The Department of Homeland Security has filed a motion for summary affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s decision. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion to remand will be denied.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including credibility findings, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On November 10, 2005, the respondent was personally served with a Notice to Appear (“NTA”) ordering her to report for a hearing at the Immigration Court in San Antonio, Texas, at a date and time to be set, and she was advised in her native language, Spanish, of her obligation to inform the Immigration Court of her address (IJ at 1; Exh. 1 at 2). The respondent did not report an address. A hearing was scheduled at the San Antonio Immigration Court for March 24, 2006. The respondent did not appear for the hearing on March 24, 2006, and was ordered removed in absentia on that date. On July 22, 2014, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and change venue, which was denied. On November 16, 2017, the respondent filed a “Motion to Rescind [the] In Absentia Removal Order and to Reopen Removal Proceedings Pursuant to Sua Sponte Authority.” The respondent sought reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel, changed country conditions, and based on the Immigration Judge’s sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings.
 
An in absentia removal order may be rescinded only (1) upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances, or (2) upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice of the hearing or was in Federal or State custody and did not appear through no fault of the alien. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); see also Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722, 723 (BIA 1999). Exceptional circumstances include serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of an immediate relative of the alien, but not less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act. Under certain circumstances, the filing deadline for a motion to reopen may be subject to equitable tolling. See, e.g., Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337 (5th Cir. 2016).
 
*2 As the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent did not comply with the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). While we acknowledge that those requirements are not in every case mandatory, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not establish that the attorney whom she asserts rendered ineffective assistance of counsel in fact represented her at the relevant time (IJ at 4). Furthermore, she has not otherwise complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, in particular the requirement that prior counsel be informed of the asserted ineffective assistance. As such, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s arguments on appeal that the Immigration Judge should have granted equitable tolling of the time and motion deadlines on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. In any event, such ineffective assistance of counsel would only relate to the first motion, not to the respondent’s failure to appear at her scheduled hearing. We conclude, for the reasons given by the Immigration Judge, that equitable tolling on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel is not warranted in this case (IJ at 4-5).
 
We also agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not establish changed country conditions as a basis for reopening. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act. The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent had not submitted information regarding relevant conditions in El Salvador in 2006, and therefore did not provide a meaningful basis for comparison with conditions in 2017, when her most recent motion was filed (IJ at 6). Furthermore, she did not corroborate a significant aspect of the claimed change, namely that gang members were now targeting members of the church to which she belongs, the Episcopal Church (IJ at 6). In view of the foregoing, the respondent has not met her burden to establish a basis for reopening based on changed country conditions pursuant to section 240(C)(7)(c)(ii) of the Act; see generally Matter of S-Y-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 247 (BIA 2007).
 
Further, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s claim that the Immigration Judge erred in declining to reopen proceedings sua sponte. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not provided evidence of circumstances that would justify the extraordinary remedy of reopening her removal proceedings sua sponte. See, e.g., Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (holding that discretion to reopen a case sua sponte is “an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that sua sponte reopening is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations).
 
*3 The respondent has submitted evidence on appeal. Upon review of the evidence, we decline to remand these proceedings to the Immigration Judge to consider this evidence, as the evidence has not been shown to affect the outcome of this case. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
The following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is denied.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 3416269 (BIA)

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SIOKAPESI ANAUKIMOANA VILINGIA A.K.A. SIOKAPESI HOLA
File: AXXX XX1 044 - Phoenix, AZ
May 14, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Benjamin T. Wiesinger, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 16, 2017, denying her second motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent removed in absentia on May 5, 2010. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
As an initial matter, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and numerically barred, inasmuch as it was not submitted within 90 days of the Immigration Judge’s May 5, 2010, decision and is the respondent’s second motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b).
 
We are unpersuaded by the respondent’s argument that the numerical bar should be equitably tolled on the basis that she was prejudiced by ineffective assistance of counsel who filed her prior motion to reopen. Although the respondent complied with the procedural requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), she did not establish that her former attorney’s actions were actually prejudicial to her case. See id. at 640; see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent was present in Court when the Immigration Judge issued the May 3, 2010, filing deadline for any application for relief and informed the parties of the consequences of failing to meet the deadline. The respondent did not seek or identify voluntary departure as a form of relief, and when she failed to submit an application for cancellation of removal, the Immigration Judge properly deemed her application abandoned and issued a removal order on May 5, 2010. The respondent did not file her first motion to reopen until nearly seven years later. In the interim, she did not maintain contact with her attorney, assumed that her removal proceedings may have been administratively closed, and failed to inquire with the Court regarding the status of her case. Such actions were not reasonable and do not warrant tolling of the filing deadline for the seven year period until her first motion to reopen was filed in May 2017. Regardless of any deficiencies in the respondent’s first motions, they were untimely filed. The respondent has not demonstrated that any of her motions to reopen should have been equitably tolled, as she has not established that the outcome of her proceedings may have been affected by the ineffective assistance she allegedly received. Thus, the respondent did not establish actual prejudice due to the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785, 793-94 (9th Cir. 2005). In light of the foregoing, we conclude that a remand for further consideration of this issue is not warranted.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 3416267 (BIA)

2018 WL 3416272 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIO SALVADOR SORIANO-MENDOSA
File: AXXX XX9 818 - Salt Lake City, UT
May 11, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jesus E. Saucedo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Saida Ulle
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals the Immigration Judge’s July 11, 2017, decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal. A motion to reconsider filed by the respondent with the Immigration Judge prior to the respondent’s filing of this appeal has been forwarded with the record for our consideration.1 The Department of Homeland Security’s (“DHS”) motion to accept its late-filed brief is granted. The respondent filed a request for a stay of removal with the Board. The appeal will be dismissed, the respondent’s motion will be denied, and the request for a stay of removal will be denied.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
An order of removal entered in absentia may only be rescinded under the following conditions: 1) if filed within 180 days after the date of entry of the final order of removal, if failure to appear was based on exceptional circumstances; or 2) if filed at any time if the alien did not receive notice. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i), (ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § Conn. Gen. Stat. §§ 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i), (ii). On appeal, the respondent argues that exceptional circumstances, namely, ineffective assistance of counsel, justify reopening his removal proceedings (Respondent’s Br. at 2, December 29, 2017). Alternatively, the respondent requests that proceedings be reopened sua sponte to allow the respondent to seek adjustment of status through his U.S. citizen wife (Respondent’s Br. at 2, December 29, 2017).
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen for the reasons stated therein (IJ at 4-6). Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872 (BIA 1994). While the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent has not demonstrated that equitable tolling applies, as he has not shown due diligence in pursuing his claim (IJ at 4). Moreover, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent has not demonstrated exceptional circumstances, as he has not met the substantive requirements of an ineffective assistance of counsel claim (IJ at 4-5). Finally, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent has not shown prejudice, as he did not demonstrate that counsel’s actions foreclosed any available relief (IJ at 5). We address the following in light of the arguments raised by the respondent on appeal.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in holding that he did not exercise due diligence in seeking to reopen his case (Respondent’s Br. at 16-20, December 29, 2017; Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider at 13-15). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not establish that he exercised due diligence in seeking reopening, where the respondent sought reopening over 5 1/2 years after being ordered removed in absentia. See Mahamat v. Gonzales, 430 F.3d 1281, 1283 (10th Cir. 2005) (an alien must also show that he exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll); Riley v. INS, 310 F.3d 1253, 1258 (10th Cir. 2002) (ineffective assistance of counsel may toll the filing period for a motion to reopen). The respondent was ordered removed on September 29, 2011, and his motion to reopen was filed on May 24, 2017 (IJ at 4). The respondent admits that he was aware that he missed his hearing date almost immediately, as his attorney called him on the day of the hearing from court to ask him when he would arrive (IJ at 4; Respondent’s Motion, tab B at 6). The respondent states that he met with attorneys in July 2012, February 2013, April 2013, summer 2013, April 2014, and at some point after that, the respondent obtained current counsel (IJ at 4; Respondent’s Motion, tab B at 6-7). In his motion, the respondent states that he met with current counsel in 2014, filed a Freedom of Information Act request, and it was not determined that the respondent had a potential ineffective assistance of counsel claim until March 2017, 2 months prior to filing a motion to reopen (IJ at 4; Respondent’s Motion at 6).
 
However, in his affidavit, the respondent states that as early as May 2011, his attorney’s “lack of competence” was becoming an issue (Respondent’s Motion, tab B at 5). Despite this, the respondent states that he was not aware that his prior attorney had done anything wrong until meeting with current counsel (Respondent’s Motion, tab B at 7). As noted by the Immigration Judge, merely consulting with attorneys, many of whom told the respondent that they would assist him in stopping the execution of his removal order, without further pursuing legal assistance to reopen his case, is not sufficient to demonstrate that he had been diligent during the entire period he seeks to toll (IJ at 4; Respondent’s Motion, tab B at 6-7). Moreover, even assuming the respondent was not aware of the possibility of ineffective assistance of counsel until meeting with current counsel, without further explanation, the respondent has not established due diligence when it took nearly 3 years to file a motion to reopen after apparently retaining counsel (IJ at 4; Respondent’s Motion, tab B at 7). Thus, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated that he acted with due diligence in pursuing his alleged ineffective assistance of counsel claim.
 
*3 On appeal, the respondent further argues that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that he did not meet his burden to establish exceptional circumstances, as he has not shown that counsel’s assistance was ineffective (Respondent’s Br. at 6-8, February 9, 2018; Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider at 11-12). Despite the respondent’s assertions, even assuming that the respondent has met the due diligence requirement, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not shown that ineffective assistance of counsel caused him to miss his hearing (IJ at 4-5). As noted by the Immigration Judge, the record reflects that the respondent was in court when notice of the September 29, 2011, hearing was provided to him and to his attorney in person (IJ at 4). See 8 C.F.R. § 292.5(a) (providing that whenever an alien is required to be given notice, such notice shall be given to attorney of record); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 259 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to the attorney of record constitutes notice to the alien). In light of this, the respondent was provided proper notice in accordance with section 239(a)(2) of the Act. See section 239(a)(2) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.13, 1003.18. However, the respondent argues that even if notice was proper for his September 29, 2011, hearing before the Immigration Judge, prior counsel did not exercise sufficient professional competence to ensure the respondent’s appearance for his hearing, which caused him to miss his hearing (Respondent’s Br. at 6, February 9, 2018).
 
We are not persuaded by the respondent’s arguments on appeal. Despite receiving proper notice of his September 29, 2011, hearing on May 26, 2011, the respondent stated that he contacted former counsel on September 5, 2011, asking him to keep him informed as to any upcoming hearing dates (IJ at 4-5; Respondent’s Motion, tab B at 5). The respondent further states that he never heard back from counsel as to when his next hearing was (IJ at 4-5; Respondent’s Motion, tab B at 5-6). As such, the respondent’s argument is essentially that he failed to receive proper notice from his attorney as to his hearing date and therefore, he failed to appear. However, as noted above, the respondent was in court when notice of the September 29, 2011, hearing was provided to him in person (IJ at 4). As such, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent was provided proper notice in accordance with section 239(a)(2) of the Act, and that any failure of counsel to remind him of the scheduled hearing does not rise to the level of ineffective assistance (IJ at 4). Therefore, even assuming that the respondent has demonstrated due diligence, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not meet his burden to establish exceptional circumstances that justify his failure to appear.
 
*4 On appeal, the respondent further argues that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that the respondent has not demonstrated he was prejudiced by prior counsel’s actions (IJ at 5; Respondent’s Br. at 14-16, December 29, 2017; Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider at 10-12, 16-18). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not shown that his prior counsel’s allegedly deficient performance caused him prejudice, as the record does not demonstrate that prior counsel failed to file an application for relief on the respondent’s behalf at the time of the respondent’s removal hearing. See Ochieng v. Mukasey, 520 F.3d 1110, 1115 (10th Cir. 2008) (alien must show that counsel’s ineffective assistance prejudiced him); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556 (BIA 2003) (“an alien alleging ineffective assistance of counsel must also show that he or she was prejudiced by the actions or inactions of counsel”). Although the respondent argues that his former attorney failed to file an asylum application on his behalf, as noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent does not describe in his affidavit that he had any conversations with counsel regarding the filing of an asylum application, nor has he provided any evidence to establish his eligibility for asylum and related relief (IJ at 5; Respondent’s Br. at 15). In fact, when the Immigration Judge asked if the respondent would be filing an asylum application, counsel stated that he had filed an I-360 and I-485 for Special Immigrant Juvenile Status (“SIJS”), as he believed this to be a better avenue for relief (IJ at 5).
 
In addition, the respondent argues that former counsel failed to respond to a Request for Evidence (“RFE”) from U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”), which resulted in the denial of his I-360 (IJ at 5; Respondent’s Br. at 14-15; Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider at 12). As noted by the Immigration Judge, the record indicates that while his first I-360 was denied for failure to respond to an RFE, USCIS later reopened the application and allowed the respondent another opportunity to reply to the RFE (IJ at 5). Although the respondent claims that his attorney never responded to the second RFE and that the application was therefore denied, the respondent did not address this in his affidavit and did not submit evidence to corroborate this claim (IJ at 5; Respondent’s Br. at 15). In fact, the evidence submitted by the respondent for the first time on appeal pertaining to this claim demonstrates that the I-360 had been pending since it was reopened in April 2011 and was recently denied by USCIS on August 24, 2017, as the respondent is now over 21 years old and married and therefore ineligible under section 101(a)(27)(J) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(27)(J) (Respondent’s Br., tab E at 39). Thus, for the foregoing reasons, the respondent has not demonstrated the requisite prejudice to support his claim, as he has not demonstrated that any alleged deficiencies of his former counsel affected the outcome of proceedings (IJ at 5). See Ochieng v. Mukasey, 520 F.3d at 1115.
 
*5 The Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent did not demonstrate exceptional circumstances sufficient to justify exercising his discretionary authority to reopen the respondent’s removal proceedings sua sponte, and we decline to disturb his determination (IJ at 6; Respondent’s Br. at 8-11, February 9, 2018; Respondent’s Br. at 23-25, December 29, 2017; Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider at 18-21). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1); see also Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (stating that “as a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”).
 
To the extent that the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge did not consider the entire record in rendering his decision, we observe that an Immigration Judge is not required to discuss every piece of evidence of record in a decision (Respondent’s Br. at 21-22, December 29, 2017). Moreover, we have held that an Immigration Judge may make reasonable inferences from direct and circumstantial evidence in the record and is not required to interpret the evidence in the manner advocated by the respondent. See Matter of D-R-, 25 I&N Dec. 445, 454-55 (BIA 2011), clarified on other grounds by Matter of D-R-, 27 I&N Dec. 105 (BIA 2017).
 
In his brief, the respondent has requested that we reissue the Immigration Judge’s September 29, 2011, decision in the interests of justice and to cure the errors made by prior counsel (Respondent’s Br. at 23, December 29, 2017). To the extent that the respondent’s request constitutes a request to reopen his removal proceedings and rescind his in absentia order, the request will be denied for the reasons discussed above.
 
The respondent submitted additional documents with his appellate brief and in a supplemental motion (Respondent’s Br., tabs A-H, February 9, 2018; Respondent’s Br., tabs A-U, December 29, 2017; Supplement in Support of Motion to Reopen, April 27, 2018). The Board does not consider new evidence presented for the first time on appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv) (limiting the Board’s fact finding authority); see also Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 73-74 and n.10 (BIA 1984). Furthermore, even assuming the evidence was new and previously unavailable, to the extent that the request to consider these documents constitutes a motion to remand, the evidence submitted does not warrant a remand for further proceedings because it would not alter the outcome of this case. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
*6 Finally, we note that the respondent requested reopening to pursue adjustment of status in conjunction with an I-601A provisional unlawful presence waiver (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 2). Effective August 29, 2016, there are new rules regarding provisional unlawful presence waivers. See Expansion of Provisional Unlawful Presence Waivers of Inadmissibility, 81 Fed. Reg. 50,244, 50,255-56, 50, 262 (July 29, 2016). The DHS has amended the rule to allow individuals with final removal orders to apply for provisional unlawful presence waivers if they have filed an application seeking consent to reapply for admission and such an application has been conditionally approved by the USCIS. See 81 Fed. Reg. 50,244, 50,275-76 (to be codified at 8 C.F.R. § 212.7(e)(4)(iv)). Thus, the respondent does not need to have his removal proceedings reopened to be able to apply for a provisional unlawful presence waiver with the USCIS. The respondent may request an administrative stay of removal from the DHS while any waiver or provisional waiver applications are pending before the USCIS. 8 C.F.R. § 241.6(a).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
Joan B Geller
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent filed a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Court on August 10, 2017 (Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider). However, the respondent perfected a timely appeal by filing a Notice of Appeal with this Board on August 10, 2017, prior to the Immigration Judge issuing a decision on the pending motion (Respondent’s Notice of Appeal). Accordingly, jurisdiction vested with the Board and the Immigration Judge was divested of jurisdiction over the motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of H-, 20 I&N Dec. 611, 612 n.1 (BIA 1992). That motion is now before us as a motion to remand, and the arguments therein are incorporated into the appeal and addressed in this decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4); BIA Practice Manual Rule 4.2(a)(ii).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RIGOBERTO HERNANDEZ-PEREZ
File: AXXX XX6 443 - Tacoma, WA
May 9, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrew Joseph Oliver, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jodilyn A. Erikson-Muldrew
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 19, 2017, denying his motion to reopen his removal proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the appeal. The record will be remanded.
 
We review the findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge, including the determination of credibility, for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including questions of judgment, discretion, and law, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent, who was previously represented by attorney Kaaren Barr, was granted pre-conclusion voluntary departure for a period of 120 days on April 24, 2008 (IJ at 1). See section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a). On April 29, 2008, the respondent, still represented by Ms. Barr, filed a motion to reopen proceedings to pursue an application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b), noting that Ms. Barr had mistakenly thought that the respondent did not have the requisite years of continuous physical presence to qualify for relief (IJ at 2). On April 30, 2008, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, finding that the respondent’s motion was “not supported by changed circumstances” because his qualifying relatives, presence in the United States, and lack of disqualifying crimes were known at the time the respondent accepted pre-conclusion voluntary departure (IJ at 2). Ms. Barr filed a motion to reconsider on May 28, 2008, which was denied that same day. Ms. Barr then filed an untimely appeal of the denial of the motion to this Board, which dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction on July 15, 2008 (IJ at 2). Ms. Barr was later disbarred, effective March 9, 2012.
 
The respondent departed the United States on or about August 21, 2008, and reentered without being admitted or paroled 5 years later (IJ at 2; Respondent’s Br. at 5). On October 5, 2017, he filed a motion to reopen to apply for cancellation of removal, arguing that his prior attorney provided him with ineffective assistance of counsel, including by failing to recognize that he was eligible for this form of relief prior to accepting pre-conclusion voluntary departure, by failing to set forth clearly a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel in the prior motion to reopen, and by appealing the denial of the prior motion in an untimely fashion. The respondent argued that the time and number limitations that ordinarily apply to motions to reopen should be equitably tolled; alternatively, he argued that proceedings should be reopened sua sponte (Respondent’s Mot. at 9). The DHS opposed the motion to reopen, arguing that the Immigration Judge did not have jurisdiction in reinstatement proceedings to reopen “the 2007 expedited order of removal” against the respondent (DHS’s Opposition to Motion to Reopen Removal Proceedings at 3).1
 
*2 On October 19, 2017, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, noting that the respondent’s motion was his second motion to reopen proceedings and that it was untimely (IJ at 2). The Immigration Judge also found that because the respondent departed the United States in 2008, he did not have the required 10 years of continuous physical presence for cancellation of removal (IJ at 2). See section 240A(b)(1)(A) of the Act. The respondent timely appealed.
 
Regarding the respondent’s eligibility for cancellation of removal, we note that the period of continuous physical presence was deemed to end upon the service of the Notice to Appear, in this case March 26, 2008 (Exh. 1). See section 240A(d)(1) of the Act. Assuming that the respondent can demonstrate that he was continuously physically present for 10 years prior to this date, he is not precluded from relief. Further, while the Immigration Judge noted that the respondent had filed a prior motion to reopen and that the 2017 motion was untimely, the Ninth Circuit has held that the time and numerical limitations on motions to reopen are amenable to equitable tolling due to ineffective assistance of counsel. See Salazar-Gonzalez v. Lynch, 798 F.3d 917, 920 (9th Cir. 2015); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1188 (9th Cir. 2001) (en banc). Equitable tolling is available “when a petitioner is prevented from filing because of deception, fraud, or error, as long as the petitioner acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error.” Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
Here, the Immigration Judge made no findings regarding whether ineffective assistance of counsel warranted equitable tolling and whether the respondent acted with the requisite diligence in discovering the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. Additionally, the Immigration Judge did not consider whether exceptional circumstances exist to warrant reopening sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, we will remand for additional fact finding and analysis in this regard. On remand, the parties may introduce any evidence relevant to these proceedings.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The DHS reiterates this argument on appeal (DHS’s Br. at 3). However, the record does not contain evidence indicating that the respondent has an expedited order of removal from 2007 or that the DHS has actually taken steps to reinstate a prior removal order. Additionally, the respondent is not attempting to reopen a 2007 expedited order of removal, but instead the removal proceedings that resulted in an order of voluntary departure on April 24, 2008.

IN RE: FILIMONE LIKILIKI AFU A.K.A. FILIMONE L. AFU A.K.A. FILIMONE AFU A.K.A. FILEMONE AFU

May 9, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Adam L. Crayk, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening; stay of removal
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on November 30, 2017, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s April 14, 2017,1 decision denying his motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed from the United States in absentia. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider and reopen based on the ineffective assistance of his former counsel, and a request for a stay of removal. Essentially, the respondent alleges that he was never notified of the hearing date because his former counsel’s office erroneously sent an electronic copy of the notice to an incorrect e-mail address (Motion to Reconsider and Remand at 1, 4-5, Exh. 1). The record before us does not contain a brief from the Department of Homeland Security opposing the instant motion.2 The motion will be granted and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
The respondent’s motion and supporting evidence convince us that reconsideration and reopening are warranted in this case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a)-(b)(1), (c)(1). In our prior decision, we concluded that the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen because he had not filed a bar complaint against his former counsel pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988);3 and declined to consider evidence submitted on appeal. Given the circumstances and evidence presented in this case, including more recent evidence that the Utah State Bar has received and is investigating the respondent’s bar complaint against his former counsel (Motion to Reconsider and Remand, Exhs. 1-2), we will grant the motion, vacate our prior decision, and remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision. On remand, the parties may submit additional arguments and evidence pertinent to this case. We express no opinion on the outcome of this case. Accordingly, the following orders are entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider and reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s November 30, 2017, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s April 14, 2017, and March 23, 2017, decisions are vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDEER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision, and for the issuance of a new decision.
 
*2 FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

Although the Immigration Judge’s written decision is dated April 12, 2017, it was not actually mailed to the respondent until April 14, 2017.

3

Under Matter of Lozada a motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires the following evidence: (1) an affidavit describing in detail the agreement with former counsel, (2) proof that former counsel was informed of the allegations and had an opportunity to respond, and (3) a statement regarding whether a complaint was filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and, if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639. See also Infanzon v. Ashcroft, 386 F.3d 1359, 1363 (10th Cir. 2004) (emphasizing the necessity of complying with the Lozada requirements).

Vina Maria Fleisig, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on February 21, 2018, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s September 7, 2017, decision to deny his motion to reopen an order of removal entered in absentia. The respondent has now submitted an untimely motion to reopen proceedings on March 23, 2018. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion, which will be granted.
 
With his motion to reopen, the respondent alleges that his former attorney’s law firm, whom he hired in 2005 and who represented him during the time his 2009 Notice to Appear and subsequent in absentia removal order were issued, failed to inform him that they had not filed his Form I-751 in a timely fashion in 2008.1 In prior pleadings before the Immigration Judge and the Board, the respondent was represented by this prior law firm, and his requests for reopening were based on, inter alia, the failure to identify the name of the attorney who was supposed to file the Form I-751 (August 24, 2017 IJ at 1; September 7, 2017 IJ at 1), and the failure to file a proper claim pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).2
 
The respondent, with the assistance of current counsel, has now filed an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his prior law firm, and has included, inter alia, an affidavit and a copy of a complaint filed with the First Judicial Department, Attorney Grievance Committee, New York, New York, which outlines the respondent’s complaints. Given the evidence of record, the respondent’s proffered motion evidence, the arguments set forth in his current and prior motions, and the lack of DHS opposition, we will reopen the proceedings sua sponte to allow the respondent to present his arguments and evidence regarding his removability and, if necessary, his applications for relief from removal. The following order will, thus, be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is granted, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.
