2018 WL 3045812 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DORA NANCY LOPEZ QUINTERO
File: AXXX XX4 237 - Newark, NJ
May 8, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sumaira Khan, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Colombia, has appealed the September 15, 2017, decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia for her failure to appear for the hearing on April 21, 2003. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that she did not receive actual notice of the hearing. She claims that the notice of hearing was improperly sent to her friend, Mr. Hoyos, who paid the respondent’s bond. The respondent states that she is now eligible for apply for adjustment of status.
 
An alien may not be entitled to receive notice of his hearing if he does not provide an address at which he may be contacted during proceedings. See section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(B) (eliminating the notice requirement where the alien fails to provide the required contact information); see also Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 674-75 (BIA 2008) (stating that an alien must comply with the statutory responsibility to provide current address information).
 
Upon de novo review, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the motion to reopen. The record reflects that the Notice to Appear (NTA) was served personally on the respondent on July 6, 2001, and it contains the respondent’s signature (Exh. 1). The NTA advised the respondent of her obligation to maintain a current mailing address with the Immigration Court and of the consequences of failing to appear for any scheduled hearings. The NTA reflects that the respondent received oral notice in the Spanish language of the consequences of failing to appear for a hearing. The NTA informed the respondent that she had been placed in removal proceedings, and it provided information regarding the nature of the proceedings, including the opportunity to seek representation, and the conduct of the hearing. Current law does not require that the NTA be in any language other than English. See section 239 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229.
 
We note that on September 28, 2001, the respondent’s former attorney, Gino M. Mesa, filed a Form G-28 and a motion to change venue with the Houston Immigration Court, both of which set forth the respondent’s address on Cook Avenue, in Madison, New Jersey. On October 10, 2001, the change of venue to the Newark Immigration Court was granted. Mr. Mesa filed a motion to withdraw as attorney of record with the Newark Immigration Court on November 20, 2001, and again listed the respondent’s address on Cook Avenue, in Madison, New Jersey. The Newark Immigration Court initially sent notices of hearing to Mr. Mesa. However, on September 23, 2002, December 24, 2002, December 31, 2002, and February 4, 2003, the Newark Immigration Court sent notices of hearing to the respondent at the Cook Avenue address in Madison, New Jersey. The envelope containing the February 4, 2003, hearing notice was returned to the Immigration Court as “not deliverable as addressed - unable to forward,” and is in the record of proceedings. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on April 21, 2003, due to her failure to appear for the hearing.
 
*2 The respondent contends that the notice of hearing should have been sent to her at the address that appears on the Form I-213, dated June 29, 2001. However, the notice of hearing was sent to the respondent at the most recent address provided by the respondent’s former attorney. The respondent has not provided any evidence about where she actually lived, and she never provided a change of address to the Immigration Court in the 16 years since she was served with the NTA, as she was instructed to do. While the respondent contends that the Cook Avenue address belonged to a friend who posted bond, the respondent did not update her address with the Immigration Court, nor has she alleged ineffective assistance of counsel for her failure to appear. See Ramos-Olivieri v. Att’y Gen., 624 F.3d 622 (3d Cir. 2010) (court held that Board acted within its discretion denying motion to reopen since an evidentiary hearing on receipt of notice not warranted when alien both fails to arrange for a responsible person who still resides at the alien’s former address to forward all mail he receives and the alien fails to notify immigration authorities of a change in address); see also Santana Gonzalez v. Att’y Gen., 506 F.3d 274 (3d Cir. 2007); (holding that a weaker presumption of receipt applies when a notice of hearing is sent by regular mail); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Consequently, the Immigration Judge correctly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 3045812 (BIA)

2018 WL 3045829 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE GABRIEL FIGUEROA RODRIGUEZ A.K.A. JOE FIGUEROA
File: AXXX XX5 507 - Napanoch, NJ
April 26, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Disha Chandiramani, Esquire
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated December 18, 2017, finding him ineligible from relief from removal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent was charged with being subject to removal under sections 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), (A)(iii), and (B)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii), A(iii), and (B)(i), as an alien who was convicted of a violation of a crime involving moral turpitude, a violation controlled substance and an aggravated felony, respectively. The Immigration Judge sustained the charge of removability and found the respondent ineligible for any relief from removal (IJ at 2). We agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision which has not been meaningfully challenged on appeal. The respondent is barred from cancellation of removal, as this form of relief is not available to lawful permanent resident aliens who, like the respondent, have been convicted of aggravated felonies. See section 240A(a)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229b(a)(3). The respondent to be statutorily ineligible for asylum and withholding of removal due to his aggravated felony conviction for a particularly serious crime for which he received a sentence of at least 5 years (IJ at 2). See sections 208(b)(2)(B)(i), 241(b)(3)(B)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.13(c), 1208.16(d)(2). The respondent did not apply for deferral of removal under the Convention Against Torture (IJ at 2; Tr. at 13). With regard to the relief of adjustment of status, under section 245 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255, the respondent has not shown that he is currently eligible for adjustment of status. There is no evidence that the respondent has an approved visa petition, or that a visa petition has ever been filed on his behalf.
 
The respondent claims on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying him a continuance to collaterally attack his conviction pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010) (Tr. at 6-7, 13-16).1 Though the respondent has filed a post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction, it does not render the conviction non-final. We note that the respondent’s conviction became final for immigration purposes when the respondent failed to appeal his conviction, allowed the appeal period to lapse, waived his right to a direct appeal, or exhausted the direct appeal of his conviction. See section 101(a)(48)(A) of Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(48)(A); Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009). We point out that this Board and the federal courts have consistently held that an alien may not collaterally attack the legitimacy of an otherwise valid state or federal criminal conviction in immigration proceedings. See Matter of Ponce De Leon Ruiz, 21 I&N Dec. 154 (BIA 1996, 1997; A.G. 1997); Matter of Gabryelsky, 20 I&N Dec. 750 (BIA 1993); Matter of Fortis, 14 I&N Dec. 576, 577 (BIA 1974).2
 
*2 The respondent also asserts ineffective assistance of counsel on appeal. We find that respondent did not meet the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The instant claim of ineffective assistance does not comply with Lozada’s requirement that the claim be supported by an affidavit by the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement between himself and his legal representative and what actions were to be taken on his behalf. Furthermore, the respondent has not established that he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. at 561-62. This means that it is likely that the respondent would have prevailed at the hearing had the negligent representation not occurred. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in his deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates his right to counsel.

2

The respondent has not filed a motion to remand with evidence that his efforts to secure post-conviction relief have been successful.

2018 WL 3045833 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMMAD ASSIF ASSEFI A.K.A. MOHAMMED ASSIF ASSEFI
File: AXXX XX3 779 - Los Angeles, CA
April 25, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ayda Rashidian, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent timely appeals from the Immigration Judge’s November 11, 2017, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings based upon ineffective assistance of counsel he received during his hearing on June 26, 2012. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed a brief on appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On June 26, 2012, the respondent’s counsel withdrew the respondent’s application for adjustment of status and declined to apply for any other forms of relief. The respondent was then ordered removed. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on October 20, 2017. In that motion, the respondent sought reopening based upon his prior counsel’s ineffective assistance. The respondent sought equitable tolling of the time and numerical limitations for motions to reopen. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent substantially complied with the procedural requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), but that reopening was not warranted because the respondent did not exercise due diligence in filing his motion to reopen and did not establish prima facie eligibility for relief, i.e., withholding of removal and protection under the Convention Against Torture (IJ at 3).
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the credibility determination, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent has not established that his former counsel failed to perform with sufficient competence and that he was prejudiced by her performance. See Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004). The respondent argues that he was prejudiced by his former attorney’s performance because she did not explain to him that asylum (or asylum related relief) was a viable option and that his attorney should have known that he was ineligible for adjustment of status and advised him to pursue withholding of removal and protection under the Convention Against Torture instead.
 
The record does not support the respondent’s argument. As highlighted by the respondent on appeal and noted by the Immigration Judge below, the option of asylum (or asylum related relief such as withholding of removal) was discussed during the initial hearing and the respondent’s attorney expressed that although the respondent was generally afraid of returning to Afghanistan and his attorney suggested applying for asylum, the respondent was reluctant to apply (IJ at 3; Respondent’s Brief at 14). Additionally, the letter from the respondent’s former attorney states that she was paid to represent him in an “““adjustment of status case and/or asylum case,” the respondent was informed that he could apply for asylum, and that the option of asylum was discussed. Thus, the evidence of record indicates that the respondent chose not to pursue asylum related relief.
 
*2 The respondent asserts on appeal that he chose to pursue adjustment of status in lieu of asylum related relief because his former attorney advised him that an adjustment of status application would likely be successful. Though pursuing adjustment of status was an unsuccessful tactical decision due to the respondent’s criminal record, the respondent’s eligibility for adjustment of status is not at issue at this time. Rather, the issue is whether the respondent was advised that asylum related relief was an option and if he was given the opportunity to apply for such relief. It appears from the record before us that the respondent was both advised of the option of asylum related relief and that he was given the opportunity to apply for such relief. Indeed, the respondent and the Immigration Judge note that asylum related relief was “““suggested” by the respondent’s former counsel, but that the respondent declined to apply (IJ at 3; Respondent’s Brief at 14). Consequently, we find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent did not establish that his prior counsel’s performance was prejudicial.
 
Finally, although not explicitly requested, the respondent has not shown that an “exceptional situation” exists that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (stating that “as a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”). Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 3045833 (BIA)
End of Document

2018 WL 3045836 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAVIER RAMIREZ-VEGA
File: AXXX XX3 390 - El Paso, TX
April 24, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ray A. Ybarra Maldonado, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lorely R. Fernandez
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from a July 7, 2017, Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen proceedings.1 We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, under the “““clearly erroneous” standard and we review all other issues de novo, including questions of law, discretion, and judgment. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii); Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002).
 
The Immigration Judge found that the respondent was ordered removed on January 29, 1999, departed the United States, and reentered the country in 2000. In May 2017, the respondent moved to reopen his proceedings, more than 18 years after he was ordered removed. The Immigration Judge also found that the respondent’s motion was untimely and that he did not meet the requirements for equitable tolling of the 90-day filing deadline. Given these determinations, the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent is precluded from relief due to a “departure bar.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (2017); Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337, 344 (5th Cir 2016); (July 7, 2017 IJ Dec. at 1-4).
 
A respondent may file one motion to reopen proceedings within 90 days of a final order. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). Here, the respondent did not file an appeal and the Immigration Judge’s January 29, 1999, order became the final decision on the matter. Given that date, the respondent had until approximately April 29, 1999, to file a timely motion to reopen, but he did not file it until May 2017, more than 18 years after the filing deadline. Id. An alien has a statutory right to file a motion to reopen, regardless of whether he has left the United States, provided that the motion meets the time and numerical limitations specified by the Act. Section 240(c)(7) of the Act; Garcia-Carias v. Holder, 697 F.3d 257, 263-64 (5th Cir. 2012).
 
On appeal, the respondent asserts that his proceedings should be reopened because his 1998 conviction is no longer considered an aggravated felony and he is not removable as charged. The respondent notes that he was placed in removal proceedings, ordered removed in January 1999, and left the country. However, the respondent stresses that a lawful permanent resident card was issued to him (errantly) in March 1999, after he was ordered removed and departed the country. He was admitted to the United States in 2000 using that mistakenly-issued card, and believed that he was lawfully present in the country. He attempted to renew that lawful permanent resident card in 2008, learned that it had been issued in error, and ultimately was placed into proceedings in the Phoenix Immigration Court in 2010. Those proceedings are still open. The respondent moved the El Paso, Texas, Immigration Court to reopen his original removal proceedings in May 2017.2
 
*2 It is undisputed that the respondent’s motion was not timely filed; the respondent did not move to reopen proceedings until more than 18 years after he was ordered removed. However, in the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this case rests, the 90-day filing deadline may be equitably tolled where a respondent demonstrates that 1) “he has pursued his rights diligently,” and 2) “some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way and prevented timely filing.” Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337, 344 (5th Cir. 2016) (internal citation omitted). The legal authority the respondent relies upon to assert that his conviction no longer rendered him removable was issued in 2001, but the respondent did not move to reopen proceedings on this basis for another 16 years. That is not due diligence.
 
Even presuming, without deciding that the errant March 1999 issuance of a lawful permanent resident card constituted an exceptional circumstance, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not pursue his rights with reasonable diligence after learning in 2010 (at the latest) when he was placed back into proceedings that he was not lawfully present in the United States. The respondent offers no explanation for the 7-year delay in filing the motion to reopen after learning that his lawful permanent resident card was not properly issued. Thus, the respondent has not demonstrated that he warrants an equitable tolling of the 90-day filing deadline. Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d at 344.
 
Because the respondent did not file within the time limit or meet the requirements for equitable tolling, his motion does not comply with the requirements for a statutory motion to reopen. Section 240(c)(7) of the Act;; Garcia-Carias v. Holder, 697 F.3d at 263-64. If a respondent does not meet the statutory limitations, an Immigration Judge may exercise its regulatory sua sponte authority to reopen a case. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). However, unlike a statutory motion, a regulatory motion is subject to the “departure bar,” which prohibits motions to reopen “by or on behalf of a person who is the subject of removal, deportation, or exclusion proceedings subsequent to his or her departure from the United States.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b); Navarro-Miranda v. Ashcroft, 330 F.3d 672, 675-76 (5th Cir. 2003). Here, the Immigration Judge could not exercise sua sponte authority because the respondent left the United States in January 1999 after a final order of removal, and thus the regulatory “““departure bar” applies. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1); see also Ovalles v. Holder, 577 F.3d 288, 296-97 (5th Cir. 2009) (upholding Board’s determination that it lacked sua sponte authority to reopen case due to regulatory departure bar, where alien’s motion was untimely and he thus had no statutory right to have his motion heard). For these reasons, we will affirm the denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
*3 Finally, the respondent mentions in passing that, if the Board concludes that counsel may have been ineffective by not moving to reopen sooner, we should remand the record to the Immigration Judge to allow counsel to withdraw so that the respondent can retain “new counsel and file a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel” (Respondent’s Br. at 9). We do not sua sponte review the record for ineffective assistance of counsel. Instead, an ineffective assistance of counsel claim must be supported by (1) an affidavit from the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with former counsel regarding the matters the attorney was retained to address, (2) documentary evidence that the counsel in question was informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond, and (3) proof that a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to the alleged ineffective assistance (and if this is not filed, why it was not). Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988).
 
Here, the respondent did not comply with any of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639. Indeed, counsel did not expressly assert that he was ineffective or provide any other relevant information regarding the filing of the motion to reopen. The United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit requires strict compliance with Lozada in order to put forth an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 647 (5th Cir. 2014). Thus, we will not address this issue further.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be issued.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HECTOR ANTONIO CHAJON-MEJIA
File: AXXX XX3 083 - New York, NY
April 24, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven A. Mundie, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s September 18, 2017, decision denying his motion to reconsider the August 23, 2017, denial of his motion to reopen and rescind a December 1, 2014, in absentia removal order. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board must defer to the Immigration Judge’s factual findings, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, unless they are clearly erroneous. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent has not identified a factual or legal error in the Immigration Judge’s August 23, 2017, decision denying his motion to reopen. See section 240(c)(6)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6)(C) (a motion to reconsider shall specify the errors or law or fact in the previous order and shall be supported by pertinent authority); Matter of 0-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). The respondent’s appellate brief is almost identical in content to his July 31, 2017, motion to reopen. The respondent argues therein that it was improper to serve the Notice to Appear on him because he was a 16-year-old minor at the time. The respondent urges this court to “overrule its current precedent” and follow Flores-Chavez v. Ashcroft, 362 F.2d 1150 (9th Cir. 2004), which provides that if an alien under the age of 18 is released into the custody of an adult, the Notice to Appear should be served on the adult as well as the alien.
 
We decline to do so. We have held that outside the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, personal service of a Notice to Appear on a minor who is 14 years of age or older at the time of service is effective, and that the regulations do not require that notice also be served on an adult with responsibility for the minor. Matter of Cubor-Cruz, 25 I&N Dec. 470 (BIA 2011) (declining to follow Flores-Chavez v. Ashcroft). Therefore, in this case arising in the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit, the respondent was properly served the Notice to Appear. See Matter of Cubor-Cruz, 25 I&N Dec. at 473, n.3 (noting that the Second Circuit sought publication on this issue in Llanos-Fernandez v. Mukasey, 535 F.3d 79, 86 (2d Cir. 2008), but that subsequent proceedings in the case rendered it moot). The respondent has also not rebutted the presumption of proper delivery of the hearing notice notifying him of the December 1, 2014, court date, because he was personally served the Notice to Appear and the notice of hearing was sent to his last known address care of his aunt in Jamaica, New York (Exhs. 1, 4). See Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665 (BIA 2008). The record does not reflect that the hearing notice was returned as undeliverable. Accordingly, the respondent has not demonstrated that his case should be reopened due to lack of notice. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
*2 We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not identify any factual or legal errors in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not establish that exceptional circumstances warrant granting his untimely motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order (9/18/17 IJ Dec. at 2). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The respondent contends that his aunt received the hearing notice, but threw it away. In her August 23, 2017, decision, the Immigration Judge properly found that the respondent had not presented adequate evidence that his aunt threw away the notice, noting that the respondent did not submit a sworn declaration attesting to his version of events, or a statement from his mother or aunt who had knowledge of the situation (9/18/17 IJ Dec. at 2; 8/23/17 IJ Dec. at 2).1 The Immigration Judge also noted that the respondent, who knew he was in removal proceedings in 2014, did not act with due diligence (8/23/17 IJ Dec. at 2). The respondent has not meaningfully challenged these findings on appeal. Nor does the respondent meaningfully challenge the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent has not articulated a valid ineffective assistance of counsel claim (9/18/17 IJ Dec. at 2).2 As previously stated, the respondent’s appellate brief is almost identical in content to his July 31, 2017, motion to reopen, and solely focuses on the Flores-Chavez v. Ashcroft issue. We therefore deem the exceptional circumstances and ineffective assistance of counsel issues waived and will not further address them. See Matter of G-G-S-, 26 I&N Dec. 339, 340 n.2 (BIA 2014), overruled on other grounds by Gomez-Sanchez v. Sessions, 2018 WL 1661652 (9th Cir, Apr. 6.2018)
 
In his motion to reconsider, the respondent sought reopening to apply for asylum and related relief and protection from removal based on changed country conditions in Guatemala (Motion to Reconsider at 5-8). 8 C.F.R. 1003.23(b)(4)(i). He also argued that sua sponte reopening was warranted because he has a United States citizen son (IJ at 8-10). The Immigration Judge rejected the respondent’s arguments, and the respondent has not meaningfully challenged these findings on appeal. We therefore deem these issues waived and will not further address them. See Matter of G-G-S-, 26 I&N Dec. at 340 n.2
 
Based on the foregoing, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider the August 23, 2017, decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind the December 1, 2014, in absentia order. The respondent has not identified any error of fact or law in the Immigration Judge’s August 23, 2017, decision.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

1

Instead, the respondent submitted the declaration of his girlfriend, whose knowledge of the information was secondhand.

2

We note that current counsel represented the respondent when he filed his motion to reopen, but a different attorney represented the respondent when he filed his motion to reconsider.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RANA SAMEH MAHAAFZA A.K.A. RANA SAMEH ABDEL-RAHMAN
File: AXXX XX7 866 - Los Angeles, CA
April 23, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lisa Pickering, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Jordan, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on December 4, 2012, after not appearing at her hearing. She filed a motion to reopen on February 20, 2013, alleging insufficient notice, which the Immigration Judge denied on June 5, 2013.1 On October 17, 2017, the respondent submitted a second motion to reopen, and appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 26, 2017, denying the motion.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that “exceptional circumstances,” to wit, ineffective assistance of counsel, prevented her appearance at the hearing, inasmuch as her former attorney did not notify her of the court date (Respondent’s Br. at 8-12). See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C) (providing that a motion to reopen based on “exceptional circumstances,” as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1), must be filed within 180 days of the removal order). The respondent filed her October 17, 2017, motion more than 180 days after the hearing. It is therefore untimely. Moreover, the motion is number-barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
In addition, we decline to equitably toll the time and number requirements. See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672, 677 (9th Cir. 2011) (requiring for equitable tolling that an alien acted with “due” or “reasonable” diligence in discovering any “deception, fraud, or error” that prevented an alien from timely filing a motion). Initially, the respondent has not satisfied the procedural requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 598 (9th Cir. 2004) (stating that an alien’s letter of complaint to a bar association about a former attorney did not substantially comply with the Lozada requirement that an alien notify former attorney of an ineffective assistance allegation and give the attorney an adequate opportunity to respond). The respondent has submitted evidence that she has filed a complaint against former counsel with a state bar association. However, she has not established that she notified former counsel that she had accused him of ineffective assistance of counsel in proceedings before the Immigration Court.
 
*2 Furthermore, the respondent states in her bar complaint that she obtained a copy of her file from former counsel in May 2016. Presumably, the respondent was then aware that the Immigration Judge had denied her first motion to reopen and no appeal was filed from that decision. However, the respondent waited until October 17, 2017, to file her second motion to reopen. Hence, the respondent did not act with due diligence. In light of the foregoing, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s October 17, 2017, motion to reopen based on untimeliness.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Thomas G Snow
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Immigration Judge correctly concluded that an alien is properly notified of a hearing if the Immigration Court sends a Notice of Hearing to the alien’s counsel. See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (requiring notice to be provided to counsel of record).

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAMRA ORIC
File: AXXX XX1 016 - Chicago, IL
April 20, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Katherine M. Dittmeier Holm, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on February 20, 2018, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of her request for a continuance. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen proceedings on March 9, 2018. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion, which will be granted.
 
The respondent’s motion is supported by evidence that the Jefferson Circuit Court, Commonwealth of Kentucky, has withdrawn the respondent’s guilty plea underlying her conviction pursuant to K.R.S. § 514.030, based on due process violations regarding ineffective assistance of counsel. Given the evidence presented, we find that the respondent’s vacated conviction may not be considered a conviction for immigration purposes. Matter of Adamiak, 23 I&N Dec. 878 (BIA 1006); Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003). Inasmuch as this conviction is the sole conviction underlying her removability, we find that the respondent is no longer removable as charged. Accordingly, we will grant the respondent’s unopposed motion, and terminate the respondent’s proceedings.
 
ORDER: The motion is granted, and the proceedings are terminated without prejudice.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

2018 WL 3007177 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SALAMATOU ISSAKA MAIKIDO
File: AXXX XX5 890 - Charlotte, NC
April 19, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Peter E. Torres, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent moves the Board to reconsider our October 25, 2017, decision dismissing her appeal from the Immigration Judge’s June 20, 2017, decision denying her untimely, number-barred motion to reopen.1 See section 240(c)(6) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). The motion will be denied.
 
In her prior motion to reopen, filed before the Immigration Judge on June 2, 2017, the respondent alleged ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of an attorney who filed an application for prosecutorial discretion on her behalf in 2014, asserting that the attorney failed to submit an application for asylum and withholding of removal (Form I-589) to the Immigration Judge. In his decision, the Immigration Judge noted that the respondent had previously filed a motion to reopen that was denied on May 9, 2014, and that the respondent waited 3 years thereafter to file her second motion to reopen. In our prior order, we found that the respondent did not establish that she suffered prejudice as the result of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
In the instant motion, the respondent asserts that she substantially complied with the requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel claims set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). However, although the respondent may have offered a statement in support of the motion, the statement did not set forth the terms of the attorney’s representation. Thus, the respondent not only failed to comply with the Lozada requirements, she has not shown that that the failure to submit a Form I-589 was the result of any error on the part of counsel. Id. The respondent, therefore, failed to show that her former attorney’s handling of her case resulted in prejudice. Further, the respondent, who was represented by a third attorney upon the filing of her first motion to reopen in 2014, did not show that she exercised due diligence in discovering the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. Indeed, she has not explained the delay in bringing the matter before the Immigration Judge 3 years after the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel occurred. Under the circumstances, neither equitable tolling of the filing deadline nor reopening sua sponte was warranted. See Akwada v. Ashcroft, 113 F. App’x 532, 538-39 (4th Cir. 2004). See also Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).
 
*2 In sum, we find no error of fact or law in our prior order to warrant reconsideration. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1). Accordingly, the motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s petition for judicial review of the Board’s prior order is currently pending. Maikido v. Sessions, No. 17-2329 (4th Cir., filed Nov. 17, 2017).

2018 WL 3007178 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SILVIA ELIZABETH AREVALO-CASTILLO
File: AXXX XX1 124 - Memphis, TN
April 19, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marisa-Andrea L. Moore, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, has appealed from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated August 17, 2017, denying her motion to reopen the proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered against her on July 10, 2013. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994). The respondent received proper notice of her removal hearing. On April 8, 2012, the respondent was personally served with the Notice to Appear (“NTA”) and, thus, was aware she had been placed in removal proceedings (IJ at 1, 3; Exh. 1). See Matter of Cubor-Cruz, 25 I&N Dec. 470 (BIA 2011) (personal service of an NTA on a minor who is 14 years of age or older at the time of service is effective). The NTA informed the respondent of her obligation to keep her address updated with the court as well as the consequences of failing to appear at a hearing (IJ at 3-4; Exh. 1). Section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1)(B); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.15(b)(7), 1003.15(d).
 
On January 11, 2013, the notice of hearing was properly mailed to the respondent’s last known address, which was that of her father (Exh. 3). Sections 239(a)(1)(F), 239(c), and 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229(a)(1)(F), 1229(c), and 1229a(b)(5)(A); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.13, 1003.26(d). The respondent had moved from that address in May 2012 and did not comply with the statutory address obligations by providing a current address pursuant to section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Act (IJ at 3-4; Exh. 4; Motion at Tab A, no. 13). For aliens not in detention, written notices of hearing are not required if the alien has failed to provide the address required under section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Act. Sections 239(a)(2)(B) and 240(b)(5)(B) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(d). Because the notice of hearing was mailed to the respondent’s last known address and she failed to provide the court with her current address, she received proper notice of her hearing.
 
*2 With respect to the respondent’s claim of exceptional circumstances, her motion is untimely because she did not file her motion within 180 days of the Immigration Judge’s decision.1 Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The Immigration Judge properly declined to equitably toll the applicable filing requirements. The doctrine of equitable tolling excuses an alien’s untimely motion if she can show that “despite diligent efforts, she did not discover the injury until after the limitations period had expired.” Barry v. Mukasey, 524 F.3d 721, 724 (6th Cir 2008) (quotation omitted). “Equitable tolling may apply when a petitioner has received ineffective assistance of counsel.” Mezo v Holder, 615 F.3d 616, 620 (6th Cir. 2010). The respondent must show that she diligently pursued her rights and was prejudiced by counsel’s ineffective assistance. Mezo v Holder, 615 F.3d at 620. Prejudice means that “but for counsel’s error, [the respondent] would have been entitled to continue residing in the United States.” Hulcochea-Gomez v INS, 237 F.3d 696, 699-700 (6th Cir. 2001).
 
Here, equitable tolling is not warranted. The respondent’s vague statements about consulting with an unnamed attorney to get her immigration paperwork are insufficient to establish a claim to ineffective assistance of counsel (IJ at 5; Motion at Tab A, nos. 19, 20). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.3d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The Immigration Judge properly rejected the respondent’s claim that her minority and the irresponsibility of her guardian (her father) constitute an exceptional circumstance (IJ at 5). The respondent was an adult at the time of the Immigration Judge’s July 10, 2013, decision, and her father’s filing of a Missing Juvenile Report with the police and hiring of an attorney to file a Form EOIR-33 indicating the respondent’s address was “unknown” after she ran away, do not support her claim (IJ at 5; Exh. 4). Moreover, even if the respondent tried unsuccessfully to get her immigration paperwork from her father, she does not claim to have contacted the Immigration Court to ask about the status of her case or any scheduled hearings or to provide her new address (Motion at Tab A, nos. 16-18, Tab B at nos. 8-9, Tab C at no. 10, Tab J, Tab L at 8-12). The respondent has not shown that she diligently pursued her rights.
 
Nor has the respondent shown that the application of the extraordinary remedy of sua sponte reopening is warranted. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). A removal hearing is reopened sua sponte only in “exceptional circumstances” and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. at 984. The respondent has not presented exceptional circumstances which would merit sua sponte reopening of the proceedings. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.2
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent filed her motion on July 14, 2017, more than 3 years after the 180-day deadline for a motion based on exceptional circumstances had passed.

2

The respondent submitted a Form I-589, Application for Asylum and for Withholding of Removal with her motion (Motion at Tab O). A motion to reopen may be filed at any time if the alien is seeking asylum or withholding of removal based on changed country conditions arising in the country of the alien’s nationality or the country to which the alien is to be removed. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i). Matter of J-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 161 (BIA 2013). The respondent did not claim changed country conditions in her motion or on appeal. Therefore, we will not further address this issue.

2018 WL 3007202 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FELIPE LOPEZ-CAMARILLO
File: AXXX XX7 718 - San Diego, CA
April 12, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Murray D. Hilts, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael K. Adams
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Termination
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s June 7, 2017, decision denying his motion to suppress evidence and to terminate proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s factual determinations, including credibility determinations, for clear error. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board uses a de novo standard of review for questions of law, discretion, judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge made the following findings of fact without clear error. The respondent first appeared at a May 2015 master calendar hearing and stated that Spanish was his best language (IJ at 2). Thereafter, he appeared with counsel on June 22, 2016, admitted all factual allegations, conceded the charge of removal arising under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, and was granted until February 2017 to seek prosecutorial discretion or apply for relief (IJ at 2). At the next hearing, the respondent appeared with two attorneys, sought to substitute counsel, and stated that he only spoke a little Spanish, and that the language he spoke and understood best was Mixteco (IJ at 2-3). At the next master calendar hearing on March 10, 2017, the respondent appeared with new counsel and filed a motion to withdraw his plea, to suppress evidence, and to terminate proceedings (IJ at 3). In support of that motion, the respondent submitted a declaration made under penalty of perjury wherein he states that he ditcated the declaration in Spanish, his native language, that he was pulled over by immigration officers in February 2015 while driving lawfully, that he did not feel free to leave once pulled over, and that he was not provided with evidence against him or told how to “““fight his case” when he first appeared before the Immigration Judge (IJ at 3, Exh. 7B).
 
The Notice to Appear was personally served on the respondent on February 19, 2015, upon his arrest after a traffic stop (Exhs. 1, 7A). The respondent’s motion to terminate is based on his argument that evidence of his alienage, as set forth in, inter alia, the Form I-213, Record of Deportable/Inadmissible Alien (Exh. 7A), was obtained by the Department of Homeland Security (“the DHS”) via an egregious violation of his Fourth Amendment rights. In his declaration in support of his motion, the respondent stated that he was “not breaking any laws or causing any disturbances” when he was stopped by the officers (Exh. 7B). The I-213 indicated that the respondent was driving a “““target vehicle” identified by make, model, color, and license plate (Exh. 7B). The vehicle was stopped, the respondent was identified by a Mexican identification card, and he admitted his Mexican nationality (Id).
 
*2 In removal proceedings, an alien seeking the exclusion of evidence based on the Fourth Amendment bears the burden of establishing a prima facie case that evidence should be suppressed. Matter of Tang, 13 I&N Dec. 691 (BIA 1971). While the Fourth Amendment exclusionary rule is generally inapplicable to deportation proceedings, this rule may be applied if there are egregious Fourth Amendment violations that transgress Fifth Amendment notions of fundamental fairness, undermining the probative value of the evidence. See also Matter of Sandoval, 17 I&N Dec. 70 (BIA 1979); Matter of Garcia, 17 I&N Dec. 319, 321 (BIA 1980) (respondent made prima facie showing that admissions were given involuntarily and the government presented no contrary evidence; proceedings terminated); Matter of Tow, 17 I&N Dec. 340, 343 (BIA 1980). The request to exclude evidence should be made via a motion to suppress that is supported by an affidavit or other objective evidence that explains why suppression is appropriate. Matter of Barcenas, 19 I&N Dec. 609 (BIA 1988); Matter of Wong, 13 I&N Dec. 820, 822 (BIA 1971). When an alien has come forward with adequate evidence in support of suppression, the burden shifts to the DHS to justify the manner in which it obtained the evidence. Matter of Barcenas, 19 I&N Dec. at 611. However, the identity of an alien (as distinguished from alienage) is not suppressible, even if it is conceded that an illegal arrest, search, or interrogation occurred. See INS v. Lopez-Mendoza, 468 U.S. 1032, 1051 (1984); Matter of Cervantes-Torres, 21 I&N Dec. 351, 353 (BIA 1996) (alien’s previously-submitted application for work authorization could be submitted as evidence of alienage even if egregious arrest occurred).
 
In this case, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s finding that the record does not contain evidence to support the respondent’s contention that the evidence of his alienage was obtained via an egregious violation of his Fourth Amendment rights (IJ at 5). The record lacks evidence that the respondent was targeted for an inappropriate reason such as race or that he did not voluntarily admit to his alienage (Id). Therefore, the burden did not shift to the DHS to justify the way that it obtained the evidence of the respondent’s alienage. Because no prima facie evidence of an egregious violation of the Fourth Amendment has been proffered, we reject the respondent’s contention that he should have been permitted to confront the author of the I-213 (Respondent’s Br. at 12)1
 
*3 To the extent that the respondent argues on appeal that his prior counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel and his plea should be withdrawn based on this, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that this allegation has not been supported by adequate evidence or by adherence to the procedural prerequisites to making such a claim (IJ at 4-5). See Respondent’s Br. at 5-6. As noted, the respondent’s claim not to have understood prior proceedings in which his alienage and removability were admitted is undermined by his preparation of an affidavit in the Spanish language (IJ at 5-6). Furthermore, although the respondent argues on appeal that he did not know of the evidence against him and would not have conceded the removal charge, he has not shown that his attorney’s decision to concede this charge was ineffective assistance, since there is no evidence in the record reflecting that he is not an alien or that he has any right to remain in the United States. Cf. Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931 (9th Cir. 1986) (finding that the decision to forego contesting deportability in favor of a generous grant of voluntary departure is a tactical choice). He also has not complied with the procedural steps-such as notifying former counsel or filing a claim with relevant bar authorities-which we have indicated are a prerequisite for making such a claim. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We therefore find no cause to disturb the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to terminate or to withdraw his concession of the charges based on due process violations stemming from ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Finally, for the reasons stated by the Immigration Judge, including but not limited to the fact that the respondent unlawfully re-entered the United States after his nine prior voluntary returns, we affirm the denial of the respondent’s application for voluntary departure.
 
For the foregoing reasons, the appeal will be dismissed on the entry of the following order.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Anne J. Greer
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

An updated I-213 reflects that the respondent’s vehicle was targeted because he previously had been convicted of driving under the influence and had been identified as a fugitive based on his unlawful entries following his nine prior voluntary returns from the United States (IJ at 6; Exh. 10).

IN RE: WILLIAM EDUARDO RECINOS-RODRIQUEZ

April 12, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joshua A. Berman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Billy J. Sapp
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s October 23, 2017, decision finding him inadmissible for having been convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude, as set forth at section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), and denying his request for a continuance to pursue related post-conviction relief. During the pendency of the appeal, he also filed an unopposed motion to remand the record to the Immigration Judge.1 The motion will be granted, and the record will be remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and the entry of a new decision.2
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgment, and all other issues de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent entered the United States without inspection (IJ at 1; Tr. at 3-4; Exh. 1). Then, on September 27, 2016, he was convicted of reckless endangerment in violation of Md. Crim. Code § 3-204(a)(1) (2003), committed in March 2016, and for which he was sentenced to 18 months of confinement (IJ at 1-2; Tr. at 3-4; Exhs. 1, 2). The Immigration Judge concluded that the reckless endangerment conviction was a conviction for a categorical crime involving moral turpitude, and thus, that the respondent was inadmissible as charged pursuant to section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act (IJ at 1-2). Matter of Silva-Trevino III, 26 I&N Dec. 826, 830 (BIA 2016).
 
During the pendency of this appeal, the respondent filed a motion to remand accompanied by an order and memorandum opinion dated February 22, 2018, from the Anne Arundel County Circuit Court vacating the underlying conviction and entering a judgment of acquittal based on the criminal court’s finding of ineffective assistance of counsel (Respondent’s Unopposed Motion to Remand at Tab B).
 
The respondent’s motion to remand is based on new previously unavailable evidence material to the Immigration Judge’s decision to sustain the charge brought under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act (IJ at 1-2). See Matter of Coehlo, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992) (explaining that the same substantive requirements apply to motions to remand that apply to motions to reopen); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (outlining the substantive requirements for motions to reopen); see also Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003) (holding that where a conviction is vacated based on a defect in the underlying criminal proceedings, and not solely for immigration hardships or rehabilitation purposes, the respondent no longer has a “conviction” under section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(48)(A)). The respondent conceded below that the conviction also precluded him from pursuing certain forms of relief from removal, such as cancellation of removal for certain non-lawful permanent residents pursuant to section 240A(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b) (Tr. at 9-10, 16, 18; Respondent’s Motion to Remand at 2).
 
*2 Thus, we conclude that remand of the record is appropriate to allow the Immigration Judge to address the impact of the vacatur in the first instance. See Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002) (explaining that the Board has limited fact-finding authority on appeal); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is granted and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Anne J. Greer
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7).

2

The Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s request for post-conclusion voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b), conditioned on the posting of a bond (IJ at 2-3). The respondent has provided proof on appeal that he posted the required bond. However, in light of our remand of the record, we need not reinstate the grant of voluntary departure at this juncture.

APPLICATION: Reopening
  
REISSUED DECISION
 
*1 The respondent has filed a motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). He seeks reissuance of the Board’s March 8, 2017, decision dismissing his appeal. The respondent’s counsel avers that they provided ineffective assistance of counsel by contracting to prepare and file a petition for review, but neglected to do so.1 The Department of Homeland Security was granted an extension of time to file a response, but has not filed an objection. In light of counsel’s admissions, as well as the other evidence presented, the motion to reopen and reissue is granted. See Dearinger ex re. Volkova v. Reno, 232 F.3d 1042 (9th Cir. 2000). The grant of voluntary departure will again be reinstated. Accordingly, the following orders will be issued.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen and reissue is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s decision dated March 8, 2017, attached hereto, is hereby reissued and shall be treated as entered as of today’s date.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
*2 WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
Edward R. Grant

Nevertheless, it appears that counsel for the respondent filed a petition for review. Baltazar-Cazares v. Sessions, No. 18-70068 (9th Cir.). The parties should notify the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit of this decision.

APPLICATION: Reopening; termination
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen and terminate these removal proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion.
 
With the instant motion, the respondent has proffered evidence that the 2008 Maryland state conviction that formed the sole basis for his removability has been vacated due to ineffective assistance of counsel (Motion to Reopen, Exh. B; see also Board Dec. dated Jan. 11, 2018; IJ Dec. dated July 31, 2017; Admin Record, Exh. 1). Based on the proffered evidence, we hereby grant the respondent’s motion to reopen. See Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621, 624 (BIA 2003) (holding that a conviction that has been vacated by the criminal court based upon a procedural or substantive defect in the underlying proceedings is no longer a conviction for immigration purposes). These proceedings are hereby reopened, and terminated without prejudice.

2018 WL 3007233 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LAKHWINDER SINGH
File: AXXX XX8 712 - Boston, MA
April 2, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Georges S. Zeidan, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on October 19, 2017, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his application for adjustment of status. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider and request for a stay of removal with the Board. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion. The motion and stay will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider shall specify “errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); see also Matter of 0-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). The respondent’s motion does not establish any error in our previous decision affirming the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not testify and dismissing his appeal.
 
To the extent that the respondent is attempting to raise new arguments related to his application for adjustment status, these arguments are not properly raised for the first time in a motion to reconsider. See 0-S-G-, 20 I&N Dec. at 58 (stating that “[a] motion to reconsider based on a legal argument that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied” and “arguments for consideration on appeal should all be submitted at one time, rather than in piecemeal fashion.”) (citation omitted). A motion to reconsider is neither a vehicle for advancing supplemental legal arguments that could have been previously raised nor a mechanism for submitting a late-filed brief. Moreover, we are not persuaded that the respondent’s supplemental arguments regarding the nature of his marriage and his new allegation that his marriage has been over since 2012 establishes any error in the Board’s or Immigration Judge’s decision. Disagreement with the result is not sufficient. Therefore, the motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is more in the nature of a timely motion to reopen. Applicable regulations provide that a motion to reopen “shall state the new facts that will be proven at a hearing to be held if the motion is granted and shall be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). A motion to reopen must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief “and all supporting documentation.” Id. In addition, where an alien seeks to apply for relief for which he or she has previously been provided an opportunity to apply at the former hearing, a showing of changed circumstances since the time of the hearing must be made. Id. Moreover, the movant must establish prima facie eligibility for the relief sought. INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314, 319 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104-05 (1988).
 
*2 The respondent alleges for the first time that his marriage has been deteriorating since 2012 and attributes the adverse credibility finding to the state of his marriage (Motion at 4-5; Exh. A). He further argues that his counsel made a “good faith error” in not arguing that the marriage was bona fide at inception (Motion at 5).
 
To the extent the respondent is arguing that he is the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not complied with any of the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).1 Regardless, the respondent has failed to explain why neither he nor his wife mentioned the deterioration of their marriage in their extensive testimony.
 
Finally, the evidence submitted along with the respondent’s motion, his own affidavit attesting to the deterioration of his marriage, is neither new nor previously unavailable and the respondent has not explained why he did not mention this at his hearing below. Thus, the respondent has not submitted persuasive new or previously unavailable evidence that is sufficient to meet his “heavy burden” of showing that it is likely that the result would change if the proceedings were reopened. See generally Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). Therefore, we do not find that he has met his burden of showing that reopening of these proceedings is warranted.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Under Matter of Lozada, an ineffective assistance of counsel claim must satisfy the following procedural requirements: (1) an affidavit of the alien attesting to the relevant facts, including a detailed description of the agreement with former counsel, (2) former counsel must be informed of the allegations and provided with an opportunity to respond, and (3) indicate whether a complaint has been filed the appropriate disciplinary authority, and if not, why not.
